BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
SUMIARY AND CONCLUSIONS
This report appraises a project to modernize and expand agro-
industries in the eight central and southern states of Brazil in order to
nelp meet rapidly growing domestic and export market demands for processed
food and feed products. Brazil seeks to expand and diversify exports to
alleviate balance of payment problems and to stimulate general economic growth.
The proposed project would be an extension of thie First Agro-industries Credit
Project (Loan 924-BR of 1973 for US$54 million) and would pursue similar
objectives. The project would support the Government's ongoing export corri-
dors program, which aims at increasing exports, especially exports of agricul-
tural products, through the development of five transportation corridors. Tile
Grain Storage Project (Loan 857-BR of 1972 for US$30 million) and two loans
for livestock development (Loan 516-BR of 1967 for US$40 million and Loan
868-BR of 1972 for US$26 million) and an agricultural research project (for
US$40 million) are complementary to this program. The Bank is also financing
more socially oriented projects, including the recently approved nutrition re-
search and development project (for US$19 million), located mainly in the
northeastern states.
ii.       The Brazilian economy has continued to expand rapidly in recent
years, averaging 10.6% per year over the period 1971-74, although agriculture
at 5.4% grew more slowly than industry at 12.4% per year. Agricultural ex-
ports during the same period grew in constant (1973) values from US$3,214
million in 1971/72 to US$3,796 million in 1973 and US$3,456 million in 1974,
the latter year showing a decrease in the export of traditional products such
as coffee, sugar and cotton, as well as a severe contraction of the beef export
market. Other exports, particularly non-traditional products such as soybean,
grain and livestock products, climbed from 11% of total agricultural exports
in 1962 to 43% in 1973. The food and feed processing sectors nave not, however,
kept pace with the growth of agricultural output.  The proposed project would
therefore nelp increase the country's processing capacity for some of these
non-traditional export products and help reduce import requirements for certain
other products.
iii.      Over a three-year period, the project would assist in financing in-
vestments in meat processing (estimated at US$120 million), grain processing
and nandling (US$50 million), and milk processing (US$25 million). Assistance
would also be granted to firms processing miscellaneous commodities (US$65
willion). Total project cost is estimilated at US$260 million, of which the
foreign exchange component would ue about US$83 million (32%). Sub-borrowers
would finalnce about 20% of total costs, tnie Government, 41/O; participating
banks, about 7%, and the bank, 32A, or US$S3 million (equal to the foreign
exciiange component).



iv.       hioe Jank loan would Se nmade to tne Government, Wiiici would pass on
tLe proceeds, tOgetLaer witLi its own contribution, to tile Clentral 3ank for
caanneiing to t1he participating banks, wno, in turn, would on-lend the funds to
qualified sub-borrowers for periods of 10 years, including grace periods
of up to three years, at interest rates ranging between 3% and 5%, plus full
iiidexing, according to thle region. Subloans would be made to existing enter-
prises to finance their expansion and/or rehabilitation, or to enable them to
adopt new technologies or meet new sanitary standards.  Subloans would also
be made to initiate entirely new ventures.
v.        As with tne first agro-industries credit project, the Central Bank
would be responsible for overall execution of the project and would continue
to .iaintain a Project Team to administer the project and review and super-
vise tine work of the participating banks. The participating banks would
appraise subprojects according to criteria set forth in subsidiary loan
agreements to be signed with the Central Bank and would submit their appraisals
to the Central Bank for review. The Central Bank would establish subloan
approval limits for the participating banks as deemed appropriate. Prior
bank approval would be required for subloans with a value above US$5 million
as well as for each subloan submitt:ed from a new participating bank and each
subloan with a value above US$3 million falling under tlhe non-food and feed
processing miscellaneous category. In general, the participating banks are
considered to have competent management and staff who are capable of appraising
subprojects and supervising tne sub-borrowers in the implementation of their
plans.
vi..      Procurement would be undertaken according to the requirements of
tne particular enterprises involved, as decided by the owners of the enter-
prises and the participating banks. At least three quotations would be ob-
tained by sub-borrowers from contractors or manufacturers and these would be
submicted to the participating banks for review. Brazilian firms produce the
wide range of equipment needed and aave sufficient capacity to supply the
goods and services required. Their costs are generally comparable to inter-
national firms and they have demonstrated an ability to compete successfully
for international contracts. Most major foreign firms are represented in
".'razil through affiliates or sales agents.
vii.      Financial rates of return, ranging from 13% to 35%, would provide
incentives adequate to induce investors to participate in the project.
Indicative economic rates of return for individual subprojects would range
from 37% to 55% for beef processing,; 45% for oilseed processing; 54% for milk
processing, and 24% to 47% for miscellaneous processing.  The project would
also generate approximately 7,000 permanent jobs, plus about 4,000 jobs during
tne construction phase.
viii.     with the assurances listed in Cnapter VIII, the project is suitable
for a dank loan of US$83 million for a term of 15 years, including a grace
period of four years. The Government of Brazil would be the borrower and
would assume thie foreign exchange risk.



BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-I'NDUST'RI`S CREDIT PROJECI
I. INTRODUCTI1N
1.01      Thle Government of i.razil nas requested a Bank loan of US$83 million
to nelp finance a second credit project to expand and modernize agro-indus-
tries in the eight central and southern states of the country.  The borrower
would be the Federative Republic of Brazil. The direct beneficiaries would
be about 120 agro-industrial enterprises, while the indirect beneficiaries
would be a jreat many iadividual farmers and a substantial section of the
consuaing public.  The proposed loan would De a continuation of the first
loan (924-BR of 1973 for US$54 million), but it would emphasize development
of the entire agro-industrial sector rather than focus mainly on the moderni-
zation and expansion of export-oriented industries. both projects would
support brazil's ongoing export corridors program, which seeks to increase
exports, especially of agricultural products, through the development of five
transportation corridors - four in the central and southern states and one
in the northeast. The Grain Storage Project (Loan 857-BR of 1972 for US$30
million) and two loans for livestock development (Loan 516-BR of 1967 for
US$40 million and Loan 868-BR of 1972 for US$26 million) have also contributed
to the program. Concurrently with these and an agricultural research project
(for US$40 million), the Bank has also initiated more socially oriented
projects. The first of these is a settlement project in Maranao (Loan 853-BR
of 1972 for US$6.7 million); the second an emergency development project at the
mouth of the Sao Francisco River (Loan 1153-BR of 1975 for US$23 million); the
third a rural development project in Rio Grande do Norte (Report No. 921-BR
of 1975 for US$12 million); and more recently, a nutrition research and devel-
opment project (for US$19 million). Those projects are entirely or partly
located in the northeast. A number of other larger and more ambitious projects
of a similar nature are in preparation.
1.02      The proposed second agro-industries project was prepared in 1974
oy the Central Bank with the assistance of Bank staff engaged in the imple-
mentation and supervision of the first project.
II. BACKGROUND
A. Agriculture in the National Economy
2.01      The Brazilian economy has continued to grow at a considerable pace
despite world recessionary problems and the impact of the recent oil crisis.
During the period 1971-74, GDP has shown an annual growth rate of 10.6%;
industry, 12.4%; agriculture, 5.4%. In 1974 alone, GDP increased by 9.6%;
industry by 8.2%; agriculture by 8.5%, and services by 10.8% (Annex 1, Table 1).
The 4.2% growth rate of 1975 was the result of continuing balance of payment



2-
problems and severe frost in the States of Sao Paulo and Parana. In the same
year, industry grew at 4.2% and agriculture at 3.4%. Projected average growth
of GDP for the 1977-80 period is 8%, with 7.8% for industry and 7.5% for agri-
culture, again impressive figures by most standards. 1/
2.02      Thp increase in the average per capita income (in real terms) in
the 1971-74 period has also been remarkable, rising from US$640 in 1971 to
US$810 in 1974, desDite the population zrnwing at an annual rate of 2.8% to
reach a current total of about 105 million (Annex 1, Table 2). Per canita
income in 1975 was tlS$820, with the projection for 1980 reaching US$1,000 in
1973.
2.03       The impressive performance of the agricultural sector has been as
a result of an abundance of land and a thriving private sector, combined
with a generous allocation of public resources to bring new land into produc-
tion, the availability of credit, substantial investments in agro-industrial
and marketing infrastructure, increased productivity, the subsidizing of
agricultural inputs, and the intensification and improvement of support
services. As a result of these policies, agricultural production has in-
creased substantially, agro-industry has become more diversified, and exports
of agricultural products have risen dramatically. This is best exemplified
by the following figures:
Total Production            Exports

2.04       Growth of the food and feed processing sector has not kept pace
with the growth in output and exports of non-traditional products. The pro-
posed project would therefore help increase processing capacity for some
of the key items on the non-traditional export list, especially soybeans, for
1/   Source:   Bank estimate.



- 3 -
which both export and domestic demand is expected to grow rapidly. It would
also help to reduce the import of milk products.
B. Agriculture in the Project Area
2.05      The eight states of the center-south comprise one of the most pro-
ductive areas of Brazil, with great potential for further expansion. Recent
(1974) estimates by the Instituto de Economia Agricola (IEA) value total
agricultural production in the project area at around US$7.5 billion, with
the southeastern region contributing 48.4%; the southern region, 44%; and
the central-west region, 7.6% of gross agricultural income. The area already
produces about 80% of Brazil's beef, all of its soya, about 80% of its milk,
and 85% of its fruit juices. Beef production occurs throughout the region,
with the greatest concentration in Rio Grande do Sul, the southernmost state
of Brazil. Soya production is concentrated in the southern half of the
region, milk production is centered in its northern part, and fruit juice
production is mainly in Sao Paulo. Corn, rice and cotton are also widely
grown. Annex 1, Table 3 gives data on area planted, production, yields, and
values for the main crops grown in the project area. The recent frost,
affecting especially the coffee plantations in the States of Sao Paulo and
Parana, has emphasized the need for further diversification of production
and processing to avoid excessive dependency on a single crop.
C. Government Agricultural and Industrial Credit Policies
Agricultural Credit
2.06      Since 1965, the Government has made a continuous effort to increase
the amount of funds for agricultural credit. In general, agricultural loans
under Government schemes are made at lower interest rates than loans to other
sectors. In the project area, interest rates range from 9% to 15% without
monetary correction. Most areas north of the 16th parallel benefit from
even more favorable interest rates as a result of the Government's effort
to offset their remoteness and relative lack of development.  Several special
funds (such as FUNDEPE, FUNDAG and PROTERRA) have been created to finance
agricultural investments and the purchase of agricultural inputs.  In addi-
tion, the Government has instituted a number of economic incentives to
stimulate agricultural production, such as:  minimum prices for particular
crops, exemption of agricultural inputs from the value added tax, almost
total exemption of rural producers from income tax, and income tax credits to
corporations investing in agricultural and livestock enterprises. Because of
these policies and the size of the country, Brazil now has one of the largest
agricultural credit systems in the world. For example the loans to agricul-
ture outstanding by the end of 1974 totalled Cr$ 56 billion (US$7.6 billion),
involving an average loan size of Cr$ 33,000 (US$4,400). The total value of
loans amounted to 56% of the value of gross production, as compared with 37%
in 1971. The Banco do Brasil, which financed 56% of all agricultural credit
in 1974, is the leading agricultural lending agency.



-4-
Industrial Credit
2.07       The Government's overall ?olicy in the industrial sector is to
stimulate development, based on modern technology, that will compete inter-
nationally, will help to alleviate balance of payments constraints to
economic growth, and will increase employment opportunities. Within this
framework, the Government industria:L credit policies are designed to support
those industries that (a) expand the production and export of agricultural
products, (b) operate within sectors of specified high priority such as
the capital goods industry, (c) cont:ribute to regional balance of indus-
trial activity, and (d) substitute domestic production for manufactured
imports where economically justifiecl. Institutional credit to industry as
a whole amounted to Cr$ 23 billion by the end of 1974 (12% of production value).
Ofl-icial long- and medium-term lending operations are administered largely by
the Banco Nacional de Desenvolvimento Economico (BNDE) through the state and
regional development banks. In 1974, the average loan amounted to about
Cr$ 1 million, while the share of long- and medium-term loans provided by
BNDE was Cr$ 12.5 billion (52%). In the project area, typical interest rates
to sub-borrowers are between 3% and 9% with full monetary correction, through
a system under which principal and interest payments are adjusted to reflect
changes in the price level, thus assuring interest rates that are positive in
real terms. Private investment banks presently play a minor role, although
they serve as lending channels for industrial loans for BNDE and Caixa
Economica Federal (CEF). Despite the significant expansion in total credit
for the sector, long-term financing, particularly for medium and small firms,
is quite limited. In an effort to imnprove matters, the Government is making
substantial funds available to BNDE to increase the availability of industrial
cre,dit. Despite this, demand still exceeds supply, particularly for agro-
industries. The latter have reasonable access to short-term credit from the
commercial banking system but long-term credit is particularly limited. 1/
2.08      Providing funds to the Cent:ral Bank to stimulate agro-industries,
which was the purpose of the first project and is still the objective of
the second project, has increased the flow of funds and should increase the
number of credit outlets. 2/ A further constraint to expanding long-term
lencling activities in the agro-industrial sector is the scarcity of trained
personnel, since most banks experience difficulties in retaining and expanding
their trained technical staff. This problem too would be addressed in the
proposed project, at least insofar as the Central Bank is concerned.
D. The Banking System
Banco do Brasil
2.09       Banco do Brasil (BB), the country 's largest commercial bank, with
assets at the end of 1974 of Cr$ 131 billion (US$17.6 billion), is expanding
1/   No meaningful figures exist for agro-industries alone.
2/   Central Bank agreed with BNDE that from 1975 all agro-industrial investment
lending involving Bank funds wou;Ld be handled by the Central Bank.



- 5 -
its operations in agro-industry as a complement to its primary emphasis on
agriculture. Until 1964, BB performed the functions of a central bank and
today still acts as financial agent for the Federal Treasury and other Govern-
ment agencies. Its primary objective is to promote and advance national
production. BB's main sources of funds are deposits (short-term, sight,
and demand) from the public, from banks, from the financial institutions,
from the Treasury, and from the Central Bank. At the end of 1974, RB's loans
outstanding to the private sector for food processing (mainly sugar manufac-
ture) totalled Cr$ 2.8 billion (US$0.4 billion). Interest rates, monetary
correction, other charges, loan and grace periods, and security requirements
vary according to the size and the purpose of the loan and the source of
funds. In 1974, the weighted annual average yield (interest and other
charges) on all loans was 13.4%.. (For details see Annex 2.) At the end of
September 1974, BB had 878 branches distributed throughout Brazil, of which
about 600 were in the nine states comprising the project area.
State Development Banks
2.10      The state development banks seek to promote economic development by
providing long-term credit to enterprises in priority sectors. Although they
are small in comparison to BB, the assets of those participating in the pro-
ject at the end of 1974 ranged from Cr$ 340 million (US$43 million) to
Cr$ 2,109 million (USS266 million). They have been an important source of
long-term financing for industry (Annex 2), especially agro-industry. The
state development banks are comparatively new institutions, all of them
having been established within the last 15 years. Some have emerged as
successors to state development corporations. BDMG, BANDES, BADESP, and
BADEP 1/ are wholly or nearly wholly owned enterprises of the respective
Governments of the States of Minas Gerais, Espirito Santo, Sao Paulo, and
Parana, while BRDE 2/ is jointly owned by the Governments of Rio Grande do
Sul, Santa Catarina, and Parana. Each is governed by a specific law enacted
by the state legislature, but all are subject to the overall control of
the Central Bank. Under the terms of Monetary Council Resolution No. 93 of
1968, the state development banks are not permitted to operate branch offices,
which limits their activities. Legally, they are limited companies, subject
to the payment of Federal and State income tax. BRDE is an "autarquia," a
semi-Government institution covered by a special law. It is exempt from
income tax but is not allowed to accept deposits from the public.
2.11      Nearly all the share capital of the state development banks is
provided by the states. The ratio of capital and reserves to total loans
and investments (1 to 5 for loans in national currency and 1 to 15 for loans
in foreign currencies) is under constant supervision by the Central Bank.
1/   Banco do Desenvolvimento de Minas Gerais, Banco do Desenvolvimento de
Espirito Santo, Banco do Desenvolvimento do Sao Paulo, and Banco do
Desenvolvimento do Parana.
2/   Banco Regional do Extremo Sul.



- 6 
Loan arrears have been at a reasonable level, amounting to not more than 1%
of portfolio.  In recent years, and especial,  in 1974, profits of the banks
have been insufficient to compensate for the effects of inflation on capital
and reserves. This capital erosion haas beeu vaused mainly by loans having
been made without monetary correction (the spread and leverage failing to
compensate for inflation); a reduction in spreads in some credit lines from
BNDE and from the states, in effect requiring the participating banks to
contribute "to the maintenance of the momentum in the economic development";
and, in the case of certain banks, high administrative costs resulting from
relatively small loans. The Federal Government has agreed, within the con-
text of the recent development banking project (Report No. 932a-BR), to
undertake a study of the equity erosion problems of development banks and
take the necessary measures to recapitalize them.
Private Investment Banks
2.12      Most private investment banks are part of major commercial banks
and operate through their national branch networks. They obtain resources
mainly through savings certificates and debentures and also serve as channels
for public investment financing institutions and banks such as BNDE, the
National Housing Bank (BNH), and the Social Security Program (PIS). They also
serve as channels for external lenders. As regards capital and resources,
they are about as large as or larger than the major state development banks.
Several investment banks have their own technical staff of engineers, agrono-
mists, economists, and other specialists, while others keep consulting firms
on a retainer basis.
E. The Structure of Agro-industries
2.13      Agro-industries in the project area can be divided into four major
groups -- large international and national companies, medium-sized national
companies, farmer cooperatives, and small cottage-type industries. The
complexity of food processing operations tends to favor the large companies
because they can afford the modern management techniques that are required,
and they can also vertically integrate their operations, as in the case of
the pork industry. The large companies are sophisticated, comparing favorably
with similar industries in developed countries. Medium-sized companies are
normally limited in their expansion capability by lack of capital and techni-
cal expertise. They operate on a local basis, providing less sophisticated
products for local consumption as, for example, the poultry slaughterhouses.
The cooperatives are starting to process the products of their members
and may gain in importance because of their control over raw material supplies
such as fresh beef. As these groups are primarily agricultural, they lack
industrial experience and managerial expertise, which is difficult to acquire
in Brazil because of the growing demand derived from rapid growth in all
sectors of business. At present the cooperatives limit their activities
mainly to manufacturing semi-processed items or products that can be sold
through existing distribution outlets. In a few cases, a number of producer



-7-
cooperatives have joined forces and formed marketing cooperatives. Small
and cottage-type industries, particularly in the milk processing and miscel-
laneous processing categories, have trouble competing with the more advanced
companies or cooperatives, especially in operations such as cheese and butter
processing and fruit preserves and juices, which require large volumes and
mechanized handling to be significantly profitable. The success of these
smaller industries depends on the ability of the manager/owner and is usually
limited by a lack of capital necessary to expand their operations.
F. Government Policies for Agricultural Production and Food Processing
Agricultural Production
2.14      The Government attaches the utmost importance to the expansion of
agricultural production in order to sustain high levels of economic growth.
In addition, agricultural growth is seen as a key potential generator of
foreign exchange and, by meeting a growing domestic demand, a means of active
price stabilization. To support the growth objectives for agriculture, the
Government has allocated substantial public resources to agricultural programs.
Federal Government policies to increase agricultural production have been
backed up by credit programs geared to eliminate or reduce some of the factors
inhibiting agricultural development. Credit lines with subsidized inter-
est rates have been established for storage facilities, an extensive program
has been initiated for pasture improvement, and a Federal scheme to reimburse
farmers for a large portion of any losses in agricultural ventures has been
formulated. Geographic areas of special interest to the Federal Government
have received further attention through specific regional programs that in-
clude subsidized credit, such as POLONORDESTE, POLOAMAZONIA and POLOCENTRO.
All of these are geared to assist agriculture under conditions of drought,
tropical rain-forest and "cerrado" acid soils. However, the project area
is for the most part not included in the first two programs but is in the
third. Other smaller agricultural development programs deal with specific
sectorial problems, such as the Programa de Desenvolvimento da Agroindustria
do Nordeste, or are derived from geo-political situations such as that created
by fusion of the States of Rio de Janeiro and Guanabara, resulting in the
Programa Especial do Norte Fluminense.
2.15      Federal policies are complemented by individual state policies which
place special emphasis on specific geographic areas or agricultural subsectors
within a state. Thus, Rio Grande do Sul gives special attention to the concen-
tration of livestock activities in order to free agricultural areas for crop
production, particularly the expansion of soybeans. Within the project areas,
Santa Catarina is concerned with increasing agricultural productivity of its
traditional crops, particularly potatoes, peanuts and rice, in order to make
the most rational use of scarce arable land. Parana is giving attention to
soil conservation problems, particularly in its northwest area. Sao Paulo is
concerned with increased crop productivity in the already cultivated portions



of the state and in expanding agriculture towards the west in order to achieve
a more even geographic distribution. Rio de Janeiro is encouraging crop and
dairy production to substitute imports from other states, while Espirito Santo
and Minas Gerais are trying to attrazt outside investors to develop agriculture
and industry. The State of Mato Grosso, with vast unused agricultural resources,
is concerned mainly with improving infrastructure such as roads and rural
electrification. Most of the priority programs are supported not only by
technical assistance from the different state governments, but also by a
number of special subsidized credit lines.
Food Processing
2.16      Traditionally the Government's main effort has been to increase the
production and export of raw materials (paras 2.14 and 2.15), but more recently,
there has been increasing interest in the export of processed products such
as soybeans and beef. In response to this, the Government has initiated
special policy measures, including the strengthening of the Departmento do
Inspecao de Produtos de Origem Animal (DIPOA), an agency of the Ministry of
Agriculture, which is responsible for establishing and enforcing regulations
applicable throughout the country for the inspection and control of all pro-
cessed products of animal origin. This inspection and control is carried out
through three subdivisions of DIPOA, one responsible for all meat products
(DICAR), a second for all milk products (DILEI), and a third for all fish
products (DIPES). These subdivisions of DIPOA establish the regulations and
quality standards for their respective areas of responsibility, approve all
plans or modifications of processing plants before they are licensed, and
control each manufacturer to insure that the regulations and standards are
complied with. So far, DIPOA has focussed its attention on beef, which has
resulted in a thorough restructuring and upgrading of the beef processing
industry. Recently, it shifted emphasis to milk, and there is reason to
expect similar improvements in that industry.
G. Support Services
2.17      Agricultural research is largely, but by no means exclusively, a
Federal responsibility, and the recently created Empresa Brasileira de
Pesquisa AgroDecuaria (EMBRAPA) has been established to direct a wide range
of research activities. EMBRAPA has recently received Bank assistance under
the recently approved agricultural research loan. Agricultural extension
activities are more decentralized than agricultural research and are
conducted by individual state agencies (ANCARS). The activities of indi-
vidual ANCARS are, however, now being nationally coordinated by the newly
created Empresa Brasileira de Assistencia Technica e Extensao Rural (EMBRATER).
The creation of both national institutions, EMBRAPA and EMBRATER, are
expected to result in more effective and better coordinated services to
farmers. A third support institution is the Centro Brasileiro de Assistencia
Gerencial e Pequena e Media Empresa (CEBRAE) which is financed by BNDE and



-9-
the state development banks. CEBRAE operates through individual state
agencies to supply technical assistance and training of management to small-
and medium-sized industries. The program has achieved variable success in
different states. In 1975, the Federal Government provided Cr$ 50 million
(US$6.1 million) to assist CEBRAE, increasing to Cr$ 60 million (US$7.3 million)
in 1976 and Cr$ 65 million (US$16 million) in 1977. These funds are suffi-
cient for CEBRAE's current operations, but in view of the magnitude of the
managerial problem in small- and medium-sized industries, particularly in
relatively less developed states such as Santa Catarina, Espirito Santo,
Mato Grosso and Minas Gerais, CEBRAE may need ad7ditional support.
III.   PERFORMANCE UNDER THE FIRST AGRO-INDUSTRIES PROJECT
A. Introduction
3.01      The first agro-industries project (Loan 924-BR) was appraised in
November/December 1972, approved by the Executive Directors in June 1973, and be-
came effective in March 1974. The primary objective is to help modernize and ex-
pand agro-industries in the central and southern states of Brazil, the main thrust
being to stimulate production and increase the export of meat products through
an expansion in processing facilities. BUriH aLppr.isal, tatal project-cost
was estimated at US$135 million (US$108 million allocated to beef processing,
US$8 million to oilseed processing, US$10 million to milk processing, and
US$9 million to miscellaneous export-oriented food and feed processing facili-
ties) 1/. The Bank provided the Government with a loan of US$54 million,
which, with the Government's counterpart contribution of US$54 million, is
channeled to qualifying agro-industries through six participating state
development banks. Sub-borrowers collectively contribute an additional
US$27 million. Funds are on-lent for period of up to 10 years, including a
two-year grace period, at an interest rate of 5% plus full ORTN 2/ indexing.
3.02      As required by the Loan Agreement, the Central Bank, which is
primarily responsible for project execution, established a Project Team to
administer day-to-day operations and review the work of the participating
banks. The Project Team is required to appraise subprojects according to
criteria set forth in subsidiary loan agreements between the participating
banks and the Central Bank.
1/   In 1975 funds were reallocated.  Beef processing was decreased to
US$50 million, while the miscellaneous category was increased to
US$33 million and oilseed processing to US$17 million.
2/   National Treasury Bonds.



- 10 -
B. Lend ing Operat ions
3.03      Before the loan became effective, the Central Bank prepared a
project manual detailing the procedures to be applied in the evaluation,
appraisal, and supervision of subprojects. Subsequently, the participating
banks found that in many cases the procedures were too cumbersome, resulting
in delays in subproject approval. With the approval of the Bank, the pro-
cedures have been revised to prevent unnecessary bottlenecks in subproject
review.
3.04      The Loan Agreement originally required prior Bank approval of sub-
loans exceeding US$1.5 million equivalent (Bank participation of US$0.75 mil-
lion), or, which together with one or more earlier subloans exceeded US$2
million equivalent (Bank participation of US$1 million) in aggregate lending
to any single beneficiary. Also, prior Bank approval was required for the
first two subloans proposed in respect of meat, oil and milk processing, and
the first subloan in respect of any eligible sub-category under the miscellaneous
category. In addition, prior Bank approval was required for the first loan to
be made by any new participating bank. Subloans with a value of US$1 million
equivalent, or US$1.5 million equivalent in aggregate lending to a single
borrower, were subject to prior approval by the Central Bank, whereas subloans
with a value under US$1 million equivalent could be approved directly by the
participating banks. In July 1975, based upon satisfactory performance by
the Central Bank and the participating banks, the Bank deleted the requirement
that it approve each subloan in a new sector under the miscellaneous category.
In October 1975, the Bank agreed to further changes in the approval procedures
for sub-borrowers in the project.  The size limit above which prior Bank
approval of subloans was required was increased from US$1.5 million to US$4
million (Bank participation of US$2 million). Correspondingly, the Central
Bank established different approval limits for the various participating
banks, namely, US$2 million for Banco do Desenvolvimento do Estado do Sao
Paulo S.A. (BADESP), Banco de Desenvolvimento do Parana S.A. (BADEP), Banco
Regional do Extremo Sul (BRDE), and Banco do Desenvolvimento do Minas Gerais
(BDMG), and US$1 million for the remaining participating banks. In October
1975, the Bank also agreed to a reduction from 5%, plus full monthly correction,
to 3%, plus full monetary correction, in the interest rates on subloans in the
States of Goias, Mato Grosso, Espirito Santo and Santa Catarina in order to
stimulate further agro-industry activities in those less developed states.
In addition, the Bank agreed to include permanent working capital in the
investment costs of subprojects eligible for Bank financing and to the
elimination of the requirement that participating banks charge a 1% subproject
appraisal fee.
3.05      In general, these changes have contributed to other efforts being
made by the Central Bank and the participating banks to minimize the delays
in approval of individual subloans. Performance of the Project Team has
improved, as experience has increased the proficiency with which subloan
applications have been evaluated. In reviewing subprojects, Central Bank
has placed primary emphasis on insuring a ready supply of raw materials and



- 11 -
on the financial soundness of the sub-borrower. Aspects of the evaluation
that have sometimes been weak and require further improvement are market
analysis and management evaluation.
C. Commitment of Funds and Disbursements
3.06      Commitments under the first project have been slower than anticipa-
ted, partly because of initial delays in establishing the Project Team and in
formulating the lending procedures. As of June 18, 1976, subloans totalling
US$87 million were approved and subloans for a further US$4 million were under
review by the Central Bank and should be approved by end June 1976, together,
committing about US$45.5 million of the Bank loan. Additional subloans for a
total value of US$62 million and Bank lending of approximately US$31 million
are either under evaluation or in active preparation, most of which are likely
to qualify for Bank financing. Hence, subloans approved and those in the
pipeline would exceed resources available under the first project which is
likely to be fully committed by the end of July 1976. Details of subprojects
approved and those under preparation/consideration are listed in Annex 3,
while the map shows the actual locations of approved subprojects.
3.07      Corresponding to the rate of commitment, disbursements of the Bank
loan have been slower than anllcipated.  As of June 18, 1976, actual Bank
disbursement was US$15.9 million, representing about half of the appraisal
projection.
D. Impact of the Loan
3.08      So far, only a few of the approved subprojects have actually started
operations and not enough time has elapsed to permit meaningful evaluation.
However, it is apparent that the project is not likely to be as heavily oriented
to beef processing as was originally envisaged. Due to import restrictions,
especially in Europe, Brazilian beef exports dropped significantly during 1974.
As a result, It is unlikely that Brazil will become a major exporter of beef
in the next few years as was anticipated at appraisal. However, the project
has contributed to a significant increase in beef production, which, by
meeting rising domestic demand, has helped the Government to stabilize local
prices. It is difficult to predict whether export markets will expand in
the future, but in view of increasing world demand for animal products in
general and beef in particular, and, in view of Brazil's potential for live-
stock production, it is likely that exports will expand again as current
European surpluses disappear.
3.09      As a result of the loan, the Central Bank has already become a pri-
mary source of funds for agro-industries and the Project Team is now coordi-
nating major investments in the agro-industrial sector initiated by the



- 12 -
Ministries of Agriculture and Finance and the Secretariat of Planning. The
Bank has assisted the Project Team with technical advice, and the Bank's
participation in the review of subprojects has increased the quality of
the appraisal work, both by the state development banks and the Central Bank.
Furthermore, through Bank support, the Central Bank has been able to initiate
improvements in the participating banks' handling of bidding procedures, dis-
bursement and supervision of loans. The loan has also helped to insure that
sufficient resources are available for expanding the country's agro-industries
potential, including the successful modernization and expansion of the slaugh-
terhouse sector.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Brief Description
4.01      The project would be a continuation of the First Agro-industries
Project (Loan 924-BR), which has financed the expansion of agro-industries
in the nine central and southern states of Brazil and was primarily designed
to stimulate the growth of exports and reduce imports of processed agricul-
tural products. Over a period of three years, it would help finance facili-
ties for meat processing (46% of total investment), grain handling (19%),
milk processing (10%), and various agro-industries, including some engaged
in supplying basic agricultural inputs (25%). The relative size of the project
components has been determined largely by an analysis of subprojects financed
or under consideration for financing under the first project. It is evident
from such analysis that there is still substantial demand for increased and
improved meat processing facilities despite a reduction in national beef
exports. Thus, the project would (a) aim at satisfying the rapidly increasing
domestic market for all meat products (beef, pork and poultry), (b) assist a
considerable number of existing food and feed processing facilities to meet
more stringent veterinary and sanitary standards, and (c) support a growing
requirement for additional facilities brought about by the pressures of
increased agricultural production. Although meat processing remains the
single biggest component of the project, its relative importance is less
than in the first project. The 110 to 130 industries expected to be financed
under the project are anticipated to range in size from medium to large and
would be owned mostly by private enterpreneurs or cooperatives. The project
would also include financing for the training of Central Bank technicians.
As was the case in the first loan, the proposed Bank loan would be channeled
through the Central Bank to the participating banks, which would have pri-
mary responsibility for appraisal of all subloans. The Project Team in the
Central Bank would be responsible for overall coordination and implementation
of the project.



- 13 -
4.02      The number and size of subloans would vary considerably according
to the types of investment involved. The expected phasing of subloans is
indicated below:
Average

B. Detailed Features
Meat Processing
4.03      The project would provide for investment in some 57 meat processing
and packing plants. Facilities for handling cattle, pigs and poultry would
all be included. Essentially, investment would entail the construction of
new plants, including cold storage and, in some cases, distribution outlets
and refrigerated transport. The capacity of the plants and slaughterhouses
handling cattle would range from 200 to 600 head per day. Meat processors
have the necessary managerial experience and technical capability to success-
fully operate the facilities. Details are given in Annex 4.
Grain Processin&
4.04      The project would include financing for one or two soybean proces-
sing plants using solvent extraction. Investments would be used to expand
existing facilities, converting them into efficient working units. The
capacity of these facilities after project implementation would be about 300
to 600 tons/day, a size range which would allow them to compete internationally.
Approximately 20 small and two large feed processing facilities are also
expected to be financed. Plant facilities would range from 6.5 tons to 25
tons per hour. The feedmills would be technically simple, consisting mainly
of storage, mixing and bagging facilities. Some of the feedmills would be
operated by cooperatives which already operate slaughterhouses. Details are
provided in Annex 5.
Milk Proce ssing
4.05      Some 20 milk processing facilities are expected to be financed, of
which the majority would be located in Sao Paulo and Minas Gerais. The sub-
projects would produce mainly powdered milk, butter and pasteurized milk for



- 14 -
local consumption. The location of these subprojects would be in milk produc-
ing regions which have access to the large consumer centers, such as the
western part of Sao Paulo and the northern and eastern parts of Minas Gerais.
Other subprojects would be small units processing milk into cheese and butter.
It is not expected that any subprojects located close to the urban centers of
Sao Paulo and Rio de Janeiro would be financed since both the cooperatives
and the private sector have already established a number of large processing
units in these areas which are not yet fully utilized. Details are provided
in Annex 6.
Miscellaneous
4.06      Subprojects under this category would cover a wide range of indus-
tries that process agricultural produce such as egg packing, fruit and vege-
table canning, and tanneries. Also included would be industries that produce
certain basic inputs for agriculture such as small specialized farm equip-
ment, i.e., sprayers and hand tools, and installations for mixing and handling
of fertilizers. These industries are crucial to the expansion of agricultural
development and the Central Bank has requested their financing in order to
insure that sufficient funds are available to prevent bottlenecks affecting
other activities. In general, the industries to be financed would range
from small to medium in sive. Dptailx are given in Annex 7.
C. Cost Estimates
4.07      Total project cost excluding taxes is estimated at US$260 million
(Cr$ 2,130 million), of which US$83 million (Cr$ 681 million), or 32%, re-
presents the foreign exchange requirements. Estimated project costs are
given below and phasing is indicated in Annex 8.

facilities financed by the participating banks, on data provided by Brazilian
manufacturers, and on the findings of a study conducted by consultants on the
likely costs of agro-industrial equipment in Brazil between 1976 and 1978.
Physical contingencies are included in the cost of installation.

The foreign exchange component includes both the estimated direct and indirect
foreign exchange content of the investment. It is expected that Bank funds
provided under the project would be committed over a three-year period
(Annex 3).
D. Financing
4.09      The project would be financed as follows:
US$ Million          7
Sub-borrowers                       52             20
Federal Government
(Central Bank)                        106             41
Participating Banks                       19              7
Bank                                      83             32
TOTAL PROJECT COST                  260           100
4.10      The Bank loan of US$83 million would finance about 32% of total
project costs, which would be equivalent to 100% of the estimated foreign ex-
change component of fixed assets (US$230 million) and of the permanent working
capital (US$30 million). The Central Bank would contribute 41%. The balance
would be provided by sub-borrowers from their own resources, by the participating
banks, and by additional debt financing obtained from non-project funds. The
participating banks would require sub-borrowers to maintain prudent debt/equity
ratios (Schedule A). It is estimated that, on average, participating banks
would contribute about 7% of total project costs. Working capital would
normally be financed by commercial banks, but, in special cases, if no other
sources were available, permanent working capital, not exceeding 30% of the
fixed investment cost of each subproject, would be financed by the project.
4.11      The Bank loan, to be disbursed over five and a half years, would be
made to the Government, which would bear the foreign exchange risk. The
Government would make the proceeds of the loan available to the Central Bank
on the same financial terms and conditions as the Bank loan. An assurance
on this was obtained at negotiations. The Central Bank would on-lend to the
participating banks under subsidiary loan agreements incorporating the
provisions of Schedule A; their execution to the satisfaction of the Bank
would be a condition of disbursement.
4.12      Loans to sub-borrowers would have terms of 10 years, including
grace periods of up to three years. Hence, with loans commencing in three
successive years, the last repayment would be made 13 years after the start
of the project, expected to be around August 1976. The cash flow projection



- 16 -
made by the Central Bank indicates that sub-borrowers' debt service payments,
at an estimated average interest rate of 4.3% with indexing, would generate
sufficient funds to repay the Bank loan within a period of 15 years, includ-
ing four years' grace, and accepting a reasonable degree of slippage in
disbursements and payments (Annex 9).
E. Procurement
4.13      Procurement procedures would be the same as those applied under the
first loan. Equipment needed for the project would be procured by sub-
borrowers either from suppliers of their existing equipment, if required for
purposes of standardization, or from new suppliers after price comparison
among at least three suppliers (see Schedule A). Bid awards would be reviewed
by participating banks. It is expected that most of the procurement would be
local. Domestic firms produce a wide range of the equipment needed and they
have demonstrated an ability to compete internationally. Foreign firms are
widely represented by domestic affiliates that are expected to bid for project
contracts.
F. Disbursement
4.14      Disbursement of the Bank loan would be made against 40% of subloans
disbursed by participating banks to sub-borrowers. All participating banks
would make disbursements in installments to insure that the release of each
installment would be conditional upon satisfactory implementation of each
stage and that agreed procurement procedures had been followed. Disbursement
requests to the Bank would be accompanied by statements of expenditure
certified by the Central Bank. The supporting documentation would be retained
by the participating banks for review by the Bank during supervision. The
participating banks' disbursement to sub-borrowers would take about five years,
while disbursement of the Bank loan is expected to take about five and a half
years. The estimated schedule for disbursements is given in Annex 10.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Project Administration
5.01      The Project Team established within the Central Bank to administer
the first project would continue to handle the proposed second project. Ori-
ginally the Central Bank contained an administrative unit entitled Gerencia
do Coordenacao do Credito Rural e Industrial (GECRI) whose function it was to



- 17 -
administer both industrial and rural credit projects, including foreign lines
of credit in support of special development programs. In January 1975, GECRI
was reorganized into two divisions: Gerencia do Credito Rural (GERUR), dealing
was the administration of rural credit, and Gerencia do Credito Industrial
e Programas Especiais (GESPE) with responsibility for industrial credit and
special programs. The Project Team was placed within GESPE and consisted of
10 financial and technical specialists (representing about one-third of GESPE's
total professional staff). Its main function was to evaluate subloan applica-
tions submitted by the participating banks and insure satisfactory execution
of subprojects.
5.02      The reorganization that has occurred is considered to be generally
satisfactory. However, with the commencement of the second project, the
Project Team would need further strengthening. First, the staff would require
greater expertise in the highly specialized field of food processing technology.
Second, it would need expertise in marketing, since the sensitivity of a
specific subproject to short- and long-term marketing projections is of the
greatest importance. This is particularly true since the Central Bank must
decide priorities among various alternatives submitted by various participating
banks. Third, the staff of the Project Team would require additional capabi-
lity to assess adequately the managerial capacity of subprojects. The Central
Bank is aware of these needs and has agreed that it should arrange for the
training of two senior members of the Project Team in the fields of food
technology and the management and financing of agro-industry programs. Im-
provements would also be made in the capability of the Project Team to assess
market prospects and to evaluate and design management systrims. The
Central Bank would continue to consult with the Bank on the qualifications
and terms of reference of key staff for the Project Team and to hire con-
sultants as required for the technical review of subprojects. Additionally,
the Central Bank would, with Bank assistance, provided through the Development
Banking Project, support the efforts of BNDE to improve the management and
technical capabilities of the participating banks. An assurance on these points
was obtained at negotiations from the Central Bank.
B. Participating Banks
5.03      The main participating banks in the first project are the Banco de
Desenvolvimento do Estado de Sao Paulo (BADESP), the Banco de Desenvolvimento
do Extremo Sul (BRDE), the Banco de Desenvolvimento do Parana (BADEP), and
the Banco do Brazil (RB). Less active participants are the Banco do Desen-
volvimento do Espirito Santo (BANDES) and the Banco do Desenvolvimento do
Minas Gerais (BDMG). All six would continue to participate in the second
project. In addition, several other banks would probably participate, includ-
ing the Banco do Estado de Santa Catarina (BESC), which is a commercial bank
with a development finance department: the Banco do Estado do Rio de Janeiro
(BDRIO); and a number of private investment banks, provided they are found
suitable by the Central Bank. Participating banks would lend to sub-borrowers
in their own particular state, while the BB and eligible private banks would
lend throughout the project area as a whole.



- 18 -
5.04      The participating state development banks are competently managed.
The biggest problem facing many of them is an erosion of their capital
(para 2.11). Under the development banking project, the Government has
agreed to recapitalize these banks. An assurance was obtained at negotia-
tions from the Central Bank that it would support such measures. BB is a
very large organization which is fully competent to participate in the project,
although its relative lack of regional decentralization and expertise in the
particular type of subloans involved could restrict its participation. Pri-
vate investment banks are deemed generally adequate but would be subject to
evaluation by the Central Bank in certain cases.
C. Project Fund and Project Account
5.05      Arrangements similar to those governing the first project would be
established for the second project. The Central Bank would establish a
separate Project Fund, which would comprise the proceeds of the Bank loan,
plus the Government's contribution. The participating banks would draw from
this Fund to cover subloan disbursements as stipulated in the subsidiary
loan agreements (Schedule A). The Central Bank would also establish a Project
Account, which would reflect the operations and financial conditions of the
Project Fund, including all sublending transactions under the project. In
addition, each participating bank would maintain a separate account for all
transactions under the project.
5.06      The participating banks would draw 91% of the value of approved
subloans from the Project Fund at interest rates ranging between 1/2% and
2-1/2%, with full indexing based on ORTN prices, and would lend to sub-
borrowers at interest rates of 3% to 5%, also with full indexing based on
ORTN prices.
D. Lendin Policies and Procedures
Subloan Appraisal_and Supervision
5.07      Potential sub-borrowers would prepare, or commission qualified con-
sultants to prepare, detailed feasibility studies covering the technical,
organizational, financial, and commercial aspects of subprojects to determine
the soundness of the investments and the creditworthiness of the applicants
(Schedule A). All appraisals for subloans exceeding amounts to be determined
for each participating bank would be submitted to the Central Bank for review.
As required by law, meat processing facilities would be subject to approval by
DIPOA (para 2.16). The Central Bank would set subloan approval limits for the
participating banks as appropriate and would advise the Bank accordingly.
Prior Bank approval would be required for:



19 -
(a)  the first subloan presented by a new participating bank,
together with adequate documentation, the Central Bank
attesting to that bank's qualification to participate
in the project:
(b)  subloans with a value in excess of US$5 million to an indi-
vidual sub-borrower; 1/ and
(c)  subloans in excess of US$3 million in respect of non-food or
feed processing subprojects under the miscellaneous category. 2/
Assurances that these procedures anA the lending criteria outlined in
Schedule A would be followed were obtained from the Central Bank during
negotiations.
Subloan Terms and Conditions
5.08      Subloans would be limited to 80% of the total cost of fixed invest-
ment (excluding the cost of land) and permanent working capital and would
have a maturity of up to 10 years, including a maximum grace period of three
years. Interest rates would range between 3% and 5%, depending upon the
state involved, with full indexing based on ORTN prices. 3/ Since the ORTN
prices are tied to the general price index, the real rate of interest would
be about the same.
5.09      The participating banks would pay the Central Bank 1/2% to 2-1/2%
on refinancing, indexed on the ORTN, thus retaining a margin of 2-1/2%.
During the grace period, interest repayment would not be required but would
accrue with principal, the aggregate of which would be subject to indexing on
ORTN prices. After the grace period, principal and interest payments would
be made semi-annually on the basis of the adjusted balances outstanding. A
commitment fee of 1/2% per year would be charged on undisbursed subloan
balances. At the discretion of the participating banks, in consultation with
the Central Bank, an appraisal fee of up to 1% may be charged on the amount
of the investment cost of subprojects.
1/   Under these arrangements, it is expected that the Bank would have to
review from five to 10 subloans per year in order to scrutinize the
generally adequate appraisal procedures and methodology of the Central
Bank.
2!   The lower ceiling for (c) is lower than for (b) because these would be
new areas of lending.
3/   3% for Espirito Santo, Mato Grosso, Goias, Santa Catarina and the region
north of the 18th parallel in Minas Gerais; 5% for all other states.



- 20 -
E. Accounts and Auditinq
5.10      The accounting systems of the participating state development banks
are generally satisfactory and are subject to inspection by the Central Bank.
Each of the participating banks would undergo regular independent audits by
reputable firms which would be additional to, and more comprehensive than,
the inspections by the Central Bank. The Bank would require regular annual
examination by external auditors, acceptable to the Bank (Schedule A).
5.11      The Project Account (para 5.05) in the Central Bank would be audited
annually by an independent auditing unit of the Ministry of Finance. A signed
copy of the auditor's report, including a description of the audit procedures,
together with certified copies of appropriate statements on the Account, would
be forwarded to the Bank within four months of the end of the financial year.
Assurances on all these provisions were obtained from the Central Bank during
negotiations.
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, PRICES AND OPERATING RESULTS
A. Production
6.01      The investment in meat processing facilities should result in higher
quality meat, increased production for both the domestic and foreign markets,
and better utilization of by-products. The output from the beef processing
plants should be about 300,000 tons per year, of which about 30,000 tons is
expected to be exported. Output from the pork processing units should be
about 40,000 tons per year, and from the poultry processing about 100,000 tons
per year, both items being primarily for the local market. The soybean mills
expected to be financed under the project should produce about 50,000 tons of
oil and about 200,000 tons of meal annually. Investment in feedmills should
provide milling facilities for about 600,000 tons of grain per year, which
is equivalent to about 10 of national production during 1974. The milk
processing units that would be financed under the project should produce
about 16,800 tons of powdered milk per year, 8,500 tons of butter, and 16,000
tons of cheese. Investments in the miscellaneous category are presently un-
specified so that the resulting increase in processing capacity cannot be
predicted.
B. MarketinR
6.02      Domestic demand for meat has been in a stable upward trend.  Abun-
dant supplies have been more than ample to meet local market requirements
and the Government has intervened on occasion to reduce seasonal price fluc-
tuations. Due to the need to maintain a steady expansion of beef supplies



- 21 -
to meet rising domestic demand, the export trade has become of secondary
importance to the Government. Around 7n7 of processed beef is exported.
There exists currently a small export trade for high quality pork and there
is substantial potential for increased exports because of relatively high
production costs in Europe. Localized and occasional over-production of
poultry has precipitated producer demands for Government assistance and
export promotion of this product is fast becoming a Government responsibility.
This would require significant up-grading of processing facilities to meet
export market standards. Likely export markets for processed poultry are
Europe and possibly the Middle Eastern countries. There is also likely to
be strong domestic demand since current levels of consumption (2.2 kilogram
per capita per year) are low.
6.03      Prospects are good for soya products produced by the increased
processing capacity. Demand for soya meal is expected to continue to grow
at a steady rate, and recent unfavorable developments are considered as only
temporary factors. World demand for soybean oil is not expected to be as
dynamic as that for soya meal, but the oil can be disposed of profitably on
both the domestic and export markets. The market for processed animal feed
is directly related to the expansion of beef, poultry, and pork production
and has, therefore, considerable potential. While market prospects are
promising, improved quality control will be required to maintain and expand
the domestic market and to benefit from any export potential.
6.04      Marketing problems in the dairy industry reflect mainly the cyclical
character of production, which parallels closely the seasonal beef cattle
cycle. Because of narrow margins between production costs and fixed consumer
prices, fresh milk distribution is barely profitable. Profits at present
must come from dairy products where prices are not regulated.
6.05      Local markets for orange juice concentrate are difficult to expand
because of competition from other fruits, fresh or processed. For the time
being, therefore, the emphasis would be on export markets, where exporters
are confident that the existing penetration could be broadened despite tariff
barriers. The expanding shoe and leather goods industry provides a perma-
nent domestic market for tanned hides, and export potential for hides and
skins appears to be promising. Demand for wool is restricted to the domestic
market and its expansion is related to that of the local textile industry.
C. Prices
6.06      Consumer price policy is regulated by a Comissao Interministerial
de Precos (CIP), which has the responsibility for supervising prices of all
processed or manufactured goods, with the objective of maintaining orderly
price trends. CIP price policy falls into four general categories: (a)
prices regulated a-priori on the basis of consultation with relevant institu-
tions, such as the Superintendencia Nacional do Abastecimiento (SUNAB), Con-
selho Nacional de Abastecimiento (CONAB), and Companhia Brasileira de



- 22 -
Alimientos (COBAL): (b) supervised prices, which can be raised by enterprises
upon notification to CIP; (c) supervised but unregulated prices, which are
under constant surveillance by CIP but are not subject to control; and
(d) sectorial agreements under which CIP, together with the industry concerned,
determines changes in certain price levels.
6.07      Government policy is to control prices with a light hand, allowing
for reasonable cost increases. As soon as the supply of a controlled item be-
comes plentiful, the price is immediately decontrolled. Probably the best
example of the intent and workiings of prtce policy ts the case of fresh meat
which falls under the supervision of SUNAB. In this case, only the beef fore-
quarter is controlled, the better quality hindquarter and edible offal being
allowed to find their own price levels. Poultry and pork are uncontrolled so
that these products can find their respective price levels in relation to the
basic beef price.
D. Operating Results
6.08      Financial models have been prepared to demonstrate the return from
typical investments under the project. The financial rates of return range
from 13% for tanneries to 35% for beef processing plants. Details are pro-
vided in Annexes 4 to 7 and are summarized on the ext page. Such returns
should be sufficiently attractive to insure participation in the project.

VII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
7.01      In assessing the project's economic feasibility, each major compo-
nent has been analyzed separately to determine its economic rate of return
and its contributions to employment and balance of payments. On the basis
of these criteria, meat, oilseed, grain and milk processing are all econo-
mically justified. The economic justification for miscellaneous processing
would vary with the nature of each individual subproject and cannot presently
be determined.



- 24 -
7.02      Primary benefits from increased and improved beef processing facili-
ties include an improvement in beef quality, increased production, and better
utilization of by-products. Benefits from oilseed processing, which would
involve turning small inefficient plants into larger more efficient installations,
would principally take the form of net value added, more efficient oil extrac-
tion and expansion of the feed industry. Milk processing and the processing
of miscellaneous agricultural products would generate primary benefits in
the form of net value added and, in some cases. import substitution.
7.03      For the project as a whole, secondary benefits would accrue to in-
put suppliers. including farmers, civil works contractors, equipment suppliers,
and workers employed in agro-industries. Product users, namely, the general
consuming public, would also benefit from more assured supplies. Aside from
the general secondary benefits, the pror,osed project is designed to give
added impetus to the development of the relatively less developed regie-n"  ^f
the project area, such as Mato Grosso, Santa Catarina and Esnirito Santo.
7.04      On the basis of expected economic costs and benefits, indicative
economic rates of return for individual subprojects have been calculated and
subjected to sensitivity tests. These are detailed in Annex 11, Table 2, and
can be summarized as follows:
Sector
Beef Processing Oil Processing Dairy
Best estimates                       37              45            47
Investment costs up 15%              12              23            18
Operating cost up 20%                32              38            39
Operating income down 15%             5              16            10
7.05      During construction, the project should provide annual employment
for about 4,000 people. Thereafter, approximately 7,000 permanent jobs
should be created in the new plants and processing facilities set up under
the project. While the project would indirectly benefit a wide variety of
people (para 7.03), the relative impact on each group of beneficiaries is
not possible to quantify. Annual revenues generated by the project in the
form of income and value-added taxes can be estimated at about Cr$ 250 million
(US$32 million) at full development. The net export income for the lifespan
of the project is estimated at US$216 million and the foreign currency invest-
ment at US$83 million (para 4.07 and Annex 11).
7.06      Improvements in hygiene and better utilization of by-products should
considerably reduce the existing meat processing plants' undesirable effects
upon the environment. Most new facilities to be financed under the project
would be located close to production areas where population density is con-
siderably lower than in urban centers: hence, the need for safeguards against
air pollution would be minimal. Those located in urban areas are generally



- 25 -
covered by existing zoning laws which take cognizance of such aspects as
noise, odor and air pollution. Adequate safeguards against water pollution
would be provided for under the project through requirements to adhere to
existing regulations. and, in the case of meat processing, specific treatment
of effluent.
7.07      In essence, the project would serve as an important catalyst to
stimulate agro-industrial development in Brazil. It would complement present
efforts to increase agricultural production, thus providing the agricultural
sector with expanding markets and the consuming public with better quality
products at lower real prices. By indirectly assisting the participating
banks, it would help improve their ability and capacity to meet Brazil's con-
tinuing need for medium- and long-term credit. Additionally, the experience
gained with the first and second agro-industrial credit projects would also
be important for other projects of a similar type in areas with a less
developed institutional framework, such as the north and northeast regions of
Brazil.
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.01      During negotiations, assurances were obtained that:
The Government would:
(a) make available US$83 million to the Central Bank on the same
financial terms and conditions as the Bank loan (para 4.11);
and
(b) ensure that Project Account would be audited annually by an
independent auditing unit in the Ministry of Finance and
certified copies and a description of audit procedures would
be forwarded to the Bank within four months of the end of the
financial year (para 5.11).
The Central Bank would:
(a) consult with the Bank on the qualifications and terms of
reference of key staff, including consultants. for the
Project Team. In addition, it would hire consultants as
required for review of subprojects and would train two
senior executives of the Project Team in food technology
and in the economuics of agro-industries (para 5.02);
(b) support the initiatives of BNDE to improve the management
and technical capabilities of the participating banks
(para 5.02);
(c) support BNDE in its efforts to recapitalize the state
development banks (para 5.04);



- 26 -
(d)  obtain prior Bank approval for the first subloan presented
by each new participating bank (para 5.07):
(e)  obtain prior Bank approval for subloans which bring the
aggregate financing under the program for any one
sub-borrower to more than US$5 million or USS3 million
in respect of non-food or feed processing subprojects
under the miscellaneous category (para 5.07); and
(f)  ensure that the lending criteria outlined in Schedule A
would be followed (para 5.07).
8.02      A condition of disbursement against Central Bank refinancing of
subloan disbursements of participating banks would be the signing of sub-
sidiary loan agreements between the Central Bank and the participating banks.
These agreements would incorporate the provisions outlined in Schedule A
(para 4.11) of this report.
8.03      The proposed project constitutes a suitable basis for a Bank loan
of US$83 million, for a term of 15 years, including a four-year grace period.
