BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Lending Criteria
The following provisions would be included in the subsidiary loan
agreements to be signed between the Central Bank and the participating
banks for implementation of the project:
Equity participations
1.   In cases where the participating bank has an equity interest
in the applicant firm, arrangements satisfactory to the Central
Bank would be made for an independent appraisal of the proposed
subproject.
2.   Project funds would not be made available to any participating
bank if:
(a) the aggregate of the participating bank's lending to, and
equity participation in, any single enterprise exceeded
either of the following:
(i)  25% of the participating bank's net worth, i.e.,
paid-up equity plus free reserves, or
(ii) 50% of the total assets of an enterprise
(these limitations would not apply to short-term
financing of seasonal working capital needs), or
(b) the equity investments of the participating bank in any
single enterprise exceeded 20% of the capital of the
enterprise, except in extraordinary circumstances.
Provisions
3.   The participating banks would establish and maintain provisions
by annual appropriations at a level deemed appropriate by the
Central Bank to cover bad debts.
Auditing
4.   Participating banks would be subject to regular examination by
external auditors, acceptable to the Bank, in respect of all their
activities, and a signed copy of the auditors' report, together
with certified copies of annual accounts, would be forwarded to
the Bank within four months of the end of each financial year.



SCHEDULE A
Page 2
In the course of the examination, the auditors would make a
specific review of project accounts and would carry out spot
checks of individual sub-projects to insure that project
conditions were being complied with. The auditors' report
would describe the review and checks undertaken.
5.   In the case of the Banco do Brasil (BB), the following
requirements on accounts and auditing would apply:
(a)  the BB would maintain separate accounts at its head office
for operations under the Lending Program, including disburse-
ment documents for each subloan, and would be subject to
regular annual audit by the Central Bank of project accounts,
including accounts of branches involved in the project;
(b) A signed copy of the Central Bank's auditors' report, to-
gether with certified copies of annual project accounts
kept by BB would be forwarded to the Bank within four
months of the end of BB's financial year. In the course
of their examination, the Central Bank's auditors would
carry out spot checks of individual beneficiaries to insure
that project conditions were being complied with. The
auditors' report would describe the review and checks
undertaken.
Subloan Conditions
6.   Subloans would be limited to 80% of the total cost of fixed
investment (excluding the cost of land) and working capital
up to 30% of fixed investments financed by subloans and
would be granted for a term of 10 years, including a
maximum of three years' grace. They would carry an interest
rate of 3% to 5%, 1/ plus adjustment based on the National
Treasury Bonds (ORTN) price, and a commitment fee of 1/2%
would be paid on undisbursed loan balances. The partici-
pating banks would also charge an appraisal fee of up to 1% of
the investment costs. Refinancing from the Central Bank would
carry an interest rate of 1/2% to 2-1/2% with ORTN indexing.
7.   The participating banks would meet additional short-term financing
requirements for working capital not filled by the sub-borrowers
or the commercial banks or the project.
1/   3% for Espirito Santo, Mato Grosso, Santa Catarina and the region
north of the 18th parallel in Minas Gerais; 5% in all other
states.



SCHEDULE A
Page 3
8.   The participating banks would reimburse the Central Bank
according to the same repayment schedules established for
sub-borrowers.
9.   The participating banks would require a feasibility study to be
undertaken for each subproject. Any applicants considered
by the participating banks as not having adequate expertise
to prepare their own studies would be required to engage
consultants to perform them. Participating banks would
prepare a list of approved consultants for use by the
applicants.
10.   Feasibility studies would cover the technical, organizational,
financial and commercial aspects of subprojects. Particular
attention would be paid to the following:
(a) inclusion of financial statements certified by auditors
approved by the participating banks in the case of sub-
projects involving the rehabilitation or extension of
existing enterprises where total investment under the
project exceed USS1 million:
(b) compliance with all relevant Government regulations,
especially environmental protection standards and, in
the case of beef and milk processing, the federal hygiene
regulations
(c) inclusion of filtration or pondage systems for treatment
of effluents in meat subprojects;
(d) provision for future plant expansion in meat subprojects;
(e) indication of export potential for subprojects falling
in the miscellaneous processing category;
(f) inclusion of a component for training of technical personnel
in each subproject, as appropriate;
(g) presentation of cash flow projections and calculation of
the financial rate of return for each subproject and the
calculation of the economic rate of return for subprojects
requiring authorization by the Central Bank. The appraisal
of projects with a value in excess of US$2 million equivalent
will include the calculation of the economic rate of return,
made either by the state and regional development banks
or by the Central Bank:
(h)  analysis of the adequacy of the supply of raw materials
and the demand for final products, both domestic and foreign,
including projections of both supply and demand.



SCHEDULE A
Page 4
11. Appraisal of subprojects by the participating banks would
cover the same aspects as those included in the feasibility
studies. In addition, the following points would be given
particular emphasis:
(a) managerial competence of the applicants;
(b) financial condition of the applicants, including the
adequacy of their debt/equity ratio and the debt
service coverage after the inclusion of the proposed
investment;
(c) availability of sufficient working capital for the
applicant's present and future operations; and
(d) overall supply and demand situation for the industry,
taking into consideration present and planned capacity
within the participating banks' respective spheres of
operation and, to the extent feasible, in other regions
as well.
12.   The Central Bank and the participating banks would hire consultants
to evaluate the technical aspects of the subprojects involving
expertise not found among their regular personnel.
13.   Procurement procedures would be decided upon by the sub-borrowers
with the concurrence of the participating banks. As a general
rule, the participating banks would require that sub-borrowers
obtain quotations from at least three contractors or suppliers
and submit them to the participating banks for review of quality,
suitability, and price of goods.
14.   The participating banks would disburse directly to suppliers,
except in extraordinary circumstances.
15.   The participating banks would make quarterly inspection visits
to sub-borrowers during the implementation period and annual
visits during the remainder of the subloan term.
16.   The participating banks would insure that all project meat and
milk plants were inspected by DIPOA or equivalent agencies.
17.   All oilseed processing subprojects would involve the adoption of
solvent extraction techniques.



ANNEX 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
General Economy and the Agricultural Sector
1.        The general economy of Brazil and its agricultural sector in
recent years achieved considerable growth. The major parameters of the
economy are reflected in Table 1, which presents some selected national
account statistics.
2.         The growth of the total and economically active population (Table
2) indicates an increasing domestic demand, particularly for the major prod-
ucts of the agro-industrial project which, combined with increasing per
capita incomes, result in a significant expansion of the consumer market.
3.         The value of agricultural exports has also increased, largely
through increased prices in the case of traditional exports, but also in
volumes of non-traditional exports. Table 3 presents the value of selected
traditional and non-traditional agricultural projects for the period 1971-80.
4.         Table 4 presents the estimated production, consumption and demand
of project-related agricultural projects in 1975 and 1980, while Table 5 in-
dicates actual production, area planted, average yields and crop value in the
project area in 1973. These tables indicate the production potential and
projected demand that provide a large portion of project justification.
5.         The Fundacao Getulio Vargas (FGV) published in early 1974 demand
projections for an array of basic consumption items - based on a series of
data for the period 1950-70 - and family budget data collected in consumer
surveys during 1967/68. Per capita consumption data and per capita real
income data were derived on the basis of standard population estimates, and
the income series and cost-of-living index computed and published by FGV.
The latter is used by the Government as the official index. Two sets of
projections of aggregate demand for a range of consumption items are set
forth in Table 6, based on two different sets of elasticities. In Table 7
a range of projections of demand for individual commodities based on differ-
ent rates of increase for the real income variable are presented. The lower
limit of that range is given by FGV nodel, and the upper limit represents
the maximum tolerance for disposable income of consumption expenditures es-
timated according to forecasts of the balance of payment situation.
6.         Finally, production forecasts have been compared with the most
likely demand projection level for each commodity to compute a supply balance
as presented in Table 8. A positive balance indicates a surplus condition,
or export availabilities where external markets exist. A negative balance
would indicate probable import necessity or policy measures to encourage
incremental production to fulfill indicated domestic shortfalls.

ANNEX 2
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Industrial and Agricultural Credit
A. Overview of the Financial System
1.         Brazil's economy is extensively and competitively served by a
network of public and private financial institutions (see Chart 1). The
system is headed by the National Monetary Council and controlled by the
Central Bank (BC). Its other predominant institutions are the Bank of
Brazil (BB) and the National Development Bank (BNDE), the latter supplying
financing facilities to public and private investment banks. Besides the
federally and state-owned commercial and development banks, the country fea-
tures a very substantial and active private banking sector.
B. General Banking Aspects
2.         Whereas the most important source for long- and medium-term credit
to agriculture is BB, BNDE handles by far the largest volume of industrial
investment loans, based mainly on national but also on foreign resources. In
mid-1975 the effective cost of commercial short-term loans was about 30% to
35% per annum, compared to an annual rate of inflation estimated at 25%.
Medium- and long-term non-agricultural loans were generally indexed according
to ORTN 1/ and carried 3% to 12% interest. Agricultural loans bore 0% to 17%
interest without monetary correction; thus, interest in real terms was heavily
negative. 2/
3.         Lending in Brazil is strongly collateral-oriented' and arrears are
small. Few institutions are familiar with the techniques of project lending
and most still have difficulties in project preparation and even more in pro-
ject supervision.
C. Detailed Institutional Features
4.         The Brazilian financial system is controlled by the National Monetary
Council (CMN) appointed by the President of the Republic and headed by the
Minister of Finance. Its members include the Presidents of BC, BB and BNDE.
1/    Monetary correction, widely used in Brazil, is based mainly on the
index of National Treasury Bonds (ORTN), which is based, in turn, on
the general price index.
2/    The exception was the Bank-financed loans for livestock, grain storage,
and agro-industries that do carry monetary correction.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
Its tasks are wider than the name implies and include all facets of financial
and monetary policies.  It is the central policy-making and governing body of
the economy.  The CNN is assisted by a series of advisory committees, includ-
ing committees for agricultural credit and for industrial credit. The CMN
has three executing agencies, BC, BB and BNDE, dealing with various aspects
of financing and controls.
Banco Central do Brasil (BC)
5.         BC is responsible for executing the overall monetary and credit
policies. Its regulations govern the financial system and pertain to finan-
cial transactions as well as to institutional responsibilities and limita-
tions. In addition to its normal functions, it attends to special tasks,
such as the financing of BB, the National Cooperative Bank (BNCC) and other
federal banks, especially in order to assume adequate credit to agriculture.
Recently it established and now administers an agricultural credit insurance
system (PROAGRO). BC is the primary financial channel of the First Agro-
industries Project and supplies all the counterpart lending funds. In 1974
it established a separate department for industrial and special credit (GESPE)
that handles the First Agro-industries Project and would handle the Second
Agro-industries Project by means of a special project unit. Whereas the
majority of the industrial funds are managed by BNDE, the Central Bank ad-
ministers two internal industrial development funds 1/, including the Bank's
Agro-industrial Project.
Banco do Brasil (BB)
6.         BB is placed directly under control of the CMN and only partly
supervised by BC. BB is the country's oldest financial institution and, with
assets equivalent to US$17.6 billion by end 1974, is the largest bank in
Latin America 2/. Its services include comprehensive commercial banking and
a very extensive agricultural credit portfolio. In addition it attends to
specific tasks for the Government such as tax collection, administration of
minimum price programs, purchasing and storage of certain basic commodities,
and financing and control of exports and imports by means of its foreign
trade department (CACEX), which also performs para-fiscal duties such as the
issue of import and export licenses. A "thumbnail sketch" of BB's assets
and liabilities shows:
1/    FIBEB = Financing Fund for the Importation of Productive Assets;
PAFAI - Financial Assistance for Farm Machinery Production.
2/    Until 1964, BB performed the functions of a central bank also.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
(US$ billion equivalent)
Assets          1974      1973       Liabilities          1974       1973
Loans and Credits   16.8 /1   12.1  Capital and Reserves    1.7           1.2
Cash                  0.2      n.a.  Deposits                   9.9       7.4
Others                0.6        0.7  Rediscounts etc.         5.4        3.4
Others                   0.6        0.8
Total                     17.6       12.8                           17.6       12.8
Its debt/equity ratios were 10.3:1 and 11.1:1 in 1974 and 1973, respectively.
7.        The profitability of BB is high, amounting to 52% of yearly average
equity in real terms in 1974. A large volume of Government deposits at zero
percent interest and rediscounts at concessionary rates resulted in the aver-
age cost of deposits being about 1% in 1974, whereas the return on the average
loan was 7.3%; BB's profitability was correspondingly high (para 17).
8.        In 1974, BB accounted for 63% of all agricultural and about 24% of
all industrial loans in Brazil. Though its main business is short-term lend-
ing, its medium- and long-term loans to agriculture and livestock amounted to
US$1.8 billion in 1973, or 39% of all such loans (figure for end of 1974 not
yet available). Some 2,400 of its 57,000 employees are seconded to other
institutions, including Banco Central, thus extending its influence well
beyond its formal boundaries. BB has about 880 branches, 860 of which are
in the country and more than 600 in the project area. Branch managers enjoy
considerable independence of action within strictly defined limits and this,
plus the vast resources they can draw on, make them most formidable compet-
itors for standard banking business. But, lacking regional decentralization,
all operations - including all loans under the Agro-industries Project -
beyond the branches' authority are highly centralized and correspondingly
cumbersome.
9.        BB is the executing agency for the Grain Storate Project (857-BR,
US$30 million of 1972), and, with its deep involvement in agricultural credit
and extensive network of branches, could effectively complement the state
development banks (paras 14 through 18) in executing the Agro-industries
Project. This has not happened so far, however, because of lack of decen-
tralization and technical staff to handle this type of relatively large sub-
project. Branch managers do not appear to have professional backgrounds of
the type that would be conducive to the kinds of subloans that would be dealt
with under the project. Additionally, amounts involved exceed the managers'
lending limit, which means they must get authorization from the head office,
apparently rather time consuming.
10.       BB recently established a Division for Technical Analysis (DIPRO)
in Brasilia to evaluate subprojects, but the unit is small (19 technicians,
to be expanded to 36 within 12 months). Considering the number and dispersal
of branches and managers' lack of familiarity and pressure of other work, it
1/   Including agricultural credits of US$4.7 billion -- industry, US$3.3
billion and US$0.4 billion for food industries, mainly sugar.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
seems doubtful whether one technical unit dealing with the project located
at the head office would have much effect. DIPRO handles, in addition to
agro-industries, all agricultural loans beyond US$250,000 and loans for urban
infrastructure and roads, performs sector studies, and administers BB's tax
incentive investment portfolio. All these tasks seem to prevent BB from
playing a major role in executing the Second Agro-industries Project. To
promote the project by equalizing receipts from it with those of other loans,
the head office gives branches a "book-keeping" credit of 7% interest in ad-
dition to the 5% paid by the sub-borrower. 1/ It also intends to conduct
short courses for branch managers on project preparation.
Banco Nacional de Desenvolvimento Economico (BNDE) 2/
11.       BNDE, also directly controlled by the CMN, is the central agency
for executing public investment and development policies and its influence is
felt throughout the financial system (total assets US$4.1 billion by end 1974).
BNDE lends both directly and indirectly; it is the major financial resource
of the state development banks and is important also to the private invest-
ment banks. Equity amounted to US$2 billion and total borrowings to about
the same amount by end 1974 (1973-US$0.8 billion) 3/, about 17% from external
sources. It follows that the debt equity ratio (1:1) is very low. BNDE
assists the Government in executing its development policies by sectoral and
regional allocation and differential pricing of loans, nearly all of which
carry monetary correction, with interest rates varying between 3% and 9%.
Equity investments amounted to US$0.6 billion in 1974 (US$0.4 billion in
1973). A Bank project to assist the development of small- and medium-scale
industry has been appraised and would be handled by BNDE.
Sectoral Development Banks
12.       The two sectoral development banks -- Banco Nacional da Habitacao
(BNH) and the Banco Nacional do Credito Cooperativo (BNCC) -- do not parti-
cipate in the agro-industries project, but, as the state development banks
draw part of their funds from BNH, some indirect connections exist. BNH is
federally controlled by the Ministry of Interior and channels funds deposited
with the Federal Savings and Loan Association (Caixa Economica Federal) into
mortgage loans for housing. Its operations are substantial, with assets in
1974 of US$6.9 billion. 4/ BNCC is controlled by the Ministry of Agriculture
and lends to agricultural and other cooperatives throughout Brazil by means
of about 20 branches. Its impact is limited by its size. Loans in 1974
amounted to US$70 million.
1/   BB's internal branch profit and loss accounts apparently do not feature
incomes and expenses from monetary correction.
2/   For a detailed analysis see Brazil, Appraisal of Development Banking
Project, Report No. 932a-BR.
3/   In 1974, it started to concentrate the social security funds belonging
to the majority of the federal employees (PASEP and PIS funds)
supposedly amounting to US$150 million yearly.
4I   BNH is associated with the Bank's water supply projects.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
Regional Development Banks (RDBs)
13.       The regional development banks include federally controlled institu-
tions such as the Banco do Nordeste (BNIB) and the Banco da Amazonia (BASA)
established by special law to implement federal policies in their respective
regions. Both are controlled by the Ministry of the Interior and both handle
commercial banking business, basing part of their financial strength on de-
posits from federal tax initiatives. By contrast, the Banco de Desenvolvimento
do Extremo Siil (BRDE), formed under a different decree, is owned by the states
of Rio Grande do Sul, Parana, and Santa Catarina. It does not have access to
federal tax initiatives and is not allowed to accept deposits or conduct
commercial banking business. In a way, it is a true regional development
bank closely resembling the state development banks. Two of the three states
it serves feature also separate state development banks and Rio Grande do Sul
is in the process of organizing one. No division of labor between BRDE and
the respective state development banks has, however, been formulated so far.
State Development Banks (SDBs) 1/
14.       State development banks (SDBs) are the principal financial channels
for the First Agro-industries Project. By end of 1974, Brazil featured 10
SDBs 2/, six of them in the project area. But only two -- BADEP and BADESP --
were actively promoting the project, besides BRDE, a regional development bank
discussed above. SDBs, established since the 1960s, operate within the frame-
work of federal and state policies. They seek to promote economic development
by providing long-term loans to enterprises in priority sectors. Generally,
they do not accept deposits and are not permitted to have branch offices.
Although SDBs are small in comparison to BB and BNDE (the assets of those
participating in the project at the end of 1974 ranged from US$40 to US$350
million), they are an important source of long-term financing for industry,
and especially agro-industry (Tables 1 and 2). SDBs' share of medium- and
long-term loans varies from state to state. In Sao Paulo, it is about 10%
and in Rio Grande do Sul, it is claimed to be about 50%. Almost all the
share capital is in the hands of the respective state governments, with
additional funds coming mainly from BNDE.
15.       SDBs are regulated by the Central Bank, though their financing as
well as their operational affinity allies them strongly with BNDE. The
Central Bank's regulations determine the volume of contractual obligations
per unit of capital: in assets, the banks are allowed to handle loans in
national currency up to five times their equity, plus loans in foreign cur-
rency twice their equity; in liabilities, they may contract external funds
up to 15 times their paid-up capital and reserves. The loan portfolio of
the SDBs is mainly long-term, although some of the banks carry up to one-
third of their portfolios in short-term loans, about two-thirds of total loans
go to industry and the rest mainly to agriculture.
1/   For detailed analysis see Brazil, Appraisal of Development Banking
Project, Report No. 932a-BR.
2/   The SDB of Rio de Janeiro is to be enlarged in the wake of the creation
of the new State of Rio de Janeiro; its paid-up capitalof ^r$ 40 mil-
lion is to be increased to Cr$ 100 million by mid-1975. The 11th SDB,
BADESUL, sponsored by the State of Rio Grande do Sul, is to be launched
in early 1976 with an initial capital of Cr$ 150 million.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
16.        The Boards of Directors are politically appointed, with the State
Governor having the principal say. The banks have rather extensive techni-
cal staff, 1/ and are thus conditioned to project lending to an extent not
normally found in Brazil. Recently, however, most SDBs seem to have diffi-
culty in paying salaries adequate to expand their trained technical staff
or even to retain personnel they do have. Loans under the First Project
have been based on generally competent appraisals. Arrears on total port-
folio do not exceed 1% so far, but the banks are relatively young and many
loans are still in their grace period.
17.        In recent years profits have been insufficient to compensate for
the effects of inflation on the equity of the actively participating SDBs,
as shown below:

Though almost no dividends were paid by the SDBs in the period shown and no

erosion was roughly equal to the rate of inflation. (This contrasts sharply
with BB's profit of 22% in real terms for the last two years.) This capital
erosion (though replenished by additional shareholders' equity investment) has
been caused mainly by certain types of loans made without monetary correctioLi,
the spread and leverage failing to compensate for inflation (para 18), a
reduction in spreads in certain lines of credit by BNDE (asking the SDBs to
contribute "to the maintenance of momentum in the economic development") and --
in certain banks -- high adi;,inistrative costs connected with relatively small
loans. The managements of the state development banks have been only partly
alerted to the erosion of Lhieir capital, were unable to define their loss
enters, and have not ye. taken remedial action or even planned such action to
increase their profitability in real terms.
1/    BADESP employs 63 economists, 28 engineers and agriculturalists.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
18.        In order to determine the loss and profit centers, the mission
analyzed Banco de Desenvolvimento de Sao Paulo (BADESP) data for the first
five months of 1975 and found that losses were mainly in rural credit.
Assuming an annual inflation rate of 25%, no loan losses, and a 5% return
on equity in real terms to build up reserves for future losses and/or pay
dividends, the interest spread on the total loan portfolio to cover the
non-financial lending costs had to amount to 2.3%. The average spread on
non-agricultural credits (2.02%) approached this figure, whereas the margin
on agricultural loans averaged only 0.27%. Agricultural loans were given
without monetary correction and their overall spread and leverage (1 to 4.5)
were absolutely unable to compensate for the rate of inflation (assumed at
25% per year). Although agricultural credits account for only 22% of total
loans. BADESP must expect a further erosion of its capital in 1975. Only
if the yearly rate of inflation would drop below 15% or margins and/or lever-
age would improve considerably could BADESP break even (Table 3). The sub-
ject of capital erosion and eventual Bank actions to confront have been
elaborated further in the context of the ongoing DFC loan to BNDE and SDBs.
SDB's accounts are professionally kept and adequately audited by external
auditors.
Private Investment Banks (PIBs)
19.        Brazil features 41 private investment banks (179 branches), fi-
nanced mainly by placing bonds on the money market and accepting term deposits
featuring various types of monetary correction. Additional means are supplied
by BNDE and external lenders such as the US Export-Import Bank. Several of
the PIBs are affiliated with Brazil's major commercial banking groups, and,
by means of the latter's network of branches, are able to operate nationwide.
Out of their internal resources, they lend both for working capital and for
medium-term investments. Normal rates of interest on their own resources are
12%, plus monetary correction, or 30 to 40% without monetary correction. Some
of the PIBs have technical staffs of engineers and agronomists, while others
contract consultants. Considering the financial strength of the major banks,
their facilities for short-term working capital loans, their decentralized
operations and the availability of well paid technical staff, selected PIBs
appear to be promising channels for the project.
D. Evaluation
20.        In the final analysis, Brazil has an extensive, basically sound
and well regulated banking system, effectively used as an instrument for the
execution of Government policies. Also, the system has had more experience
in operating under inflationary conditions than its counterparts in most
other countries.
21.        BNDE and the regional and state development banks are more devel-
opment oriented than the private banking sector, but also more sensitive to
political pressures. Financial management expertise is still rather limited
and thinly spread.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
22.        Banks lend mainly according to account profitability and collateral.
Project lending -- though making progress -- is still in iLs infancy. Some
broadening of the panel of PBs would insure the use of a larger slhare of the
banking system's scarce technical staff. Loan contracts in Brazil seem to be
deficient in that they do not feature certain convenants important for conduct-
ing business, such as assurances by tne borrower to maintain adequate equity.
23.        Though laudable first efforts have been made by the Centro
Brasileiro de Assistencia Gerencial a Pequena e Media Empresa (CEBRAE) to
supply extension to small industries, these will encounter difficulties in
obtaining long-term financing, partly because of lack of extension support.

ANNEX 4
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Meat Processing
1.         Beef processing for the export market was the principal sub-
sector in Agro-industries Credit Project I, with emphasis on expansion and
modernization of slaughterhouses to bring its beef industry up to interna-
tional standards. However, a lagging export market during 1974, and early
1975, has increased the importance of beef processing for the domestic
market, while at the same time official steps to raise the price of beef
in Brazil gradually to bring it closer to the international price have
opened possibilities for building up a good market for alternative sources
of meat, particularly pork and chicken.
2.         The expanding activities of the Departmento Nacional de Inspecao
de Produtos de Origem Animal (DIPOA), which supervises and controls the
processing of animal products, including beef, pork, poultry, eggs , and
other meats, such as mutton, rabbit, horsemeat and goatmeat, have resulted
in considerable pressures being placed on industrial establishments to bring
their operations up to the standards set by the agency. The seriousness of
the effort to improve is reflected in the fact that in the State of Rio de
Janeiro alone, 36 slaughterhouses were expected to be closed down in July
1975 for not measuring up to minimal standards. Pork and poultry processing
industries mostly seem to meet requirements, possibly because they are fairly
new.  These meats not only provide an economical alternative to beef, but their
use will help free beef products for the export market when it opens up again,
particularly in Europe.
3.         Expansion of the pork and poultry processing industries is closely
related to expansion of the feed industry (Appendix 3-2) and, of course, to
growth of the oilseed processing industry (Appendix 3-1). Pork and poultry
constitute not only an alternative source of meat, but a logical market for
the expected increase in oilseed meal production, particularly soybean meal.
4.         In order to best deal with the major meat processing industries,
this annex has been subdivided into three appendices - beef processing,
Appendix 2-1; pork processing, Appendix 2-2; and poultry processing industry,
Appendix 2-3.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Beef Processing
A. Production
1.         Brazil's total cattle herd was estimated at 82,463,300 head in 1973
(Table 1) according to IBGE and the Banco Central, and of these, 66,218,000,
or 80%, were in the project area. The Instituto de Planejamento Social e
Economico (IPEA) estimates that the percentual distribution by type of herd
is as follows:
Milk      Beef       Mixed
Brazil                    14.9      49.6       35.5
Southeast                 17.0      39.2       43.8
South                     18.0      67.6       14.4
Center-West                8.9      49.5       41.6
2.         In 1972, a total of 12,160,000 head were slaughtered in the project
area, also representing 80% of the total slaughter in Brazil.  Estimates made
by the Banco Central indicate a cumulative growth rate of 5.1% for the national
herd since 1970, and, although productivity rates vary from one region to
another, they were similar in 1970 among the three regions comprising the
project area (16.8% for the Southeast; 16.2% for the Center-West, and 15.8%
for the South). The supply of cattle for slaughter is expected to increase
only moderately because of increased retention of cattle of reproductive age.
The expected annual slaughter rates are 13.3% for 1975, 13.6% for 1980, and
13.8% for 1985.
3.         A livestock development program, similar to the CONDEPE program,
which includes 3.5% of the cattle population, should reach an approximate
35% of the Brazilian herd by 1980. The goals of this program include tripling
the annual supporting capacity of natural pastures and improvement of other
areas; increasing the rate of weaning from 50 to 70%; reducing the mortality
rate from 5 to 2%, and, lowering the slaughter age in males from 4.2 to three
years. These measures are intended to help alleviate the problems facing
the cattle industry, caused mainly by a rate of herd productivity that is too
low to keep pace with population increases.
4.         Efficiency of production has had significant increase only in Rio
Grande do Sul where land is limited. In that state, average slaughter age
has been lowered to three years instead of the current average 4.5 years
elsewhere. Carcass yields surpass 50% compared to 40 to 45% in other states.
Depletion of natural pastures and lack of adequate crossbreeding are among



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 2
the major causes of low productivity. Earlier first calving than the current
average four years is still the exception. Increased investments will be
necessary to improve pastures, provide supplementary feed during the dry
season, increase inoculations and the like if productivity is to be increased.
This investment, however, would result in higher beef prices and eventually in
lower domestic consumption, unless productivity gains were sufficient to com-
pensate for the new investment.
5.         A recent National Cattle Plan, devised to help the industry increase
productivity sufficiently to maintain a 12% slaughter rate, recommends meas-
ures such as providing credits for Cr$ 1 billion annually for pasture creation
and improvement, gradully freeing cattle and meat prices from controls, es-
tablishing a 150,000-ton "regulatory" stock managed by the Companhia Brasileria
de Alimentos (COBAL), exempting interstate transactions, and Government com-
mitting itself to buy if producer prices fall below the established range.
B. The Industry
Structure
6.         The beef processing industry in the project area can be charac-
terized as a nationally guided operation composed mainly of single plants
owned by individuals or corporations. Cooperatives are important only in
Rio Grande do Sul, where they own 14 of the 41 operating plants, represent-
ing 41% of the total daily killing capacity of the state. Only four com-
panies operate in more than one state, and two were international firms
that have now been sold to local interests. Frigorifico Anglo is the major
international company still in business. It has a plant in Sao Paulo and
another in Rio Grande do Sul. The total daily capacity of both is 1,680
head per day, so they are not significant factors in Brazilian meat production.
7.         The trend is toward installing larger integrated plants to supply
both the domestic and foreign markets. These plants are designed to indus-
trialize most of the available by-products that cannot be utilized by the
smaller units because of their limited volume. Of the 185 plants now oper-
ating or projected in the study area, 29 have a capacity of between 700 and
1,000 head per day, 43 between 400 and 700 head per day, 48 between 200 and
400 head per day, and 65 under 200 head per day. In the latter group, 35
kill less than 100 head per day. These smaller plants will either have to
expand their operations or go out of business due to competitition from the
larger more efficient units. An important factor in the movement towards
larger plants has been the sanitary standards established by the Departmento
de Inspecao de Productos de Origem Animal (DIPOA), wihich though not discri-
minatory, will eliminate many of the smaller "matadouros" that cannot mo-
dernize their facilities to meet these regulations.
8.         Rio Grande do Sul has the greatest concentration of plants, with
41 operating units, though their theoretical daily killing capacity is only



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 3
11,835 head per day. Sao Paulo has 36 plants in operation, with a daily
capacity of 18,820 head, followed by Minas Gerais with 24 units, Parana with
23, and Mato Grosso, Rio de Janeiro and Santa Catarina, each with 14. Sao
Paulo, with the second largest number of plants, produces 63% of the cattle
it slaughters and receives the balance of its requirements from Mato Grosso
(20%), Goias (8%), Parana (6%), and Minas Gerais (3%). Goias, Mato Grosso,
and Rio Grande do Sul produce 100% of their requirements; Parana 90%; Rio
de Janeiro 73%; and Minas Gerais 66% but imports 26% from Goias, 3% from
Bahia and 2% from Sao Paulo. Espirito Santo produces only 41% of its re-
quirements and purchases the rest from Minas Gerais (45%) and Bahia (14%).
Regulations
9.         The Brazilian standards now being used by the meat industry con-
form to those in effect in Western Europe and the United States. DIPOA is
responsible for establishing and enforcing these regulations and exchanges
information with agencies in many foreign countries to maintain standards
at current international technological levels.
10.        The regulations published by DIPOA cover both the construction of
meat processing plants and inspection procedures for meat products as they
flow through the plant. In the future, all meat plants will have to be
certified, which will insure that meat for domestic consumption will be
prepared under sanitary conditions and be fit for human consumption.
Plant Operations
11.        New and established meat processing plants certified by DIPOA are
satisfactory from a technical viewpoint. In most instances, the material
flow conforms to modern standards and operating methods. The cattle are re-
ceived, weighed and inspected by veterinarians prior to entering the holding
pens. Any suspect animals are separated for further inspection. Diseased
animals are killed in a separate building and cremated, though some of the
parts that are not condemned can be used for tankage. The killing and car-
cass handling is done in the usual manner, with veterinary inspections at all
critical stages of production. Any suspect carcasses or parts are placed in
a separate room for further inspection, which makes it impossible for these
items to return to the production line unless approved by the veterinarian.
The carcasses are chilled and then prepared for domestic sale or the export
market. Many plants are considering expanding their boned beef operations for
both markets. Edible offal is prepared in individual rooms, then packaged and
kept chilled or frozen. Inedible offal is used for tallow, blood meal, bone
meal, and pumped to a separate building for processing. Hides, horns, hooves
and hair are all used in the larger plants, which compare favorably with
operations in the developed countries.
12.        Most plants in Brazil are still being constructed on a multi-
stories basis that allows parts to flow by gravity chutes from the killing
area to lower floors for processing. Multi-storied structures cost more
than single storied bui ldings, in which sanitary conveyors can replace chutes.
In any case, chutes are much more difficult to maintain in hygenic condition
than conveyors.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 4
13.        New plants seem to be located more and more in the raw material
supply sections of the country rather than in the finished product marketing
areas. This should improve the quality and weight of the cattle as well as
the profit margins in production and sales since it should be cheaper to ship
carcasses or boned beef than live animals.
14.        In analyzing feasibility studies, special attention should be givdm
to the number in days of kill per year, which varies in each state. Based on
a report prepared by the Banco Central (BC) in 1973, the longest average
annual kill in Brazil occurred in Espirito Santo with 250 days, followed by
Mato Grosso with 234 days, Minas Gerais and Sao Paulo with 208 days, Parana
with 195 days, and Santa Catarina with 160 days. Rio Grande do Sul did not
indicate the days involved but their main season runs from March to August,
with the peak period being March to June.
15.        Another point to consider is the kill ratio of steers to cows.
Many projects plan on killing only steers, which give them a better yield
ratio, but this practice will soon increase the cow population in Brazil.
Dressed weight of carcasses also vary by state and time of year of slaughter.
Consequently, it may be worthwhile to develop some average figures for each
state that could be used as standards for the different projects.
16.        Obtaining management for the new and larger plants may become a
critical matter because the supply of experienced operating personnel is
limited in Brazil. More sophisticated management is needed in these new
plants than in smaller matadouros, especially when handling by-products,
cooked frozen beef, and boned beef for export, where sanitation and quality
controls are important.
Inspection Program
17.        DIPOA is responsible for the inspection program in all certified
plants. At present it has a staff of between 500 and 600 trained veterinar-
ians and has planned a recruiting program that this year is expected to add
125 to 200 more. The new personnel will receive four-month training conducted
at a practical level in operating frigorificos, then assigned to their per-
manent jobs. Their training, however, may not be up to past standards and
will require more in-plant training to insure a supply of competent personnel.
18.        Each plant that is certified, depending on its size, has one or
two veterinarians assigned to its staff, who are responsible for inspection
and sanitation. Trained technicians do the actual inspection on the killing
floor and any abnormalities are referred to the veterinarians for further
inspection.
19.        Before the start of the day's operation, the veterinarian must
inspect the facilities to insure that they are in sanitary condition, check
all equipment that will be used in handling meat, both for cleanliness and
operation, verify that the work shift is in clean uniforms, that the people
are healthy, and do not have cuts or injuries that can affect the material
to be processed.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 5
20.        A staff of eight to 12 technicians inspects the meat that is being
processed. The usual inspections are provided for, that is, head and tongue,
intestinal tract, liver, lungs and heart, kidneys, forequarter and hindquarter.
The veterinarians inspect any abnormalities in addition to checking on the
condition of animals to be slaughtered and making an autopsy of any animals
that have died or are suspect of diseases.
21.        Upon completion of the day's kill, the veterinarian is also respon-
sible for the cleanup and sanitation of all facilities, including the live
animal holding pens.
Equipment
22.        Most of the equipment used in meat packing plants is available in
Brazil. The quality of conveyors, tables, inspection lines, chutes, rendering
equipment, boilers, refrigeration machinery, and tanks seem to be acceptable.
The welds on stainless steel, which is all imported, is not up to normal
sanitary standards, and, though they are not as critical in equipment for the
meat industry as in others they could be improved. Some items that are highly
specialized must be imported such as hide pullers, automatic meat saws, some
refrigerator doors, paunch cleaning machines, casing cleaning and processing
equipment, place freezers, blood drying, vacuum packaging, and similar items
that are fairly sophisticated and for which the demand is so limited that
local manufacture is not warranted.
23.        Many of the large international companies either have offices or
representatives in Brazil. Consequently, they can furnish quotations on their
specialized machinery. The smaller foreign companies that are not represented
in Brazil are known by the industry through advertising in national trade
magazines and the individual entrepreneurs can request their quotations for
equipment when preparing a project.
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
24.        Beef production has been rising at a rate of 7.6% per annum and, in
1974, reached a level of 2.2 million tons, compared with 1.7 million tons in
1970. Consumption of beef is growing at 9% per year and, in 1974, totalled
2.1 million tons, compared with 1.5 million tons in 1970. Per capita con-
sumption in 1974 was about 20 kg, as compared with 16.9 kg in 1970, indicating
a growth rate of 4.6% (Table 2).
25.        Since mid-1974, availabilities of beef for local consumption have
been abundant due to the temporary closing off l>- the major EEC markets, which,
in fact, caused a backing up of beef supplies in local storage facilities.
The situation was compounded by imports of 52,000 tons of beef in 1974 from



ANNEX 4
Appen.iix 4-1
Page 6
Argentina and Uruguay under the "regulatory stocks" policy and for whose pur-
chase contracts had been signed before the EEC ban on beef imports went into
effect. The stocks of beef acted as a depressant on the domestic beef market,
but imports eventually were largely absorbed by processors and reexported in
various product forms.
26.        Purchases of 150,000 tons of domestic beef during the first semester
of 1975 under the regulatory stocks policy served as an important market
support measure, enabling the frigorificos to operate profitably and alleviat-
ing pressure on producer prices which reportedly had fallen to Cr$ 2.00 per
kg for fat cattle.
27.        Under the regulatory stocks policy, as it pertains to beef, the
Comissao Interministerial de Precos (CIP) 1/ determines consumer price policy.
Then, on the basis of estimates of domestic availabilities, consumption re-
quirements and export demand, the indicated shortfall in domestic beef sup-
plies is computed. Import policy is determined and COBAL 2/, the commercial
arm of the Ministry of Agriculture, that is authorized to arrange for imports 3/
of the quantity deemed necessary to stabilize beef prices at the predetermined
levels.
28.        Imports of frozen and chilled beef from Argentina and Uruguay are
expected to be negligible in 1975, so that during the low slaughter season
in the second semester, the large stocks should be absorbed, and the industry
should enter 1976 in approximate equilibrium. Although imports are not ex-
pected to be important in 1975, that trade could again expand in 1976 to the
1974 level. The important determinant will be the course of export trade in
the coming years. In the event that the beef export market in Europe opens
up, a larger shortfall will result, necessitating a greater volume of com-
pensating imports. In respect of beef imports, the history of that trade
merits mention. In the 1970-73 period, imports of fresh, chilled, frozen,
and dried beef, primarily from Argentina and Uruguay averaged 2,300 tons per
year. Virtually all of the 52,000 tons imported in 1974 came from those two
countries. With world beef market expected to return to normal around 1977,
long term import prospects from Argentina and Uruguay are important. How-
ever, since those countries export high quality cuts to the European and U.S.
markets while Brazil imports lesser quality cuts, there does not seem to be
reason for concern.
29.        The rural market, estimated to make up about 40% of the total beef
market in Brazil, is currently supplied mainly by small, non-inspected plants
and almost all beef is sold to the retailer, mainly as bone-in fresh sides or
1/    The agency which is responsible for price policy at all levels of
industry and trade.
2/    The agency that purchases and sells commodities in conjunction with
implementation of producer and consumer price policies.
3/    Mainly from Argentina and Uruguay.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 7
quarters, unprocessed, except for basic cleaning. The utilization of beef and
edible offal is quite high, but losses nevertheless occur because no further
processing is done; inedible by-products are usually not utilized. Unreported
slaughter supplements the small plant-local butcher supply system.
30.        In the urban market, significant changes are occurring in the dis-
tribution system. The supermarket chains are expanding steadily, both in
terms of area coverage, number of units, and size of units. In Rio, about
60% of the retail grocery trade now is handled by supermarkets. In Sao Paulo,
that ratio is about 40%. Merchandising of beef products has greatly improved
in these outlets, which also enjoy a strong purchasing position that allows
them to gain economies in direct buying in large volume from processors that
hopefully can be passed on to the consumer.
31.        The beef product mix is bound to be affected by modern merchandis-
ing methods, improved personal incomes and changing tastes. There is limited
but widening acceptance of frozen beef, mainly because of better handling
and presentation of the product and broader consumer education and knowledge.
Processed beef products such as sausages and vacuum-packed, cooked frozen and
canned beef products are also gaining acceptance in the large urban centers.
Witness to the change in taste are the considerable numbers of relatively
small specialized meat products plants that have sprung up during the last
decade near the larger consumption centers. The large slaughterhouses in or
near the major cities also manufacture and market these products. For the
present, the development of ready meats, frozen TV dinners, and boil-in-bag
are in the test market stage, but some of the large slaughterhouses may go
into regular production of limited quantities of such items in the near
future. A large segment of the urban market, however, is still dominated
by consumer preference for non-frozen fresh meat.
32.        Frigorificos presently under the DIPOA inspection and regulatory
system number 185 units: 113 of these have cold storage capacity of 300 to
399 m3 or less and 72 have capacity of 400 to 499 m3 or more. The latter
group has a total of 147,000 tons of cold storage capability and 24,000 tons
of chilling capacity, for a total of 171,000 tons. In the first semester of
1975, 70 frigorificos were selcted on the basis of DIPOA standards to process
and hold the 150,000 tons of beef bought up by COBAL under the "regulatory
stocks" policy to supplement supplies to the domestic market during the low
season (para 26). Since those enterprises need cold storage facilities for
their usual operations, facilities of the Companhia Brasileira de Armazenamento
(CIBRAZEM) 1/ as well as other private storage space was contracted.
33.        The need for cold storage capacity as well as processing plants
is clear.  On the basis of the foregoing calculations--allowing for average
turn-over of five times per year--1.1 million tons to 1.2 million tons could
be put through the industry's facilities. Production in 1980 is forecast at
1/    The government warehousing company.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 8
2.9 million tons, so that the above volumes represent 37 to 41% of that year's
output. Considering the lead time required for site selection, design, req-
uisition of materials and equipment, construction, training, and breaking in
new plant, there is clearly a degree of urgency in this situation.
34.        Transport.  By far the largest proportion of beef is transported
by truck. Trucking is usually for the account of the buyer, so that producer
prices as reported are f.o.b. ranch. For haulage of processed beef, the
refrigerated truck fleet in the country is described as adequate for the
industry. Many of the trucks, of course, are multi-purpose and are utilized
to move an increasingly complex variety of refrigerated consumer items in
addition to meat products.
35.        Compared to other regions of Brazil, the Center-South has a rela-
tively well developed transport system. The rate of expansion and improvement
of major highways has been very rapid in recent years, and the program is
being continued under a master plan, partially assisted by the Bank. The
rural road system is receiving increased attention: a program to develop
10,000 km of roads began two years ago. The railroad system, important to
beef transport in Sao Paulo state particularly, is also receiving increased
attention. In the aftermath of the petroleum crisis, emphasis has shifted
from highway development to the railroads. However, over the medium term,
most inland transport will continue to be by refrigerated truck except in
localized rural markets where unrefrigerated meat is transported by regular
trucks.
36.        Comparative Income Elasticity and Per Capita Meat Consumption.
The income elasticity of demand for beef in most countries is generally high
compared with demand for substitutes; also, it tends to increase with the
rise in incomes until consumption per head reaches the levels recorded in
developed countries. The cross-price elasticity with non-meat substitutes,
on the other hand, tends to be low at low income levels. For the world as
a whole, the income elasticity averages 0.38, while the price elasticity of
demand in major importing countries is estimated at -1.25. For Brazil in
its present stage of development, it can be assumed that rising incomes will
likely be reflected in relatively greater gains in beef consumption than in
other meats, unless price policy is manipulated to favor consumption of other
high protein foods.
Export Market
37.        Despite the fact that considerable attention has been directed to
exports, only a small proportion of output actually leaves the country. In
1970/71 exports accounted for about 8.4% of production. At the peak of
193,000 tons in 1972, fresh beef exports took 9.1% of total output. Processed
beef production is export-oriented and 70% or more is shipped abroad, but that
product accounted for only about 2% of total beef availabilities and is based
on off-grade meat. Projections of the future beef balance in the country
indicate that, in the 1975-85 period, the exportable surplus will range from
2.4% to 7.5% of aggregate output.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 9
38.        The suspension of beef exports in 1973 and the cutoff of major
European markets in 1974 caused exports to diminish rapidly, reversing the
uptrend that had been in evidence since 1968. That trend in beef trade is
shown below:
Exports                Index
Year             (1,000 tons)           1968 = 100
1968                 59                   100
1969                 97                   164
1970                118                   200
1971                123                   208
1972                193                   327
1973                134                   227
1974                 45                    76
Source: CACES and IBGE.
The contraction in beef shipments was particularly severe in 1974 and the
year-to-year decline continues, amounting to 60%.
39.        Because the bulk of exports to the EEC consisted of frozen and
chilled beef, those categories experienced the sharpest contraction in ship-
ments. Canned, cooked and processed beef products held up better because
shipments to North America and other markets for those products were not
subject to arbitrary limitations. The proportions of beef product types in
the export trade are shown in Table 3. On balance, the likelihood does not
appear strong that current actions in restraint of beef support policy will
have a discentive effect that will set the stage for a down cycle in EEC
beef production by 1978/79.
40.        The export-oriented frigorificos are designed to meet exacting
foreign sanitary regulations. Shipments of chilled and frozen beef by those
enterprises now encompass a wide range of countries. The US and Canada are
the major markets closed to Brazilian fresh beef exporters because hoof-and-
mouth disease has not as yet been eradicated in Brazil. The North American
market does, however, absorb about 54% of the processed beef exports of
Brazil. Beef exports during the 1960 to 1974 period are shown in Table 4,
and their composition by country of destination is shown in Tables 5 to 8.
41.        Export sales in Brazil are handled through direct sales, via a
foreign agent, or via a Brazilian agent. The small exporting slaughter-
houses, which do not have strong international contacts, sell through agents.
There is no direct market promotion program for beef exports. However, a
number of groups operate as trade associations and work with the Government
on beef export policy. One of the trade committees is the Comite Brasileiro
de Exportadores de Carne (CBEC) which the Carteira do Comercio Exterior (CACEX)
convenes periodically to review the beef export situation. That group com-
prises representatives from all levels of the beef industry, but is dominated
by the processors.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 10
42.        Projections.  Frozen and chilled beef will continue to be pre-
dominant in the export trade, and boneless special cuts, frozen and chilled
will show the most growth. That development, dictated by import customer
preference, is logical from the standpoint of economy and efficiency,
eliminating transport and handling of waste and low-value residue. From
the export point of view, a higher value product with greater value added
content would be shipped. This trend would also be in line with Brazil's
policy to expand exports of high-grade beef and to supplement resulting
domestic market shortfalls with lower grade imports from Argentina and
Uruguay.
43.        Cooked frozen beef products, prepared according to individual
importer specifications -- fast frozen and wrapped in cryovac -- have a large
potential in the US market. Brazilian exporters in recent years have demon-
strated ability to deliver on schedule, and that is a prime factor in importers'
preference for Brazil over other countries as a dependable source of supply.
Cooked frozen beef is not a new product for Brazil; small quantities have been
produced since the 1960s and, in recent years, a peak volume of 10,000 tons
was exported.
44.        Most exports of frozen and chilled beef have been destined for
Europe and the closing off of that market resulted in a 66% decline in overall
beef exports in 1974. Import quotas were set in mid-1975 in line with EEC
policy to maintain its supply options abroad, even though stocks of beef are
excessive. Actually, the EEC is 100% self-sufficient since it has the inven-
tories to absorb, plus the current production. The 50,000-ton import quota
for all third countries during second half 1975 is small indeed, and Brazil's
share probably will be symbolic.
45.        The medium and longer term outlook is more favorable.  Heavy
slaughterings in 1974 in a difficult market situation, with costs soaring
arnd prices stable, generated the present inventory situation, because the
market could not absorb the abnormal supply. At the same time, the premature
slaughterings have set the stage for a down cycle in availabilities in 1976/77.
Substantial import demand should result in those years. Over the longer term,
domestic EEC cattle supplies are expected to return to the 92% level of com-
nmunity requirements, leaving ample margin for imports from LDCs.
46.        An additional imponderable is the longer term effect of current
milk price control policies. If a down cycle in dairy supplies should develop
in. the 1977-78 period, a shift in EEC price policies to favor dairy cattle
could result in less than anticipated beef production over the longer term,
thus enhancing export prospects of the LDCs.
47.        While EEC beef policy and US market prospects for processed beef
appear to provide ample scope for future beef exports, the principal con-
straints at this juncture apparently are:
(a) export availabilities from Argentina and Uruguay; and
(b) export availabilities from domestic production.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 11
In the first instance, current beef export policy considerations depend im-
plicitly, it would seem, on imports from Argentina and Uruguay to compensate
expected shortfalls in domestic beef supplies as a result of exports. Re-
liance on the external trade policies of other countries, particularly in
respect of a principal export commodity, would entail risks and uncertainties.
In fact, until 1974, beef import volume has been insignificant.
48.        Export forecasts made for balance of payments projections anticipate
chilled or frozen beef exports of 30,000 tons through 1980. Population growth
and rising incomes undoubtedly will generate greater demand, so that produc-
tion must expand at a rapid rate in order to achieve an exportable surplus.
Projections cited below indicate a more moderate surplus than the export
targets currently being discussed would imply. The range of demand projec-
tions is based on differing rates of income growth, taking the mid-points of
the demand estimates, 56,000 tons of exportable surpluses are indicated in
1975, and 214,000 tons in 1980.

49.        Controls.  Under normal market conditions there must be a restriction
on exports as long as the domestic price for beef is below the world market
price. Moreover, if supply and demand do not quite match at the price which
the Government considers acceptable, some form of price controls will be
needed. Thus, export quotas and price controls, together with a storage
requirement designed to counteract the seasonal fluctuation in supply, are
the main features of the Government's effort to ensure an adequate flow of
beef to the domestic market.
50.        The export quota systems for 1972 and 1973 are indicated in Table
9. For each of the years, the overall quota is divided into an untied quota,
a quota related to storage requirements, and a special quota reserved for new
processors.
51.        Furthermore, each beef processing firm must supply the domestic
market with a proportion of its slaughters which meets or exceeds established
standards (50% in Rio Grande do Sul and 60% in Central Brazil). Between 1972
and 1973, the overall quota was reduced from 180,000 tons to 144,500 tons
(actual exports in 1972 were about 174,000 tons).  The untied quota was
reduced from 55,000 tons to 35,000 tons; the quota related to storage require-
ments was reduced from 113,000 to 69,000 tons; and the quota for new proces-
sors was increased slightly, from 12,000 to 18,000 tons.  Storage requirements
were also increased--in 1972, in Rio Grande do Sul 1 ton of beef had to be
placed in storage for every 5 tons exported while in Central Brazil, it was
1 ton stored to 1.5 tons exported. In 1973 the ratio is 2:5 in Rio Grande
do Sul and 1:1 in Central Brazil.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 12
52.        At the time of the appraisal mission for the first project
(November/December 1972), the Government was planning to raise the overall
quota for 1973 to 203,000 tons, primarily by increasing the untied quota.
A brief interlude of freed consumer prices for beef at the beginning of this
year led to increases of about 40% over the previous agreed prices, however,
and the Government subsequently reduced the quota for 1973.
53.        Trade Barriers.  Under normal circumstances, Brazilian beef enter-
ing the EEC is treated as third country beef, and is therefore subject to a
basic 16% "ad valorem" duty as well as the sliding-scale duty. The US does
not employ tariffs to control meat imports, although countries that may supply
uncooked meats are subject to individual national quotas allocated on the
basis of historical performance. There is no limitation on imports of cooked
beef.
54.        Non-tariff barriers raise more serious obstacles to expansion of
trade. Import quotas, seasonal limitations, licensing, hygiene standards,
and control procedures and tariff classification methods complicate the mar-
keting problems of an international meat exporter, largely because of the
profusion of various national standards and applications of those standards.
However, Brazilian meat exporting companies do not feel that non-tariff
barriers are insurmountable, but rather that the situation has eased in
recent years and that adjustment to non-tariff barriers is a rather mechanical
process, to which they can adjust.
Prices
55.        In 1972 beef prices rose substantially as controls were relaxed.
Therefore, in 1973, the Government introduced a new package of agreed prices 1/
and taxes in response to the experience with freed prices. The price of steers
was set at about Cr$ 4.20/kg 2/ (down from an unregulated price of about
Cr$ 4.67/kg); the agreed wholesale prices for forequaters and hindquarters
were raised from Cr$ 3.30 and Cr$ 4.20 to Cr$ 3.50 and Cr$ 5.00, respectively;
the ICM (value added) tax on cattle and beef products was decreased from about
15% to 5%; and an export tax on beef products of US$200/ton was instituted.
56.        The net effect of all these changes was to lower slightly the
price to producers (the decrease in sales price was largely off-set by the
cut in the ICM), increase the margin to processors on domestic sales, decrease
the margin to processors on export sales, increase the (official) price to
consumers, and, of course, decrease exports between 1972 and 1973. Further-
more, the Government has indicated that the states will receive at least a
part of the proceeds from the export tax as partial compensation for the loss
of revenue from the decrease in the ICM (a state tax). While the export tax
1/    At the present time, prices on cattle and wholesale prices are main-
tained through informal agreements between the Government and proces-
sors. Retailers are generally permitted a margin of 40% to 50%.
2/    Carcass weight.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 13
thus may have been instituted to recover some of the tax revenues lost, the
reduction in exports appears to have been largely a symbolic gesture. Since
exports constitute less than 10% of total production, the reduction should
not have a major influence on the domestic price.
57.        Until domestic and world prices are the same, the Government will
be faced with the necessity of juggling a series of export controls and price
agreements in an attempt to meet domestic demand at the artificially low
price while avoding a negative effect on production. The Government has
assured the Bank that it will permit the domestic price of beef to rise,
although not precipitously, in real terms until it reaches the world market
price. At that level, it is likely that Brazil will be a major exporter,
without any controls.
58.        The rising price levels of the world market, which peaked in the
second quarter of 1974, caused a widening at the differential between the
domestic wholesale price levels which are regulated, and export prices. With
the sharp decline in the world market during 1974 and early 1975, and spread
again narrowed. With world market expected to recover by 1977, present pol-
icies in Brazil tend to improve the quality level of beef production through
strict enforcement of sanitary controls while simultaneously promoting ex-
pansion of other sources of meat, particularly pork and poultry, resulting
in higher production of high quality export cuts of beef and improved,
higher priced domestic products.
59.        In the period to 1980, the Government intends to allow the domestic
price to rise gradually to a level consistent with the corresponding FOB ex-
port price. This goal of allowing price to increase continuously is consistent
with the price pattern of the last several years. Real prices received by
producers in January 1973 were 60% higher than those in January 1970, an
average annual rise of about 17%. The upward trend in world beef prices is
expected to resume in 1976 and continue over the longer term. In order to
close the gap between domestic and FOB export prices for 1980, the domestic
price should rise substantially faster than FOB export price. The extent of
the required real rise would, of course, depend on the behaviour of the in-
ternational price of beef.
Beef Price Outlook to 1985
60.        The world price of beef 1/ is expected to be nearly double the 1974
level by 1980 - rising from US$3,460 per ton to US$6,580 per ton during that
period. At the latter price, beef will cost triple the 1970-72 average. By
1985, world beef prices are expected to be triple the 1974 level. It is
significant that the quotation that may be considered the reference price
for world trade on beef now is in terms of chilled boneless cuts. What is
a clear indication of the current trend in the beef export trade -- away from
bulk frozen bone-in quaters. In Table 10, a series of constant prices is
1/    Based on Argentine chilled rump boneless, wholesale, London.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-1
Page 14
shown for the period to 1985. In real terms the projected prices cited above
will result in increases of 17.5% from the 1974 quotation by 1980, and 30% by
1985. The erosion of values due to anticipated inflation therefore will be
very steep.
61.        An illustrative projection could be made for Brazilian chilled and
frozen beef using as a basis average export prices for beef exports in 1974,
and assuming parallel price trends for all cuts of chilled and frozen beef.
The price differential between the series cited above the data set forth in
Table 4 demonstrate the obvious advantages of shifting the composition of
beef exports to chilled boneless special cuts as quickly as is practicable,
given the marketing situation and supply conditions.
