BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES PROJECT
Pork Processing
A. Production
1.         The swine herd in Brazil was estimated by the Instituto Brasileiro
de Geografia e Estatistica (IBGE) at 41,156,000 head in 1973 (Table 1), with
71% recorded in the project area. This number has continued to grow at a
rate of 7% per year, according to the Instituto Paulista de Economia Agricola
(IPEA), although increasing production costs, particularly those involving
feed, have probably held the rate below what it might have been had costs
been lower. Domestic demand for pork meat has been estimated to increase at
8.2% annually, thus indicating a deficit in supply even under the best con-
ditions, at least until 1985.
2.         Problems of technology also restrain expansion of hog production.
Efforts to improve breeds by moving toward meat production rather than fat
have met success in the more developed states, but work along these lines is
far from being widespread. One factor that has contributed to the emphasis
on the meat producing hog is the increasing use of vegetable oils, particu-
larly soybean oil. A higher slaughter rate is also realized as well as a more
efficient transformation of feed. Much needs to be done, however, to achieve
an efficient rate of production, including an extensive program of rural ex-
tension, improved selection of sires, widespread utilization of vaccines and
other medication, and ample use of balanced feeds.
3.         Some of the larger pork slaughterhouses, such as the Companhia de
Alimentos do Brasil (COIlABRA) in Ponta Grossa, Parana, are attempting to
improve breeds through the establishment of pilot farms, extension services
to their suppliers, and a pricing policy that favors more efficient breeds.
4.         In some states, the production of hogs and derived products is
receiving considerable attention, as in Rio Grande do Sul where a program
for the support of hog-derived industries is being developed by the Banco
Regional de Desenvolvimento do Extremo Sul (BRDE). The program is geared to
assist in the financing of fixed investments and operating capital for the
modernization of the hog industry to improve utilization of the installations,
increase production, and resolve some of the sanitary problems identified by
the Departamento de Inspecao de Productos de Origem Animal (DIPOA). Some of
the investments included in the program are aimed at increasing hog produc-
tion, either directly through an expansion or initiation of swine raising
operations integrated with industrialization, or indirectly through the ex-
pansion and/or construction of feed mills and breeder farms serving the
suppliers of pork industries.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-2
Page 2
5.         The Government of Rio Grande do Sul has taken steps to support the
program through its Secretariat for Agriculture and the Associacao Brasileira
de Criadores Suinos by creating a Sanitary Unit to control helmintosis in
hogs; establishing an intensive program of zootechnical extension with new
inspection centers to provide technical assistance; and creating a Conselho
de Desenvolvimento da Suinocultura (CODESU-RS) to advise the state government
on hog production and pork industrialization matters.
6.         A careful census of swine population conducted by BRDE in 1973 re-
vealed a population of 3,350,919 head in Rio Grande do Sul (Table 2), almost
one half of that shown by IBGE. The lower figure is consistent with a lack
of interest in swine production in recent years, due to instability of prices
offered the producers, the increase in production costs and the unstable
supply of feed. Of the materials used as hog feeds, such as corn, manioc,
and soy meal, corn is the main one and its price is therefore closely related
to the price of hogs in the market.
7.         According to BRDE, statistics on swine population up to 1972 were
inaccurate and the slaughter rates resulting from calculations based on
excessively optimistic population figures were low, reaching 44.9% in 1970
and 44.5% in 1972. With a more accurate population count in 1973, the slaugh-
ter rate rose to 80.5%, but this is exceedingly high and would imply the
extreme reduction of the swine population in a period of less than two years.
The indication is that either the swine population in 1973 was underestimated,
or, possibly, population figures do not take into account the total slaughter
throughout the year. This would bring the swine population figures to
6,047,919 and the slaughter rate down to 44.5%, which is consistent with
previous years even though it is still high.
8.         The State of Santa Catarina, on the other hand, accepts the figures
of IBGE and places its swine herd at 3,323,000 head in 1973 and claims a more
sensible slaughter rate of 32%. By 1974, the herd had increased to 5,'$26,000
head (growth rate, 63.3%) and the slaughter rate was reduced to 19.7%. This
increase in swine production is directly related to significant increases in
the price of pork in the state. The Instituto Brasileiro de Assistencia
Gerencial a Pequena e Media Empresa (IBAGESC) in 1974 determined that the
average price per kg has increased from Cr$ 2.3 in 1970 to Cr$ 4.86 in 1974.
IBAGESC also estimated that 97.5% of the slaughter in the state corresponded
to state-raised hogs, while the remaining 2.5% were imported from Rio Grande
do Sul, which makes the slaughter rate slightly higher in relation to the
state herd.
9.         One of the conclusions reached in a study by the Secretaria de
Desenvolvimento Economico, IBAGESC and BRDE (Table 3) was that the state
herd improved substantially in the 1970-74 period, not only numerically but
in quality, due to improved breeding and management practices. The same
study emphasizes that the advisability of expanding slaughter and processing
capacity of plants is conditional on the feasibility of increasing the supply,
particularly in micro-regions such as Blumenau, where hogs must be imported
from other regions of the state or from neighboring states to maintain a
reasonable slaughter rate.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-2
Page 3
B. The Industry
Structure
10.        In April 1975, DIPOA certified 44 hog slaughtering houses in the
southern and southeastern states; however, this figure could be a bit mis-
leading as some of the larger plants may be included under the category of
frigorificos matadouros, which are slaughterhouses with cold storage or
freezer facilities. Of the 44 plants, 31 are in Rio Grande do Sul, eight
in Santa Catarina, two plants each in Minas Gerais and Parana, and one plant
in Sao Paulo.
11.        Hog slaughtering is a national industry, with most plants privately
owned, though 20% of the units ia Rio Grande do Sul belong to cooperatives.
There are a number of large plants such as SADIA, which has a theoretical
capacity of 600,000 head per year; Companhia de Matadouros do Brasil (COMABRA),
which can kill 800 head per day and is planning to expand its capacity to
1,680; and Swift-Armour in Sao Paulo. Most of the plants in Rio Grande do
Sul and Santa Catarina are small: average annual kill per plant in 1973 was
65,000 in the former and 127,000 in the latter. The trend is toward locating
the slaughterhouses in the hog production areas and to ship the carcasses or
cuts in refrigerated trucks or rail cars to plants in the urban areas for
further processing.
12.        The market for pork products is growing at a rate of about 10% per
year, though one of the drawbacks is the high price of pork, which in Brazil
is more expensive than beef. Cold cuts and various types of sausages are
growing steadily, but this section of the industry is highly fragmented with
many small manufacturers producing for local markets. It is estimated that
the SADIA group controls about 15% of this mar.-:et, with COMABRA and Swift-
Armour producing 10% each, and the rest is processed by smaller companies.
13.        Plant visits were made to Frigobras in Sao Paulo, which is part of
the SADIA group and to COMABRA in Ponta Grossa, Parana. Frigobras is a pro-
cessing plant that converts hogs killed at the SADIA plant in Toledos, Parana,
into hams, sausages, cold cuts and luncheon meats. This plant is only 10
years old, but is already becoming cramped for space due to the continuous
increase in demand for its products. COMABRA is a hog slaughterhouse that
ships carcasses to its Osasco plant for further processing. Both plants,
though old, are well designed and can operate efficiently at their normal
rate of production. The staff is competent and technically qualified, while
operating personnel seem adequately trained. The veterinary and sani ation
inspections are under DIPOA cDntrol.
14.        COMABRA is undertaking a hog improvement program by important stock
from Europe, which it will raise at its own farm for breeding purposes. This
stock will also go to supply other hog producers with high yielding animals,
which they, in turn, can use to imprcu'- their own herds. This is a long-range
program, but COMABRA is helping to mo ernize the industry by undertaking it.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-2
Page 4
_quipment
15.        Brazil can manufacture much of the basic equipment needed for hog
slaughtering; however, specialized machinery must still be imported as the
demand does not warrant local production.
C. Marketing
16.        Consumer preference is heavily slanted toward beef.  In 1974, per
capita consumption of pork was estimated at 6.7 kg, as compared with 23.5 kg
of beef. In Brazil, consumption of pork is low compared to that of beef and
relative to pork consumption levels in other countries. That situation may
change gradually as better quality pork from meat hogs becomes more plentiful,
and more efficient production and larger supplies of pork should be reflected
in a closer beef-pork price relationship. Lower prices of pork relative to
beef undoubtedly would encourage greater consumption. Export trade is sup-
plied by processors that operate modern plants in the main producing states
and that meet international trade sanitary standards.
Domestic Market
17.        A high percentage of pork is consumed in non-fresh form, at least
partly because of marketing conditions, particularly outside the urban cen-
ters where refrigeration and storage facilities are inadequate for fresh pork.
Ham, salt pork, and bacon account for 36.2% of pork marketing; lard, 16%; and
sub-products (sausage and such) and all other items, 30%. That leaves about
17.8% of the carcass for fresh meat.
18.        Changing tastes, new merchandising systems, and rising incomes will
influence the product mix in the future. Salt pork and lard will decline in
importance, while fresh meat and sub-products will grow faster than other
items. The latter category will benefit from improved presentation, pack-
aging and product innovation. The table below lists the typical product mix
of a larger modern pork processor.
19.        With the shifting of emphasis to meat-type hogs, the outlook for
the industry is excellent. In the domestic market, socio-economic factors
favoring increased consumption of various commodities als(- will sustain rising
demand for pork. The export market for special pork cuts is good and, in fact,
a modest trade already is being conducted by the modern pork processing en-
terprises. COMABRA, a borrower under the first agro-industry loan, is a
leading force in this developing export trade.
20.        Commercial pork production, processing and marketing are concen-
trated in the south of Brazil. Parana is becoming the leader in this sector
where, historically, Rio Grande do Sul was most important. Following those
two states are Santa Catarina and Sao Paulo, a distant fourth. Pork and pork
products are supplied to the principal consuming centers from the aforementioned

states. In Minas Gerais, pork processors are active and supply local markets,
basing their operations on hogs brought from Parana. Per capita consumption,
in the U.S. and Canada and in Europe are compared below with the Brazilian
data:

Export >arket
21.        World trade in pork products approximates 1.5 million tons.  It is
in this context that Brazilian processors view their market prospects. Be-
cause of the limitations of the industry, Brazil has not exported large quan-
tities of pork, but expansion of such trade will follow installation of addi-
tional modern processing facilities that meet importers' sanitary standards.
Export capability already is being demonstrated by the few shippers. Because
of the aftosa problem, markets for fresh pork in the U.S., Japan, United
Kingdom and Sweden are closed to Brazilian exporters. The principal markets
for frozen pork exports have been the Netherlands, Spain and Italy. Smaller
quantities are also sent to other European countries (Table 4).
22.        Export volume has not been dynamic and, in fact, 1974 shipments of
frozen pork totaled 1,623 tons, as compared with 2,129 tons in 1970. The
lower figure reflects mainly deteriorating economic conditions in the major
market areas. However, export unit values were up sharply in 1974 to US$1,644
per ton, as compared with US$636 per ton in 1970.

BRAZIL
AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT II
Poultry Proces sij
A. Production
1.        A study published in 1974 by the Associacao Paulista de Avicultura
(APA) showed that per capita consumption of meat in Brazil was only 29 per
year, of which 60% (17.4 kg) was beef; 30% (8.7 kg) was pork, and 10% was
poultry (2.2 kg) and fish (0.7 kg). Alternative meats, such as lamb, mutton,
goat and rabbit, are either too localized or exotic to be of major signifi-
cance. The same study indicated that in large urban centers, such as Sao
Paulo and Rio de Janeiro, 50% of the families consume chicken once a week,
25% consume it every two weeks, 13% once a month. and 12% only sporadically.
2.        The development of more modern industrial processing methods for
chicken and efforts to bring beef prices closer to the international market
price have probably helped increase poultry consumption, particularly because
of competitive prices of chicken.
3.        The expansion of chicken production has been particularly notable
in the Sao Paulo area where population increases and changing food habits
have stimulated the market. Calculations made by the APA show a production
of 97 million chicken in 1971 for the State of Sao Paulo, with a 17.5%
slaughter rate. In 1974, production had increased to 160 million and the
slaughter rate to 61.8%. Wqhile these figures do not represent the average
for the country, it is clear that the amount of poultry in the diet is more
significant in the project area, particularly in the South and Southeast
regions, than elsewhere in Brazil.  In 1973 the total number of birds in the
project areas was 271,676,000 (Table 1).
4.        The production of fowl, of which poultry constitutes about 95%,
grew by 66%0 in tile period 1960-70, while chick.en meat production is estimated
to have increased by 1,258% in the same period. These large increases, which
took place mainly in the larger producing centers of Sao Paulo and Minas
Gerais, were due primarily to substitution of the traditional double-purpose
breeds (New Hampshire, Plymouth Rock, Rhode Island Red and Sussex) with im-
ported specialized breeds for meat (White Cornish, White Plymouth Rock) or
for eggs (cross--breeds from 'White Leghorn), as well as improved technology
for breeding and feeding.
5.        In the Southeast, and mainly in Sao Paulo, specialized units sell
one-day--old chickens to the raising farms to use as stock for other areas of
the country. One limiting factor to a continued expansion of production is
the high cost of the operation, particularly for commercial feed, due to in-
creases in the price of its components, corn, soybean, fish meal and others.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-3
Page 2
6.        According to the Instituto Brasileiro de Geografia e Estatistica
(IBGE), in 1972 chicken farms with more than 50 hens represented only 15' of
the total number of farms raising chickens in the project area. IThile the
number may not have increased significantly since, such farms contribute a
much larger share of the total production than they did in 1972, particularly
in the case of large cooperatives such as COTIA, which supply the chicken
processing market by combining the production of all its members.
7.        The Instituto de Planejamento Economico e Social (IPEA) estimates
an increase in the demand for chicken at a rate of 9.8% annually, based on
the hypothesis of a 10% annual growth of GDP. High production costs, however,
tend to limit demand because of the relatively lower prices of beef in the
market. This situation may change, of course, as the price of beef moves up
toward the levels of the international market, which would provide an added
incentive to poultry production.
8.        The State of Santa Catarina, with a poultry population of 15,184,000
in 1974 increased its slaughter from 3.5 million in 1970 to 27 million in
1974, with an average rate of increase of 65% for the four-year period. While
the poultry population of the state is smaller than that of the neighboring
states, the slaughter rate is high due to the use of modern techniques which
allow a chicken to be ready for slaughter in 56 days. Processing plants,
such as SADIA and PERDIGON, promote production by supplying one-day-old chicks,
feed and technical assistance to the producers to insure sufficient supplies
and maintain a high quality standard.
B.  The Industry
Structure
9.        Poultry processing is rapidly becoming a major industry in the
project area. Some 80% of Brazil's total poultry population is grown in
this area, but only about 24% of the birds are slaughtered in Departamento
de Inspecao de Produtos de Origem Animal (DIPOA)-approved facilities. Sao
Paulo has the largest number of approved poultry slaughterhouses with 57%,
followed by Parana and Rio de Janeiro with 10% each, Minas Gerais and Rio
Grande do Sul with 9% each, Santa Catarina with 3%, and Espirito Santo with
2%. Santa Catarina with only 3% of the approved slaughterhouses kills 28% of
the birds in Brazil, but this can be attributed mainly to the SADIA operation,
which is one of the largest plants in the country.
10.       Poultry slaughtering is a national industry that is changing rapidly
in composition due to the expansion that is taking place. In 1973 there were
five large processors, 75 medium-sized plants and hundreds of small pro-
cessors. The latter are being replaced by DIPOA-approved plants with capa-
cities of 1,000 to 2,000 birds per hour. A few larger plants in the 3,000
bird-per--tour range are planined.  Plants have an annual capacity of 1,800,000
to 5,9J0,000 birds and are modern well-designed facilities with refrigeration



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-3
Page 3
for freezing and storing packaged poultry. A few of the larger units also
prepare some of the by-products on their premises rather than sell such items
to outsiders.
11.        Many of the plants are owned by corporations formed by individual
poultry raisers, a number are owned by poultry cooperatives, while others
belong to companies specializing in slaughtering and distribution. Most of
the plants are close to urban areas to facilitate distribution and marketing.
Since poultry raising, especially for slaughterhouses, has increased the
demand for feed mills to supply balanced rations to the industry, both indus-
tries are growing in parallel.
Equipment
12.        Brazil can supply most of the equipment required for poultry
slaughterhouses, though all of the stainless steel for fabricated items must
be imported. Local companies such as Greco Mecanics Mader and Stork-Brabant
can do all the necessary engineering for the plants in addition to furnishing
turnkey operations if this is requested by the individual companies. Refrig-
eration machinery is available in Brazil though some of the higher speed
sophisticated machinery must be imported.
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
13.        The dynamic expansion of the broiler industry in Brazil is a phenom-
enon of recent years, but there is no indication that the industry has reached
maturity. The low level of per capita consumption of poultry meat in rela-
tion to beef consumption and in comparison with poultry meat consumption in
other developing and developed countries shows that the broiler industry in
Brazil can be considered to be in an early stage of development. Recent
growth is described below in terms of production data, but these data in fact
represent demand in the period under review, because output generally repre-
sents availabilities over the year that are absorbed by the market, except
for temporary gluts in supply that reflect marketing and distribution problems
rather than basic disequilibrium between supply and demand.
14.        Broiler output rose more than two and a half times in the 1969-73
period, going from 112,000 tons to 293,000 tons. Further expansion occurred
in the 1974/75 period and additional processing capacity is planned in the
oncoming years to accommodate increasing production. In the State of Sao
Paulo, where the principal broiler production centers are located, productive
capacity totals 20.5 million birds. On an average of five production cycles
per year for the broiler producers, potential output in Sao Paulo alone could
reach 102 million broilers per year. The pace of growth of the broiler in-
dustry is shown below.

16.        It is apparent that the increasing volume of poultry meat consump-
tion is almost entirely a result of new broiler availabilities in recent
years. Per capita consumption of poultry meat in Brazil is estimated at
3.1 kg less than any major Western Hemisphere nation. The potential for the
broiler industry is clearly shown by the comparative 1973 per capita consump-

17.        Assuming that the average broiler dresses out at about 1.4 kg, the
estimated production of 102 million broilers in the State of Sao Paulo would
sustain a per capita consumption of 1.4 kg. Since Sao Paulo produces about
half the broilers in the country, another 1.4 kg per capita comes from all
the other producing states. The latter production would include all stages
of industrialization, a sizeable portion of which has not yet come under the
DIPOA inspection system. Be that as it may -- about 0.3 kg per capita is
then made up of mature poultry and all other types of fowl.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-3
Page 5
18.       There are many factors impinging on broiler consumption, mainly of
a sociopsychological nature rather than socio-economic, that render difficult
the projection of poultry meat consumption in the years ahead. The principal
factor inhibiting increased sales of broilers is the antipathy toward the
frozen product. Consumers and tradesmen must be educated in the handling of
frozen meat. This is being partially dealt with in the distant consumption
centers in the warmer areas of Brazil where some merchants are offering frozen
broilers as the fresh product to unknowing customers. Promotion of frozen
broilers under a well-known quality label would go far toward dispelling the
reluctance consumers feel and would broaden the market rapidly. Such accep-
tance is a necessary condition to expanding the market in tropical climate
areas, because the efficiency and economy that can be realized in distribution
will permit prices to be held down.
Export Market
19.       Most of the European trade in poultry meat is within the Continent,
the exception being the U.S., a major supplier. The U.S. is also a principal
source for the market in Japan, which country also imports a large volume
from China, East Europe, Canada and Denmark and the east European countries.
With the devaluation of the dollar, the U.S. broiler industry has become more
competitive in world markets although a series of trade disputes have occurred
in recent years between the U.S. and the EEC because of tariff barriers and
restrictions imposed by the latter countries on third country poultry.
20.       Brazil has not entered the export trade in broilers because the
industry is in its initial phase of spontaneous growth and is far from sup--
porting the level of per capita consumption that could be realized in its
present stage of development, given the resources that are available, because
the production of broilers has out-paced the marketing and distribution sys-
tem of the industry.
21.       The first occasion on which export policy was ever brought forward
regarding broilers was in the spring of 1975 when poultry producer organiza-
tions met with the MIinistry of Agriculture to discuss their demaands for export
subsidies to ship abroad the excessive stocks of broilers that were glutting
the market. The Government agreed to give every consideration to the problem.
22.       COTTA apparently has made studies on the possibilities of exporting
to the European markets and reached the conclusion tnat it was not practic-
able because of the subsidies received by poultry producers in the EEC. As a
matter of fact, subsidies are a constant subject of controversy among the
EEC member countries because of possible intra--EEC dumping of poultry and
eggs by producers with the highest subsidies. In early 1975. an US$8 million
subsidy was announced by the French Government for (a) supporting minimum
egg prices; (b) paying hen slaughter subsidies and (c) maintaining cold
storage stocks of broilers held off the market. Although the purpose of the
subsidies is to bolster prices, those payments might in effect enable recip-
ients to ship poultry to other member countries at lower prices. freeing the
national economy of sorie burdensome surplus commodity inventories.



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-3
Page 6
23.        It is possible that earlier Brazilian efforts to study export mar-
kets were perhaps a bit premature in arriving at negative conclusions. An
important aspect of trade in poultry products is that a substantial propor-
tion of imports into European markets is now composed of special cuts, such
as legs, breast filets, boneless meat (rolls and such) and canned specialties.
These products are priced much higher than whole broilers and apparently
yield margins sufficient to enable exporters to overcome tariff barriers.
Some 80% or more of U.S. poultry meat exports now consist of chicken parts
and products. The emerging pattern and practices parallel those in the beef
trade. Special cuts are especially packaged, prepared, and shipped according
to specific customer requirements.
24.        Average unit values reflect the value added of preparation and of
elimination of waste or less desirable parts. On the cost side, more effi-
cient handling and substantial weight reduction are important benefits. In
the U.S., the problem created by large residual volume of less attractive
parts has been overcome by special packaging and special promotions in the
domestic market.
25.        Negligible quantities of poultry offal exports have been recorded
in recent years. There apparently is good demand for poultry liver, but the
international trade in poultry offal in general does not hold much promise.
Export data for poultry offal are shown below:
(Tons)     US$O000    US$/ton
France and Italy       14           6.8        486
United Kingdom          4           1.5        375
26.        Product Quality.  The basic conditions for export, uniform quality,
Federal inspection of production, modern, sanitary conditions of processing,
and steady dependable supply, are being established as the structure of the
industry changes. Those changes are briefly described below as they relate
to the export trade.
27.        In the State of Sao Paulo, there were 428 poultry plants in opera-
tion in early 1975. The DIPOA program now being implemented in that state
aims to bring the number of poultry plants down by eliminating small uneconomic
units that cannot be renovated and modernized to meet DIPOA standards. More-
over, the inspection task would be impossible to manage. Efficient marketing
and cold storage of the output would neither be feasible nor economic with a
fractional and dispersed processing industry. Presently, there are about 10
plants in Sao Paulo that meet the criteria for the formation of an efficient,
well-organized poultry industry. In that state alone, therefore, 25 new
plants must be constructed in the next five years in order to (a) replace the
plants being closed, (b) supply an expanded home market, and (c) support
possible development of export trade. Each new plant with the dimensions of,
say, the COTIA plant described below -- with capacity of 50,000 birds per day,



ANNEX 4
Appendix 4-3
Page 7
requiring fixed investment of US$300 per 1,000-bird throughput -- would require
US$15 million of capital funds.
28.       The COTIA plant in Jaguare is a typical modern poultry plant from
which broilers are shipped to all major consumption centers by refrigerated
truck. A portion of its output could be shifted to the export trade with
minor modifications to accommodate new product lines. Present output is
35,000 birds per day -- to be expanded to 50,000 in 1976. Annual rates
would be 9.5 million to 13.5 million broilers per year, which, at an average
dressed-out weight per bird of 1.4 kg, would amount to 13,500 to 18,900 tons
per year.
29.       At the COTIA plant, condemnations were said to average 2% of the
through-put. That compares favorably with the U.S. broiler industry where
condemnations in 1974 amounted to 1.96% and 2.54% of total output in 1973.
That record speaks well for the quality of production in Sao Paulo farms.
30.       Trade Barriers.  The EEC Commission is frequently adjusting tariffs
on third country imports as internal supply conditions change. Rates vary
widely according to type of product and origin. Rates for U.S. products are
high in comparison with imports from some other origins because the U.S. is
a major supplier. The actual incidence on the trade may vary, however, de-
pending on unit import values. The most recent EEC supplementary levies on
poultry imports since February 1975 are the following:

Prices
31.       Broiler prices follow a well-defined seasonal pattern, with minor
variations from year to year. The trough of the annual cycle usually occurs
in February-March and, rarely, in April. The peak quotations usually are
recorded in August-September. Typical highs and lows in wholesale broiler
prices in 1974 are shown below.

32.       In May 1975, wholesale broiler prices were down to Cr$7.00 for
fresh broilers and CR$6.00 for frozen broilers. Those prices compare with
U.S. prices of Cr$ 6.74 in 1974 and Cr$7.76 in 1973 at the wholesale level.
In the U.S., the feed price/cost ratio was so unfavorable in 1973/74 that a
drastic reduction of production occurred, resulting in an uptrend in price in
1975. However, with lower feed prices in prospect, 1976 broiler supplies
should be more abundant and prices should decline. In Brazil, broiler prices
declined in 1975 due to the surfeit of beef supplies as export trade was
curtailed. Prices are expected to recover slightly during the low season of
the annual beef cycle in the latter part of the year.

BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Grain Processing
1.        Agro-industries Credit Project I emphasized processing oilseed,
particularly soybean, for oil and its by-products for the export market.
More recent Government restrictions on the export of oilseed meal and the
expansion of beef, pork and poultry industries have added a new dimension
to this sub-sector, which is closely associated with the balanced concentrate
feed industry of which the producers of meat, particularly pork (Appendix 2-2)
and poultry (Appendix 2-3), largely depend.
2.        Although soybean continues to dominate oilseed production in Brazil,
other sources of oil, meal and cake also represent a significant part of the
domestic market and retain a minor part of the export market, the main ones
being peanuts and cottonseed.
3.        Castor beans are beginning to gain in importance, and export possi-
bilities for castor oil seem to be positive. There have been some recent
indications of export possibilities also for avocado oil to be used in the
cosmetics industry, mainly in Europe. The use of avocado pulp resulting from
the oil-extracting process in the feed industry adds interest to this product,
particularly in states with large non-arable portions such as Espirito Santo,
Santa Catarina, and Minas Gerais.
4.        Peanuts, while diminishing in planted area as a result of the expan-
sion of soybean cultivation, and also in export volume, have increased in price
in recent years due to lack of offer in the international market. Peanut
production in 1973 reached 578,000 tons in the project area, representing 98%
of the total production in Brazil.
5.        Estimates by the Instituto Nacional de Desenvolvimento Industrial
(INDI) forecast production of 157,000 tons of oil in 1975 and 188,000 tons
in 1980. Production of peanut cake for the same years is estimated at 208,000
tons and 252,000 tons, respectively. Exports of peanut oil are expected to
reach 100,000 tons by 1980.
6.        Cotton production in 1973 reached 1,372 tons in the project area,
representing 80.3% of the total production in the country. The Instituto
Nlacional do Desenvolvimento Industrial (INDI) estimates place the production
of cottonseed oil at 166,000 tons in 1975 and 177,000 tons in 1980. Produc-
tion of cottonseed cake is estimated at 498,000 tons in 1975 and 531,000 tons
in 1980.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
7.        The large 1973 and 1974 castor bean crops, estimated at 420,000
tons and 540,000 tons, respectively, are expected to be reduced in 1975 as
a result of lower producer prices. Steps are being taken, however, to slow
down the decline in prices, and the Government, under a purchase-stock financ-
ing arrangement with producers, withheld an estimated 40,000 tons of castor
oil from the market in 1974 to be released in late 1975. Forecasts for 1975
call for about a 375,000-ton production, and export shipments of castor oil
are estimated at 175,000 tons. These figures are consistent with estimates
by the Plano Director Portuario Nacional, based on observed trends, which
place exports of industrial oils, particularly castor oil, at 160,000 tons
in 1975 and 214,000 tons in 1980. These figures do not include soybean or
peanut oil.
8.        A study of exports in 1973 and the first six months of 1974 con-
ducted by Banco do Brasil indicates a considerable increase in castor and
other vegetable oils and decreases in peanut and soybean oil exports as a
result of increased domestic demand and official restrictions:

9.        To facilitate treatment of both aspects of the sub-sector, that of
oilseed processing and feed processing, this annex has been divided into two
appendices, one dealing with the oilseed processing industry in general and
with soybean processing in particular (Appendix 3-1) and one covering the
production of balanced and concentrated animal feed (Appendix 3-2).

BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Soybean Processing
A. Production
1.        Soybean has continued to dominate oilseed production in Brazil, the
1973 crop reaching more than 5 million tons in 1973 (Table 1) with average
yields of 1,386 kg/ha. The Instituto de Planejamento Economico e Social (IPEA)
predicts soybean yields of up to 1,500 kg/ha in 1980, provided seed is carefully
selected and improved agricultural practices are employed. Recent policies of
subsidized credit for fertilizers and liming, as well as for special programs
to incorporate vast areas of lands suitable for soybean cultivation with
improved agricultural practices, may have a significant impact on future
soybean production.
2.        Exports of soybean products have been increasing in spite of lower-
ing prices in the last few years. Soybean oil has been kept for the domestic
market, but grain exports reached 1,786,000 tons; meal, 1,581,000 tons, and oil,
30,500 tons in 1973, the last year in which significant exports of oil took
place. Studies made by Escritorio Tecnico de Planejamento (PLANAVE), based on
the assumption that soybean oil exports will continue as a consequence of in-
creased production and processing, indicate a production of 11 million tons by
1980 and 15.6 million tons by 1985, with corresponding consumption projections
of 6.3 million tons and 8.9 millions tons, respectively. These figures indi-
cate exportable surpluses of 4.5 million tons in 1980 and 6.7 million in 1985
(Table 2). These totals, however, will be affected by increasing consumption
in the States of Goias and Mato Grosso, for which no estimates have been made.
3.        Expansion of the feed industry as a result of improved poultry and
hog production and processing constitutes an important component in the
domestic market for soybean and soybean products. Other potential uses of
soybean oil which have not as yet been developed in Brazil also contribute
to an optimistic forecast of soybean marketing and, consequently, of sustained
incentive for increased production.
4.        A study of the evolution of planted area in soybean carried out by
the Instituto Brasileiro de Geografia e Estatistica (IBGE) in 1974 showed
that Rio Grande do Sul's contribution to the global planted area decreased
from 90.5% in 1955 to 53.9% in 1974. In compensation, however, states which
were producing no soybean in 1955, such as Parana, Minas Gerais, Goias and
2Iato Grosso, contributed 26.1%, 0.9%, 2.1% and 3.4%, respectively, to the
total planted area in the country in 1974.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 2
B. The Industry
Structure
5.        The oilseed processing industry in Brazil is composed of a few
very large companies that dominate the market and a large number of medium-
and small-sized industries that generally produce crude oil for export or
sell their product locally for further refining. Seven large companies
manufacture 60% of the vegetable oil in Brazil and, of these, three are
international companies and four are Brazilian. All of the companies have
multi-plant operations, which allow them to produce a variety of oils, depend-
ing on the production of raw material in the different locations. They manu-
facture a complete range of products such as refined oil, shortenings, crude
oil and cake or meal for both domestic and export consumption. The medium-
and small-sized plants are owned by individuals, cooperatives or companies
and range in size from cottage industries to well equipped modern small- or
medium-scale plants.
6.        In 1969, there were 410 companies registered as vegetable oil
producers, with 37% located in the States of Sao Paulo, Parana, Santa Catarina
and Rio Grande do Sul. Minas Gerais had 3Z aud the balance was divided among
the other states. Many of the registered plants are specialty oil producers
located in the northeast section of the country to utilize local raw material.
7.        The capacity of the individual plants varies from 30 tons per day
to 800 to 1,000 tons per 24-hour day. The larger size plants are located in
the southeast where the major oilseed now being industrialized is soy, which
in 1974 represented 63% of Brazil's total oil production.
8.        One of the major problems in the past has been under utilization
of the production facilities, especially in the smaller scale industries using
mechanical methods of extraction. A study was made in Rio Grande do Sul
covering a period from 1969 to 1971 and during these three years, the small
plants operated at 58.4% of their capacity, while the medium size and larger
factories had 78.7% and 81.5%, respectively. In the northeast, the situation
was much more acute for in 1969 they operated at only 34% of their capacity.
9.        Part of the problem can be attributed to sizing the mechanical
extraction equipment to meet peak seasonal demands in the specialty product
fields, which tends to misrepresent the actual annual production statistics
since the plants only operate during the harvesting period. In the south,
a lack of capital to purchase and store soybeans for off-season production
causes a similar under utilization, which could be corrected through proper
financing.
Plant Operations
10.       Most modern plants in Brazil receive soybeans in bulk trucks.  The
trucks are weighed upon arrival at the plant, after which the beans are dis-
charged onto conveyors for preliminary cleaning and drying to bring moisture



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 3
down to about 13% before storing in silos. When required for production, the
beans are removed from storage, cleaned again to eliminate any residual waste
material, weighed, and sent through a cracking mill and a dehuller, to remove
seed covers. The material then goes through a conditioner to bring the moisture
level down to about 10% to 11% and a flaking mill that flattens it prior to
extraction operations.
11.       Four methods of extraction are used in Brazil -- continuous solvent;
mechanical and solvent extraction in a continuous process; a mechanical and
soLvent extraction by batch processing; and mechanical extraction. Most of
the large modern plants use the continuous solvent extraction method, which
has a high original equipment cost and requires a higher degree of technology,
but produces a 3.5% to 4% higher oil yield over the mechanical process. The
mixed mechanical-solvent process is used by the older large and medium-sized
mills and by a few modern smaller mills. Because of a lack of investment
capital, most of the small mills use the less efficient mechanical extraction
process.
12.       After extraction that separates the oil from the cake, mills using
the solvent method send the cake to a desolventizer. The solvent thus removed
is then condensed, washed, dehumidified, filtered, and reused. The cake from
both the solvent and mechanical process is then dried, cooked, ground and,
in some instances, pelletized for the domestic and export markets, where it
is bought for animal feed.
13.       Cake for human consumption goes through a desolventizing and
deodorizing process. Then the non-protein constituents are removed select-
ively, leaving a solid residue. This residue is classified into three groups
according to protein content: flour and grits, having a 40% to 50% protein
content; concentrates containing 70% protein; and isolated, with from 90%
to 95% protein.
14.       Obtaining experienced management in soybean milling and oil extrac-
tion is less of a problem than it is in some of the other industries, for
the larger oil plants have trained many excellent technicians who would be
willing to change positions provided that they were given sufficient incen-
tives in the smaller and medium-sized mills.
15.       The number of personnel required varies with the amount of mechani-
zation in the mill and the type of finished products that are manufactured.
Modern mills can be highly automated, from raw material reception through
grinding the cake for animal feed; thus, manual labor would be required only
for discharging the beans from the receiving trucks and in the packaging and
warehousing of cake or meal. The balance of the operations would be handled
by technical personnel or supervisors checking the movement of the product
during the process. Less automated mills, including medium- and small-sized
units, require a greater amount of labor, ranging from 1.5 to three people
per ton of raw material, depending on the handling and processing methods used.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 4
Equipment
16.       Equipment for oil mills can be grouped into four major types -- pre-
paration, extraction, refining, and packaging. Preparation equipment consists
of items such as conveyors, scales, bean cleaning, drying-cracking or crushing,
drying and flaking. Extraction machinery can either be mechanical or solvent,
with the latter requiring additional equipment to recoup the solvent remain-
ing in the cake. In the refining process, the crude oil, depending on its
ultimate use, is normally cleaned, either in a centrifuge or filter press,
neutralized, clarified, and deodorized. Packaging machinery selection depends
on the finished product; thus, meal is usually ground or made into pellets,
liquid oils are packed into cans or bottles, while margarines can be packed
into plastic, cartons or cans.
17.       Brazil can supply much of the slower speed preparation machinery
but imported equipment might be advisable for the higher output per hour
production. Mechanical extraction machinery is manufactured locally, and
solvent extraction lines are imported. Most of the refining equipment can
be obtained in Brazil, though here too, higher capacity, sophisticated equip-
ment for the larger plants would probably be imported. Local packaging is
available for most applications, but high-speed lines, if required, would
normally be purchased abroad.
18.       Many of the Brazilian suppliers are either affiliates of foreign
companies or work under some form of licensing arrangement; consequently,
their equipment is comparable to that found in the more advanced countries.
Specialized items with limited demand in Brazil would have to be imported.
C. Marketing
19.       The three oilseeds which would be processed under the project would
be soybean, cottonseed, and peanut, the principal products of which would be,
namely, cake or meal and vegetable oil. Cake and meal are utilized mostly
as feed, while vegetable oil is used for human consumption.
20.       Among the three principal oilseeds, soybeans are preferred because
of the high yield of meal as compared with other oilseeds. The relative
yields are summarized below:
Soybeans      Peanuts        Cottonseed
_______________ %-------------------
Meal                   79            56               69
Oil                    19            41               28
Protein content        45            52               42



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 5
21.       The high meal yield of soybeans is combined with high protein con-
tent. Low oil yield is also an attractive charactieristic of soybeans because,
during periods of heavy demand for oilseed meals, marketing of oils becomes
a problem. There are competing oils in the world market which do not yield
meal counterparts desirable for livestock feeds because of low protein content
or high fiber content, e.g., palm, coconut, or because of toxic properties,
e.g., rapeseed. Therefore, there is relatively more scope for expansion of
markets for the desirable meals.
22.       Soybean meal accounts for 61% of world production of oilseed meals,
while soybean oil comprises 20% of world output. The likelihood is that the
importance of soybeans will increase, reflecting the massive output gain fore-
seen in Brazil and experimental work on soybean production in other regions
of the world.
Domestic Market
23.       There are two main sectors that consume soybeans -- the balanced
feed industry that utilizes meal and the edible oils industry that utilizes
the oil. In the first instance, soybeans now are the lowest cost form of
high protein input. Fishmeal is a direct competitor in other countries
because of its high protein content -- 65% by volume as compared with 45%
for soybean meal. However, since Brazil does not produce large volumes of
fishmeal, soybeans are the principal domestic source of the high protein
component of animal feeds.
24.       The doubling of output of the balanced feed industry since 1970
reflects the demand for feeds generated largely by the dynamic growth of
broiler and egg production. Trend toward production of specially bred meat
hogs with improved techniques in swine husbandry also has supported the
rapid growth of the balanced feed industry.
25.       Due to its growth in recent years, soybean oil has preempted the
leading position in the edible fat and oils market, being the lowest priced
source of oil for food preparation. Its closest competitors are peanut oil
and cottonseed oil in Brazil; as in most other countries, peanut oil is a
preferred oil but is higher priced and its output has been static in recent
years. Cottonseed oil, a by-product of cotton gins, is limited by cotton
production and its supply varies with the cotton crop. In hydrogenated form,
soybean and cottonseed oils are the basic materials for margarine production
where they compete on a price basis.
26.       The domestic feed industry doubled its output from 2.5 million to
5.2 million tons in the 1970-72 period. With about 35% of the average feed
mills' requirements consisting of soybean meal, about 875,000 tons to 1.8
million tons would have been consumed (Appendix 5-2). The bean equivalent
of those volumes would be 1.1 million to 2.3 million tons. Soybean oil avail-
abilities based on that volume of meal output would amount to 209,000 tons in
1970 and 437,000 tons in 1974. In 1970, only 2,650 tons of exports were
recorded and, in 1974, 2,300 tons were shipped abroad. Therefore, 206,250 tons



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 6
in 1970 and 434,700 tons in 1974 would have been available for domestic dis-
tribution, or about 2.2 kg and 4.2 kg per capita in the respective years.
Soybean oil availabilities are likely to be absorbed by the domestic market
for several years, until new processing capacity goes on stream, and substan-
tial increments to soybean oil supply result.
27.       The balanced feed industry is reviewed in Appendix 5-2 and projec-
tions for feed consumption requirements to 1984 are shown.  Assuming an overall
industry average of 35% by volume as an input and that practically all the
peanut and cottonseed cake are exported, soybean requirements in Brazil may
be summarized as follows:

29.       Since soybean oil is a by-product of meal processing, its output can
be determined by the extraction rate prevailing in Brazil -- which currently
is 19% by weight. The estimated availabilities of soybean oil in the years
ahead resulting from soybean meal processing for the balanced feed industry
are summarized below; it should be noted that the quantities cited are only
partial availabilities, because there will be additional oil supply from
processing of meal for export.

30.       The indicated soybean oil availabilities in 1980, based on projected
national feedstuff requirements of 1.1 million tons, exceeds forecast total
vegetable oil consumption by 300,000 to 500,000 tons. Some of that excess
will undoubtedly be absorbed by the domestic market as new product development
opens new fields of utilization. However, it appears that export markets
wilL need to be found, either for surplus soybean oil or for the peanut and
cottonseed oil that soybean oil would displace in the home market.
31.       One of the sub-projects proposed under Agro-industries Credit Project
I calls for the establishment in Sao Paulo of a large enterprise for the manu-
facture of Texturized Vegetable Protein (TVP). Extensive research has already
been conducted at ITAL in Campinas, based on earlier research and development
work in the U.S. It is therefore of interest to gain a perspective on this
relatively new Brazilian product in older markets, since present processes
for TVP are based on soybeans as the raw material. Its ramification in the
soybean and livestock industries will be important.
32.       In the U.S., renewed R & D emphasis is being placed on TVP because
of the high prices of meat. According to USDA, 25,000 tons of this product
were produced in 1972 and sold mainly to the meat processing industry. Soya
could thus present considerable competition for beef. In the U.S., it is
being used in sausage manufacture at a 25:75 ratio with beef.
33.       The USDA estimates that in 1973, 36,000 tons of "soya meat" were
sold and by 1980, this figure could be in excess of 800,000 tons. If this
forecast proves correct, vegetable protein will, by the end of this decade,



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 8
represent the equivalent of around 90% of U.S. beef and veal imports in 1972.
That, of course, implies substantial import substitution, but beef import
volume will still be large because overall demand will have expanded consider-
ably by that date.
34.       Meat industry forecasts are that 10% to 21% of the meat of ready-
to-eat meat dishes, e.g., frozen TV dinners, will be replaced by TVP by 1980.
Even if the lower limit of that forecast materializes, the U.S. meat industry
would require 2 million fewer head of cattle, 4 million less hogs, and 360,000
less sheep than otherwise would be the case. That trend should not impinge
on prospects for cooked frozen beef exports from Brazil. The opportunity for
promotion of all "all beef" products should enhance the marketing outlook for
Brazilian exporters.
35.       Predictions for the European market in 1985 are that 2.7 million
tons of meat will be replaced by TVP. That quantity is about equal to the
aggregate beef consumption forecast in Brazil for 1980. That volume also
exceeds total world trade in fresh, chilled or frozen beef, and veal in 1972.
36.       TVP is an ideal, low-cost source of protein for the lower income
classes. Moreover, cheaper protein sources will become more important in
Brazil as compared with the North American and European markets. Much will
depend on the success of product promotion of the initial enterprises start-
ing up production in Brazil in the next few years.
Export Market
37.       Exports of soybeans had increased from 290,000 tons in 1970 to an
estimated 2.8 million tons in 1974, approximately a ten-fold gain in four
years. In that short time, export revenues from soybeans rose from US$27
million to US$585 million, or 21 times. The trend in world prices enabled
exporters to obtain higher unit values, which rose from US$93 per ton to
US$215 per ton, a gain of 131% (Table 3).
38.       Exports of soybean meal, which already had reached a level of
489,000 tons in 1970, grew only about four-fold to 2 million tons in 1974.
Export value rose from US$41 million to US$406 million, nearly 10 times
greater, in the latter year. Unit prices of meal were up 70% during this
period, from US$89 to US$149 per ton (Table 5).
39.       Soybean oil exports have followed a rather erratic pattern, going
from 2,650 tons to 61,000 tons in 1973 and down to 2,300 tons in 1974 due to
restriction of exports imposed in late 1973 to ensure adequate supplies of
edible oils in the home market. That restriction was removed in late 1974 so
that oil exports may again continue an upward trend (Table 4).
40.       Exports of soybeans in 1975 are estimated in a range up to 4.2
million tons. Meal exports are as yet an unknown quantity, but should at
least equal the 1974 volume. The faster pace in expansion of soybean exports
reflects the inability of the processing industry in building new extraction



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 9
and refining facilities to keep abreast of the explosive growth in production.
Therefore, a larger proportion is being exported as beans. Greater interval
demand from the feed industry is also taxing the processing capacity of the
oilseed industry. However, sub-projects in the pipeline should add greatly
to the supply of meal for domestic and export requirements.
41.       The bulk of Brazil's soybean and meal exports has been going to
European markets, with the Netherlands, Germany and Italy being the most
important buyers. The United Kingdom, Spain and Denmark, however, show
promise for further market development, and exports to Japan may rise, al-
though much depends on that country's trade with China, which, like the U.S.
and Brazil, is a major producer of soybeans (Table 3).
42.       The soybean meal and cake category was second in importance in the
export trade in 1973, but fell to third place in 1974/75 because of the
abrupt rise in the world sugar market. From 1976 onward, the soybean group
is expected to have second place among Brazil's exports. The ranking in value
for the 1973/76 period is as follows:
1973    1974    1975    1976
------ US$ billiona --------
Coffee                           1.3     1.0      1.3     1.7
Sugar                            0.6     1.3      1.3     1.3
Soybeans, cake and meal          0.9     0.9      1.0     1.4
43.       Although peanut production has been static in recent years, and it
has been overshadowed by the soybean sector, as a secondary oilseed it holds
considerable importance as a foreign exchange earner. In 1972, peanut ex-
ports were valued at US$8.8 million, peanut meal exports at US$85.7 million
and peanut oil, the only large edible oil export, at US$27.4 million. This
commodity therefore accounted for total export earnings of US$126.7 million
in that year (Table 5). Peanut exports in all forms have higher unit values
than soybeans. Comparative trends in world prices of the major oilseed meals
are shown in Table 6.
44.       Cottonseed meal exports have fluctuated widely, following the
course of cotton production, but volume has been lower than the peak recorded
in 1969.  Cottonseed oil has been consumed entirely within the country since
that year (Table 7).
45.       Since the peak period of world demand for oilseeds and products in
1973/74, there has been a gradual easing of market conditions due to (a) better
crops in the U.S. and some other areas, and (b) lighter demand reflecting the
general economic situation and its effects on livestock production. The con-
tracting market for oilseeds and meal has caused a temporary tightness in the
vegetable oils market. Lower production of meals causes a concomitant reduc-
tion of oil supplies since the two products are linked together - oils being
a by-product of oilseed processing for meal.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 10
46.       The medium-term prospects for oilseeds have become difficult to
assess because of the massive grain purchases by the USSR in July 1975. The
resultant reevaluation of exportable surplus stocks in the main grain-
producing countries may cause a run-up in prices of cereals just as they
seemed to stabilize. The sharp decline in grain prices since 1974 had
improved feed-price ratios for livestock and poultry producers in North
America and Europe, so that the tendency to reduce herds and flocks ap-
parently was about to be reversed. That would have augured well for the
soybean export trade in particular.  However, because of the sharply rising
prices of grains, the outlook is for possibly a more modest growth of live-
stock and poultry and, consequently, for meals and for soybean meal in par-
ticular.  The other side of the coin is the greater likelihood for a continued
strong market for oils as the slower expansion of meal output will be a limit-
ing factor in oil supply.
47.       Over the longer term, there will be a basic uptrend in Brazil's
exports of soybeans and meal, based on increased demand for meat in all major
import markets. An illustrative projection of the trend of exports shown
in Table 8 indicates that soybean exports can be expected to rise from 4.0
million tons in 1976 to 5.4 million tons in 1980, an increase of 1.4 million
tons, or 35%. An average annual increase of 350,000 tons is projected. At
estimated export values of US$212 to US$318 per ton, export revenues should
expand from US$0.9 billion to US$1.5 billion in the interim to 1980.
48.       Soybean meal and cake exports are expected to follow a similar
growth path, going from 2.9 million tons in 1976 to 3.8 million tons in 1980,
an increase of 0.9 million tons, or 31%. At projected prices of US$174 to
US$309 from 1976 to 1980, export revenues generated by these exports would
expand from US$0.5 billion to US$1.1 billion. Soybeans, meal and oil could
well preempt the positions of coffee and of sugar as the leading export
commodity.
Marketing System
49.       Domestic and export trade in soybeans is handled by commodity trad-
ing houses, brokers and aqgrnts, most of whom are engaged in many commodity
lines, such as coffee, cotton, and corn. The principal international commodity
houses have branches in Brazil and, in many instances, processing subsidiaries
as well. In the domestic trade, the smaller brokers and agents compete with
the large firms and cooperatives. In the interior, intermediaries perform the
function of crop collectors for smaller, less accessible farms. Processors
which may be independent firms or connected with large trading houses, may
contract directly with producers for their crops.
50.       In the export field, larger cooperatives function in a manner simi-
lar to that of large trading companies, maintaining their own agents and
connections or even branch offices in important entrepots as well as member-
ship on the principal commodity exchanges such as the Chicago Board of Trade
and the London Terminal Market. Cooperatives service private processors,
shippers and international brokers and agents, while competing with them for
external markets.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-1
Page 11
51.       The Companhia Brasileira de Exportacao e Comerciao (COBEC), the
Government foreign trading agency, performs export sales service for the
Government or for private Brazilian firms. However, private firms and
cooperatives are also authorized by the Carteira do Comercio Exterior (CACEX)
to conduct hedging operations on foreign commodity exchanges to protect
forward export commitments.
52.       The processing industry is highly concentrated, with seven companies
accounting for 60% of the soybean oil output (para 5). Several large proces-
sors are subsidiaries of the international commodity houses. Vertical integ-
ration in edible oils processing and marketing, and also in balanced feeds
manufacture and marketing, among the large enterprises dominating the industry
has important implications for domestic marketing patterns. Relatively few
brands of consumer products share the bulk of consumer sales in the major con-
sumption centers. In fact, only the largest companies have the resources to
package and promote their brands and merchandise them effectively. Market
penetration by new firms basing their production on edible oils must be based
on product innovation to overcome the advantages held by the established en-
terprises. The important aspects from the broader national viewpoint in this
situation is for Federal agencies such as the Superintendencia Nacional de
Abastecimento (SUNAB) and the Comissao Interministerial de Precos (CIP) to
assure that competitive pricing is maintained to widen the consumption for
edibLe oils to a maximum degree.
Prices
53.       In May 1975, soybean producers were receiving Cr$ 70 per 60 kg
(US$146 per ton). Since domestic marketing costs are for the account of the
producer, an average of Cr$ 20 for transport, handling, and such (US$42 per
ton) must be deducted from the producer price, leaving an actual farmgate
price of Cr$ 50, or US$104 per ton. Producers claim their break-even point
is about that level. A sufficient incentive to expand production would be
provided by a price of Cr$ 90 per 60 kg (US$188 per ton). However, the
latter figure would be quite close to the c.i.f. Europe quotation of US$207
(June! 1975), and marketing problems would have been encountered.
54.       The producers' claims appear justified on the basis of estimated
costs of production, which, this season, are about Cr$ 1,200/ha. On an
average yield of 1,500 kg/ha, costs would come to Cr$ 88/60 kg, a losing
proposition for farmers with that average yield or less. However, on
moderately well managed farms where yields could average 1,800 kg/ha or
higher, costs would work out to Cr$ 40/60 kg. For the latter farms, producer
prices around Cr$ 70 are remunerative and substantially higher prices could
bring large windfall profits. The course of soybean producer prices since
1968 is shown in Table 9.
55.       Peanut oil and meal will experience declining shares of the market
as the dynamic expansion of soybean output continues. Reasons for the static
conditions in the peanut sector can be discerned on examination of peanut
costs and producer prices. The cost/price relationship certainly favors soy-
bean growers.

56.       It is claimed that the newer varieties of seed peanuts distributed
by the experimental station yield about 2.2 tons/ha, at which level peanut
production would be remunerative. However, at any level below the maximum,
the farmers would be switching out of peanuts. In any event, the seasonal
investment required per hectare is double that for soybeans. From that
standpoint alone, soybeans would hold more attraction for the farmer. The
trend of peanut producer prices since 1968 is shown in Table 10.
