ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Feed Processing
A. Production
General
1.        The production of animal feed in Brazil grew at an average annual
rate of about 15% between 1965 and 1974, in direct relation with the corre-
sponding increases in production of some of the raw materials, particularly
soybean and the expansion of the poultry and swine industries. The slight
expansion of milk and milk products due to increased prices in recent years
has probably also had some impact in feed production, and plans to increase
livestock raising in confinement and expand egg production will also have
an impact on the dem-nd for fed.in, the near future.
2.        Production figures for the feed industry in the 1965-74 period
are as follows:
Production           Growth Rate
Year                        (tons)                Index
1965                      1,400,000                100
1966                      1,600,000                114
1967                      1,700,000                121
1968                      1,900,000                136
1969                      2,300,000                164
1970                      2,500,000                179
1971                      2,771,507                198
1972                      3,214,384                230
1973                      4,017,580                287
1974                      5,223,374                373
Source:   Sindicato da Industria de Racoes Balanceadas do
Estado de Sao Paulo.
3.        As analysis of this historical period reveals that feed production
required about six years to double in the initial stages, but more recently
growth has been taking place at an increasing rate except in 1971 when con-
sumption stagnated at about the same level as in 1970, probably due to price
limitations in the feed consuming industries, and then increased again,
reaching about 26% per year for the 1971-74 period. Although Brazil has quite
favorable conditions for development of the feed mill sector in terms of agri-
cultural availability and animal production, the country's total production



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 2
of animal feed is still low when compared to that of more developed countries,
mainly because more scientific and industrialized breeding of poultry and swine
started only in recent years and is still undergoing development.
4.        It is reasonable to assume that the future growth of feed produc-
tion and feed consumption will maintain growth rates similar to those
historically observed since the increase in population and the elevation
of income and living standards in Brazil will require extended food produc-
tion of broilers, eggs, pork, beef and the like. Poultry production has
grown at an average rate of 10% per year and egg production is increasing
at about 7.4% annually, with a more industrialized marketing of egg yolk and
albumen under development.
5.        Poultry in Brazil in 1973 numbered 271,676,000 (Appendix 4-3) and
feed production for poultry in the same year was 3,044,750 tons. An average
3 kg of feed is necessary to bring one bird to a point where it is ready for
slaughter. Assuming that 2,500,000 tons of feed were used in the same year
for broilers and layers, and assuming four production cycles per year, it
can be estimated that approximately 75% of the poultry production in 1973
was fed by feed processed in feed mills.
6.        High-quality pork production based on improved hog-raising is
expanding, particularly in the States of Rio Grande do Sul, Parana and
Sao Paulo. It is estimated that 250 kg of feed are needed to bring a hog
up to the slaughter stage, approximately 80 kg of liveweight.
7.        Decreasing availability of pasture land in some of the states,
particularly Rio Grande do Sul, Rio de Janeiro and, to some extent, Parana
and Sao Paulo, has resulted in the initiation of improved husbandry for
production of beef and milk, particularly during the dry season. Although
these practices still represent only a small portion of the total beef and
milk production, increases in these sectors will result in higher consumption
of feeds. If Government targets are reached, beef production for export
and domestic consumption will reach 500,000 tons by the end of this decade,
with seven to 10 times the liveweight of cattle required for food. Supply
of milk and milk products, on the other hand, is expected to grow at an
annual rate of 7.7% until 1980.
8.        Based on historical production trends and on the analysis of the
prospects of performance in each of the above mentioned large consumption
categories (broiler and layer poultry, beef and milk cattle, swine and
others), the Sindicato da Industria de Racoes Balanceadas do Estado de Sao
Paulo worked out a production forecast for animal feed up to 1984 as follows:
Year            Production (tons)          Year      Production (tons)
1975               6,100,000               1980          13,000,000
1976               7,100,000               1981          15,100,000
1977               8,200,000               1982          17,600,000
1978               9,600,000               1983          20,000,000
1979              11,200,000               1984          23,900,000



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 3
9.        In 1972 the poultry industry absorbed about 4,200,000 tons of feed,
and projections indicate consumption of 8,000,000 tons in 1980. The produc-
tion of the latter amount will require 3,780,000 tons of corn, 1,350,000 tons
of soybean meal, 550,000 tons of meat meal, 550,000 tons of fish meal and
630,000 tons of wheat, rice, cottonseed, peanuts and other raw materials.
Availability of Raw Materials
10.       The primary raw materials for animal feed production in Brazil
are corn and soybean meal, while secondary raw materials are meat and fish
meals. All of these are domestically produced and the output, at least in
the case of the soybean meal, already is considerable. Additional raw
materials such as salt, blood meal, bone meal, fat, wheat and rice bran,
urea, molasses, cottonseed meal, vitamins and minerals, and antibiotics
require no special consideration since they represent only a small portion
of the final product and are easily substituted by other raw materials
available on the market.
11.       The availability of the primary and secondary raw materials is
generally good, although there is some variation in the minimum content
of amino acids, proteins, and the like from one purchase to another.
Unfortunately, only a few of the larger producers test the quality of the
raw materials received.
12.       Corn.  Corn production in the project area reached 12,536,144 tons
in 1973, representing 89% of the total production (Table 1). However, con-
servative estimates place 1974 production at the 15-million-ton level, and
forecasts for the 1975/76 crop indicate a production increase of about 6% to
7%, resulting in about 16 million tons for that year.
13.        Average yield for the project area in 1973 was 1,615 kg/ha, but
this figure can be considerably improved through better agricultural
practices, as shown by the State of Sao Paulo, which in the same year
obtained average fields of 2,020 kg/ha. The largest producer of corn in
1973 was Parana, with 21% of the national production, followed by Sao Paulo
with 18%. There are indications, however, that this order may have been
reversed later, probably due to expansion of soybean in Sao Paulo and in-
creased corn planting in Minas Gerais related to milk and poultry production.
As main producer of feeds, however, Sao Paulo is expected to provide the major
part of the corn used by feed mills and Parana is expected to make up the
eventual deficit in anticipated requirements.
14.       Corn represents the main source of amino acids among the feed raw
materials supplying the main energetic component of the mix. Its availabil-
ity is not a constraint on feed production and, in fact, expansion of the feed
industry can supply the necessary incentives for increased corn production.
The recently initiated programs to subsidize agricultural inputs, including
fertilizers and lime, opens the possibility of considerable expansion of
corn production, particularly in the cerrado soils, as long as market and
price conditions are favorable.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 4
15.       Soybean Meal.  Soybean meal, which essentially constitutes the
raw materials for the production of animal feeds, was initially a by-
product of the industrial extraction of vegetable oils. However, increases
in price and demand in recent years has tended to reverse this situation,
at least from a marketing point of view. During the last five years,
production of soybean has increased in Brazil at an average rate that was
better than 35% per year, a figure which is substantially higher than the
average growth for the world.
16.       Production of soybean reached 5,009,000 tons in 1973, and estimates
for the 1973/74 crop placed the figure at 7,151,000 tons. Projections made
by Escritorio Tecnico de Planejamento (PLANAVE) forecast crops of 11 million
tons in 1980 and 15 millions in 1985 (Appendix 5-1). While these predictions
are probably somewhat optimistic, the potential for soybean meal production
is no doubt very high. Considering that the oil extraction process yields 0.7
ton of meal for every ton of beans, and accepting the present estimate of 8.75
million tons for the 1975 crop, the potential for meal production would be 6.2
million tons. An estimate by Moinhos Riograndenses S.A. indicates that 40% of
soybean production goes into the external market, 10% is retained by the pro-
ducers for seed or animal feed, and 50% goes into the industrial domestic
market. This would place the potential production of soybean meal for 1975
at 3,062,500 tons.
17.       In 1974 Rio Grande do Sul was the main producer of soybean with
3.8 million tons, representing 53.1% of the nation's production, followed
by Parana with 2.1 million tons and Sao Paulo with 642,000 tons. The
potential for expansion of this crop in states such as Mato Grosso, for
instance, is very high and if realized could make even present production
forecasts seem conservative.
18.       Soybean meal constitutes the main source of protein in animal
feed and is therefore considered as essential for expansion of the beef,
pork and poultry industries. For these reasons, export quantities of
soybean meal are predetermined by the Government in order to prevent
domestic scarcities and upward price pressures in the domestic market.
19.       Meat Meal.  Meat meal is used in animal feeds in considerably
smaller quantities than either corn or soybean meal. It is a source of
animal protein and, when bone is included in the meal, supplies calcium and
several minor elements of the diet. Meat meal is mainly derived as a by-
product of beef processing, and, since the beef industry has been expanding,
due mainly to modernization of existing slaughterhouses and packing plants
and the construction of new plants with up-to-date mechanical equipment for
processing cattle by-products, it is easily available.
20.       Another source of meat meal, although considerably smaller than
the beef processing industry, is the pork processing sector.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 5
21.       Information available from the Departamento de Inspecao de
Produtos de Origem Animal (DIPOA) for 1973 indicate production of 136,396
tons of beef derived meals in Brazil as a whole and 131,729 tons in the
project area, representing 97% of the total production (Table 2). This
figure represents only inspected production of meat, meat and bones, hooves
and horns, blood, bone, autoclaved bone, liver and lung, and offal meals,
and, since not all of the beef slaughter is inspected, production is prob-
ably higher by 30 to 50%.
22.       Fish Meal.  Fish meal is another source of animal protein used
for poultry feeds. However, should it become unavailable or scarce in the
domestic market, it can be replaced by other raw materials such as soybean
meal or meat meal without causing the final product to lose its protein
or energy producing qualities.
23.       The leading fish meal producing states are Rio de Janeiro, Rio
Grande do Sul, Santa Catarina and Sao Paulo, with productions of 8,157.5
tons, 7,296 tons, 5,881 tons and 1,617 tons, respectively, in 1973 (Table 3).
These figures, however, represent only DIPOA-inspected products, the total
production being somewhat larger.
24.       In recent years, the State of Sao Paulo has received a large part
of the Superintendencia do Desenvolvimento da Pesca (SUDEPE) fiscal incentives
for construction of new industrial plants and modernization of existing ones,
which should raise production of this feed considerably over the next few years.
B. The Industry
25.       Feed mill industries in Brazil are owned mainly by private companies;
only 5% to 10% belong to cooperatives. Eleven large companies within the pri-
vate sector produce 70% to 75% of the total animal feed production in Brazil
(Table 4). Four of them, together responsible for more than 40% of the pro-
duction, are multi-nationally owned, while seven are nationally owned. The
feed mills are situated mainly in the southern part of Brazil, with the
following dispersion in the four zones:
Zone 1    North and Northeast of Brazil             -    8%
Zone 2    Espirito Santo, Rio de Janeiro,
Minas Gerais, Goias                       -   17%
Zone 3    Sao Paulo, Mato Grosso                    -   55%
Zone 4    Parana, Santa Catarina,
Rio Grande do Sul                         -   20%
According to Sindicato da Industria de Racoes Balanceadas in Sao Paulo, no
significant changes in the production rate between national and multi-national
producers would be expected within the coming years.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 6
26.       The two main cooperatives are COTIA and SUBBRAZIL, both federated
cooperatives, headquartered in the city of Sao Paulo. COTIA is the larger
of the two, with 10,000 members scattered over the states of Sao Paulo,
Parana, and M4ato Grosso and a few in Rio de Janeiro and Minas Gerais. COTIA
had an average monthly production of 8,500 tons of feedstuff in 1969,
increasing to 20,000 tons in 1974, or a yearly increase of about 15%. A
forecast doubling that capacity within five years has been worked out. In
Minas Gerais, a new cooperative consisting of 300 members has just been
created, but, apart from this, no plans for increasing the number of members
has been made. SUBBRAZIL has a production of about 30% of COTIA's. As
feedstuff production is only a small part of the cooperative's activities,
no expansion of the market share within the feedstuff production is expected
within the foreseeable future.
27.       Feed mills have traditionally been located close to the production
areas of corn and soybeans because of the problems associated with purchasing
raw materials.  Most are in the State of Sao Paulo in the two main production
areas in the southwest and in the district of Sao Paulo.  However, as produc-
tion increases, feed mills would likely be more dispersed in order to lower
the transportation cost of raw materials and the distribution cost of feedstuff.
28.       Feed mills vary greatly in size.  According to Sindicato da
Industria de Racoes Balanceadas do Estado de Sao Paulo, the capacity of exist-
ing mills range from 1,000 tons to 15,000 tons per month. New feed mills to
be built within the next five years, however, are expected to have capacities
ranging from 5,000 tons to 20,000 tons per month. Five new mills under con-
struction now have an average capacity of about 15,000 tons per month.
29.       The general condition of the feed mills is good.  The larger private
firms and the cooperatives have high technical standards and all the larger
mills have their own laboratories, even though not all are well equipped with
efficient testing apparatus.
Plant Operations
30.       The basic equipment installed in feed mills are weighbridges for
trucks and railroad cars, mechanical conveyors, dryers, cleaners, hammer
mills, weighing and dosing equipment, mixers, pelleting machines and mecha-
nical and electrical controls. Nearly all mechanical equipment is available
in Brazil, but some highly specialized items, representing 5% to 10% of total
cost must be imported, such as dies and rollers for the pelleting machines and
some mechanical/electrical controls and laboratory equipment. Some of the
large international feed mill manufacturers have offices or representatives
in Brazil and they are able to furnish such equipment.
31.       Raw materials are received either in bulk or bags.  Receiving
samples are drawn before the trucks and railroad cars are unloaded and
material can be rejected if the quality is found to be too low. This right,
however, is seldom exercised. If acceptable, raw materials are transported
on conveyors for any necessary drying and cleaning, after which they are



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 7
stored. The bigger feed mills generally have drying and cleaning equipment
in connection with raw material silos, but, since smaller ones do not, they
normally purchase grain and soybean already dried and cleaned. When required
for production, the raw materials are ground and weighed together according
to the specific formulas by semi-automatic weighing machines. The mixing
takes place in horizontal or vertical mixers after premixes, fat, molasses,
and such are added. After mixing, the meal products go either to the auto-
matic weighing machines for outloading or to the pelleting machines. Approx-
imately 25% of animal feed produced in Brazil is in pellet form and the rate
seems to be increasing.
32.       Feed mills are mechanized to a very limited extent and use quite
a lot of laborers. The average seems to be about 100 workers for a production
of 12,000 tons of feedstuff per month. As labor is still relatively cheap
in Brazil, it is expected that new feedstuff plants will be semi-automatically
controlled, using about 60 workers in a 12,000-ton-per-month plant. Since
there is so little automation, a breakdown of machinery does not cause a
significant interruption of production.
33.       A problem common to many of the feed mills in Brazil is the
scarcity or nearly total lack of storage facilities for raw materials such
as corn and soybeans. Storage space for less than eight days of production
is not unusual and, due to the seasonality of these crops, it is often dif-
ficult to purchase them before harvest periods. It should be emphasized that
when new feed mills are built, raw material storage for at least one month of
production should also be built unless raw material supply can be guaranteed
from other sources.
Environmental Aspects
34.       Pollution from feed mills is limited mainly to dust coming from
the receiving and discharge points and from air outlets, with losses in
older plants varying from 1% to 3% of the total feedstuff production. In
order to reduce these losses (valued at an average of Cr$ 25 per ton),
automatic dust collecting filters are often installed in new feedstuff
plants, bringing the pollution down to a negligible amount. No Government
restrictions regarding limits on dust pollution have yet been issued in Brazil.
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
35.       The market for balanced feeds has grown by 273% in the past decade,
from 1.4 million tons in 1965 to 5.2 million tons in 1974. This accelerated
growth, equivalent to 14.1% per year, was based on growth of 10%, per year in
the broiler industry and 7.4% annually in egg production, as well as on im-
proved methods of hog, beef and milk production. Among those sectors, the
poultry industry is by far the mainstay of the balanced feed industry.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 8
36.       Estimates published by the Sindicato da Industria de Racoes Balan-
ceadas do Estado de Sao Paulo (SIRBESP) indicate that the market for balance
feeds is at present distributed as follows:
Poultry                   75
Pigs                      12
Cattle                    10
Others                     3
37.       Consumption of balanced feeds on a tonnage basis, estimated by
the aforementioned association, is shown below for the past four years:
1971                1972               1973             1974
Tons        %        Tons       %      Tons         Z    Tons       %
Poultry   2,148,640    73      2,435,800   76   3,044,750    76  3,958,175  76
Pigs        394,920    14        394,920   12       490,650    12    641,745  12
Cattle      297,656    10         297,656    9      375,070     9    483,691   9
Others       86,008      3        86,008    3       107,510     3    139,763   3
TOTAL    2,927,224   100      3,214,384  100   4,017,980   100  5,223,374 100
38.       The balanced feed industry is concentrated in the Center-South region,
the area of the Agro-industries Credit Project, where its main markets and
sources of supply of raw materials are located. It is in this area also
that the corn and soybean production belts are situated. These grains are
the principal ingredients of the balanced feed, and the feed industry, in
turn, is the main domestic market for those crops.
39.       The distribution of balanced feed consumption in the country is
as follows:
Sao Paulo                                      45
Parana, Santa Catarina, Rio Grande do Sul    20
Espirito Santo, Rio de Janeiro, Minas
Gerais, Goias                             17
North, Northeast                                 8
40.       The feed milling enterprises market their basic products;  (a)
balanced feeds which are complete protein-rich rations for livestock and
poultry; and (b) feed concentrates which are mixtures without corn. Poultry
and livestock farmers in the corn belt frequently prefer to purchase concen-
trates and mix the ground corn on their own farms, thus saving a small margin
from handling, commission and transportation of the grain.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 9
41.       Sales of feed concentrates to corn-belt areas can rise to as much
as 40% of total sales in the months immediately following the corn harvest.
That pattern may be reinforced with improved on-farm storage resulting from
current governmental incentive programs to construct such facilities. In
regions more distant from the corn-production localities, such as the North
and Northeast, sales consist almost exclusively of balanced feeds.
42.       Marketing of feeds is complicated by the varying needs of hog,
cattle, dairy and poultry producers. The large feedmills offer as many as
40 different formulas for various purposes. This variety creates a complex
inventory problem, which requires expert management to maintain the most
efficient and economic production schedules, and combines custom mixing
with shipments directly from inventory. Inadequate coordination of produc-
tion and inventory controls could reduce or eliminate profit margins. There
is also a time dimension to the inventory problem since mixtures are not
generally held more than 30 days because of possible deterioration of some
of the more perishable ingredients.
43.       The modern feed mill now receives its supplies of grain and soy-
beans in bulk, by truck or rail. Marketing of feeds, however, is still
largely in sacks for convenience of retailers who must deal with many small
farmers. Larger enterprises, particularly the large broiler producers, are
gradually constructing on-farm storage in the form of bins or silos so that
they can receive deliveries in bulk. Savings can be substantial in bulk
deliveries, the cost of sacks alone amounting to Cr$ 100 per ton. Large
bulk buyers can negotiate directly with the feedmills, thereby eliminating
distributor's margins and extra handling costs.
44.       The complicated variety of balanced feed mixes offered in part
reflects the different sectors serviced as well as the broad marketing area
encompassed. A typical Sao Paulo feed mill supplies balanced feed to cus-
tomers as distant as Bahia, Pernambuco and Belem. Markets for balanced
feeds in the North and Northeast have not developed to the point where they
can sustain economic feedmill operations.
45.       Because of the antiquated conditions of the rail system, transport
requires up to 40 days to the more distant points. Moreover, the problem
of different gauges when passing from one system to another en route can be
surmounted only by transhipment which increases handling to intolerable
levels. In consequence, balances feeds are transported almost exclusively
by truck. Illustrative 1975 trucking tariffs shown below demonstrate the
cost of distribution of this basic ingredient in the development of the
dairy and swine industries in particular, in areas distant from the grain
belt of the Center-South:
Cr$/ton        US$/ton
Campinas-Fortaleza                    470              59
Campinas-Recife                       430              54
Campinas-Salvador                     320              40
Campinas-Vitoria                      160              20
Campinas-Rio-Belo Horizonte            90              11



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 10
The bulk of the average feed mill's shipment goes within a radius of 150 to
200 km.
46.       The larger integrated feed mills have their own soybean milling
facilities. Surplus soybean meal output from these operations is shipped
in bulk to smaller feed mills or to exporters. Soybean oil generally is
sold to specialized firms for further refining, and soapstock (chemical
residue from oil refining) is sold to soap manufacturers.
47.       As the production zones for poultry, hogs, beef and dairy become
more diffused, the location of the newer feedmills closer to the consumer
areas in Mato Grosso, Goias and Minas Gerais may seem a logical movement
to reduce transport costs. Such locations must be well planned in order to
achieve maximum economies. There will be some obvious trade-offs in terms
of possibly longer distances from suppliers of not only corn and soybeans,
but of the numerous other inputs that comprise the balanced ration formulas.
For the large integrated units that may include soybean processing equipment
as well, greater distances to customers for the oil, soapstock and surplus
meal will entail diseconomies that would partly offset other benefits.
48.       The larger feed mills, as part of their marketing strategy, have
a staff of animal husbandry specialists and veterinarians who perform serv-
ices similar to extension specialists. These services are, of course, limited
to users of the company's products. Despite the commercial basis of the serv-
ices rendered by these specialists, they are performing a valuable function in
providing assistance to the farmers, and helping to raise the general level of
agricultural technology in the country.
Projections
49.       Based on prevailing estimates of growth in output of poultry, hogs,
dairy and beef cattle over the coming decade, and the likely trends in usage
of balance feeds in the various sectors, forecasts have been made by the
Sindicato da Industria de Racoes Balanceadas do Estado de Sao Paulo (SIRBESP)
for an even more accelerated growth in the future than has prevailed in the
past. Demand for balanced feeds in 1984 is placed at 23.5 million tons,
392% more than in the current year, indicating an anticipated annual growth
rate of 16.5%. Growing awareness of the benefits and indeed the necessity
of improved technology in increasing productivity witnessed by the mission
in the various sectors indicate that prospects for greater use of balanced
feeds are excellent. The trend of balanced feeds usage in Brazil to 1984,
as indicated by projected demand is as follows:
Million tons                    Million tons
1975                 6.1             1980             13.0
1976                 7.1             1981             15.1
1977                 8.2             1982             17.6
1978                 9.6             1983             20.0
1979                11.2             1984            23.9
Source:   SIRBESP.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 5-2
Page 11
Prices
50.       The feed mill industry has benefited by the producer price support
policies administered by the Comissao de Fixacao de Precos (CFP), which has
helped farmers greatly in obtaining better prices during the harvest season.
It may also have assisted the feed mills by removing a considerable speculative
element in the commodity market and set a floor under the prices for the
main ingredients, corn and soybeans. Prices prevailing in mid-1975 for
several important raw materials were as follows:
Cr$/ton        US$/ton
Soybean                 950           119
Corn                    635            79
Meat meal             1,270           159
Fish meal             2,340           293
For further details, see Table 5.
51.       Feed prices are regulated by the Comissao Interministerial de
Precos (CIP) which administers price policy for all processed and manufactured
goods. Cost analyses are submitted to CIP once a year and feed prices are
determined on that basis. This procedure can also be initiated at any time
should the feed industry want to raise prices. The CIP determines prices on
the basis of the new cost data and consideration of corroborating cost evi-
dence from related and supplier industries. Corn and soybean prices, for
obvious reasons, have a major influence on the cost structure of the feed
industry. Average prices being quoted by the industry for representative
formulas are set forth below.
Cr$/ton         US$/ton
Balanced feeds         995            128
Concentrates         1,356            170
Details are given in Table 6.
52.       Fiscal incentives have been accorded the feed industry to encourage
greater production and consumption of balanced feeds. The industry has been
exempted from the turnover tax, Imposto de Circulacao do Mercado (ICM).

BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Milk Processing
A. Production
1.        An increase in population and income in recent years in Brazil has
resulted in higher consumption of animal-derived products, particularly beef
and milk. The milk industry, however, has not taken advantage of its oppor-
tunity to grow. In general terms, the growth rate of milk production has not
even been equal to the absorption capacity of the market, particularly in
the last decade. This has resulted in frequent crises of supply in the large
urban centers, particularly during the dry months, and in considerable imports
of powdered milk or other fresh milk substitutes.
2.        Other reasons for the shortage of milk and milk products include
the expansion of beef production and the predominance of the dual-purpose
herd, which is easily switched to the more lucrative beef market. Low pro-
ductivity is another factor cutting down supply. However, recent increases
in milk price got a reaction from producers that showed their ability to in-
crease the supply in short periods in response to increased profits. Average
milk production for Brazil is presently 2.37 liters/cow/day, but in the South
and Southeast regions it is somewhat higher.
3.        Of the total milk production in the country in 1973, the Southeast
region (Minas Gerais, Sao Paulo, Espirito Santo, and Rio de Janeiro) contri-
buted 56%; the South (Rio Grande do Sul, Santa Catarina and Parana), 26%; and
the Center-West (Goias and Mato Grosso), 6%. The project area, therefore,
contributed 88% to the national production (Table 1). Output of raw milk
in 1974 was forecast at 72.1 billion liters. Farm and retail price of milk
was raised several times in the same year and was expected to increase further
in early 1975. In spite of this, the outlook for milk and milk products in
the next five years is apparently that a greater deficit will occur between
demand and production, with increasing shortages of fresh milk, powdered
milk, cheese, condensed milk, butter and yogurt, unless policy decisions are
made to improve the situation for the milk producers, as well as for the
processors, distributors and industrialists.
4.        Recent substantial increases in the financing available to milk
producers, including a specific credit line for pasture improvement, plus
the announced policy of allowing higher producer prices that would be in
line with input costs, may result in a significant growth of dairy products
in the near future. However, an estimate of milk production, demand and



ANNEX 6
Page 2
deficit, prepared by the Fundacao Getulio Vargas in March 1974, indicates
production of 10,339,000 tons in 1975, demand for 11,178,000 tons, and a
deficit of 839,000 tons. For 1980, the estimate is even more pessimistic,
with a production of 13,273,000 tons, demand for 14,782,000 tons, and a
deficit of 1,509,000 tons.
5.        Increasing implementation of sanitary inspection by the Departamento
de Inspecao de Produtos de Origem Animal (DIPOA) clearly establishes the
trend towards larger and more modern milk processing plants, which require a
reliable and extensive supply of the raw products for an economically viable
operation. At present, however, the very nature of the production operation
favors smaller producers, often associated only for the marketing of the raw
milk. A study conducted by the Instituto de Economia Agricola of the State
of Sao Paulo (IPEA) showed that 88% of the milk processing units can be
considered small, with an output of less than 100 liters per day. These
units, however, contribute 40% of the total milk production in Sao Paulo.
B. The Industry
Structure
6.        There is a critical milk shortage in Brazil, especially in the
large urban areas during the dry season, when pastures do not provide suffi-
cient nutrients for the dairy herd and most of the farmers do not feed their
stock. The dairy herd in Brazil is estimated at about 12 million head, with
the average farmer owning 25 cows. The yield per cow is low, 2.37 liters per
day, compared to the more developed countries where production per cow
averages 12 to 15 liters. One reason is that much of the dairy herd is not
bred to produce milk. Only 15% are actually specialized in this field, while
352 are dual purpose milk and beef breeds and the balance are inferior breeds
with low production. In many states, the farms are located in rural areas
with no access to major roads, which complicates milk collection and results
in losses due to spoilage or milk simply being kept on the farm.
7.        Brazil does not produce high quality milk, as most of the production
on which prices are based is "C" grade. Very little "B" grade is available
at higher prices and practically no grade "A" is produced. Cheese production
is quite important since many small farmers that cannot get milk to dairies
manufacture cheese for local consumption. Also, dairies make cheese out of
milk that cannot be sold pasteurized because of high acidity or excess
capacity during peak production periods. Powdered skim milk is also manu-
factured by a number of dairies, but the supply is not sufficient to meet
the national demand. Therefore, Brazil must import powdered milk to meet
requirements. Butter is another product that is manufactured by many dairies.
However, because of the milk shortage, vegetable oils and margarine are
being used as substitutes. Condensed milk is produced by a few companies, but
production is greater than demand so Brazil is a net exporter. Yogurt is



ANNEX 6
Page 3
increasing in popularity and several new plants have been built in recent
years to supply this market. Ice cream is of minor importance though there
are a number of dairies and firms that specialize in this field.
8.        As most of the milk is produced by small farmers, cooperative
dairies have been formed to industrialize production. A few private companies
own their own herds to supply a major part of their requirements and they
purchase the balance of their needs under contracts with local producers.
State-owned dairies do not play an important role in Brazil's production.
9.        In April of 1975, DIPOA certified 1,013 milk operations, of which
98% were in the project area. These units consisted of dairies, 56%; pasteur-
izing plants, 21%; and collecting stations with refrigeration facilities, 17%.
The remaining 6% are various types of collecting or holding stations. Minas
Gerais, supplier of dairy products to Rio de Janeiro and Sao Paulo, has the
greatest concentration of milk installations -- 61%; Sao Paulo is next with
16%; Rio de Janeiro has 7%; Goias, 6%; Parana, 3%; Espirito Santo and Rio
Grande do Sul, 2% each; and Mato Grosso, Santa Catarina and the Federal
District, 1% each.
10.       The largest share of Brazilian dairies is owned by cooperatives
that are organized at local, regional and state or central levels, depending
on their marketing areas. The cooperatives range in size from very small
plants with capacities of 5,000 liters per day, small plants that can handle
up to 9,000 liters per day, medium-sized units with production of under
100,000 liters per day, and a few large plants in urban areas with capacities
of 800,000 to 1,000,000 liters per day. There are a number of large privately
owned companies that manufacture specialty products such as powdered skim
milk, condensed milk, yogurt and cheeses. The major raw milk processors
are COPL in Rio de Janeiro, accounting for 28% of all Brazil's processed
milk; Leite Paulista in Sao Paulo, 23%; and CCPR in Belo Horizonte, 9%. The.
remaining 40% consists of companies such as Leite Vigor, Dana, Lacesa, Pocos
de Caldas, Rex, Leite Big, Gloria, Nestle, and many small- and medium-sized
producers. The industry is national in character, with only a few inter-
national companies, like Leite Gloria, Nestle, Kibon and Danone who manufacture
specialty products.
11.       In January 1972, the first phase of the Programa de Laticinio was
instituted under the direction of the Banco de Desenvolvimento do Estado de
Minas Gerais (BDMG). The target was a total capacity of 730,000 liters per
day in the first phase of the program, but actual capacity in 1973, with
expansions in place, came to 840,000 liters/day. Fourteen projects involving
11 firms were completed ahead of schedule. Total investment came to Cr$ 157
million.
12.       In Phase II of the program, expansion of 600,000 liters/day is
envisaged during 1974/76. Six projects will add 210,000 liters/day and
three projects under consideration will add 390,000 liters/day. Total in-
vestment for the expansion envisioned will be Cr$ 61 million. The completed



ANNEX 6
Page 4
expansions should bring capacity in the State of Minas Gerais to 1.44 million
liters/day.
Regulations
13.       Production of fluid milk, butter and cheese have been under partial
inspection by state and federal agencies for a number of years, but starting
in January of 1975, DIPOA, through their Milk Inspection Section (DILEI),
assumed the responsibility for sanitary inspection from the various state
agencies involved. Existing regulations are outmoded, but they will be super-
seded by new ones which are being prepared on the basis of international stan-
dards. DILEI is also reviewing and approving all new dairy plant construction
and facilities to insure that they meet proposed standards.
14.       The large number of small- and medium-sized dairies in a country
the size of Brazil will present a serious inspection problem, since the
distance between plants is considerable and the individual plant size in many
cases does not warrant full-time inspectors. A shortage of trained personnel
complicates the problem. Consequently, the small- and medium-sized units
will have to come under some form of area supervision, with periodic inspec-
tions to insure that the plants are complying with the sanitary regulations.
The large plants have full-time inspectors and, according to DILEI, 40% of
all dairies now have permanent inspection.
Plant Operations
15.       The size of the average Brazilian dairy farm precludes the use of
milking machines, so that only 3% of the milk is produced in this manner.
Also, because most of the farms lack cooling equipment, a large portion of
the milk produced is never processed because of poor quality. Normally,
milk from the farms is placed in 50-liter metal containers for transport to a
collecting station or dairy, where it is cooled, analyzed and processed, or
it is taken by bulk milk trucks to the larger processing plants.
16.       In the small- and medium-sized plants, most of the operations are
done manually in batch-type equipment, which makes them labor-intensive,
while the large plants compare favorably with those in developed countries
in regard to plant layout, equipment and processing methods. As there is a
wide fluctuation in the daily milk reception due to seasonal variations, many
plants have a limited pasteurizing capacity to meet their normal fluid milk
requirements and any excess is diverted to industrialized products. Grade
"C" milk is low in fat content; therefore, any excess over the grade require-
ments is used for butter, as is fat removed from powdered skim milk. Cheese
can be made from this excess supply and also from some of the milk which is
too acid to be sold as pasteurized milk.
17.       Two types of dairies can be considered as standard for the industry --
the plants that supply fluid milk for the urban centers and industrialized
by-products and the specialized plants that have been designed to meet a



ANNEX 6
Page 5
special market sector, such as yogurt, frozen milk or ice cream, condensed
milk, and powdered skim milk. The latter type plant can also manufacture
some by-products from the components that are not required for normal
operations.
18.       Competent management can be found in all of the larger dairies,
which also train their technical and production personnel. The small- and
medium-sized plants lack the production volume to warrant a trained staff,
consequently, their technical level is inferior.
Equipment
19.       lJuch of dairy equipment is fabricated from stainless steel that must
be imported. There are a number of local manufacturers that make tanks and
tubing, though care must be exercised in preparing sanitary welds to avoid
the problem of bacterial contamination from areas that cannot be sanitized
properly. Foreign companies have established local subsidiaries that produce
a wide variety of equipment for the dairy industry and can supply a large
portion of national requirements. Specialized equipment, such as homogenizers,
plates for pasteurizers, separators, sanitary pumps, and some packaging
machinery, must still be imported.
Environmental Aspects
20.       The small size of much of the dairy industry presents a water
pollution problem, since these plants cannot afford to install waste water
treatment equipment and discharge their liquid waste material into municipal
sewage lines or streams. The larger plants, on the other hand, can afford
minimal water treatment facilities, but many still discharge waste from their
plants at contamination levels above those set by current pollution regulations.
The new plants approved by DILEI should meet these standards and as sanitary
inspections become more widespread, the older and smaller plants will have
to modernize their facilities or go out of business.
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
21.       Imports of powdered milk, butter and cheese totalled US$28.4 million
in 1974 and although milk production is continually expanding -- farm output
of fresh milk rose from 7.1 million tons in 1970 to about 10 million tons
in 1974 -- the main problem is the wide seasonal variations in output. The
annual production cycle will be somewhat ameliorated by the pasture improvement
program to which the Government allocated substantial sums in May 1975, but
substantial expansion of powdered milk processing plants and modernization
and expansion of the national dairy plant will be required to handle rising
milk production in the years to come. Even though the trough of the annual
production cycle may thus be improved, the problem of processing excess output
of milk at the peak of the annual cycle will remain.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
22.       Dairies are generally located near local consumption centers -- a
small or large urban area and the environs which it serves. In the larger
urban areas, two or more dairies may be in competition with one another.
Typically, the marketing area extends over a radius of 100 to 150 km, although
the larger dairies may market milk by-products in distant localities across
the country.
23.       The lairies usually distribute fresh milk and milk products directly
to retail outlets in nearby areas or localities. Where longer distances are
involved and large refrigerated trucks are used, only the large supermarkets,
wholesale distributors or the dairy's own branches receive direct deliveries.
24.       Fresh milk supply follows a seasonal pattern similar to beef output.
In the center-south of Brazil, full production usually runs from November to
June, while during the dry season from July to October, daily receipts of
fresh milk at dairies run about 65% to 70% of the level during the November-
June period. The usual supply-demand pattern for a dairy apparently is that
the level of absorption of the local market is somewhat below the peak pro-
duction level during the February-April period. Some of the larger dairies
have drier equipment so that they can divert the excess supply of fresh milk
to powdered milk production. Other dairies make more by-products such as
cheese, butter, and "doce de leite" 1/ that can be stored. The powdered milk
is used as an extender in the fresh milk during the low season. The market
for by-products is still limited in Brazil, so that smaller dairies that do
not have drier equipment have great difficulty in adjusting their operations
to fluctuating deliveries of fresh milk. Building up inventories of by-
products also creates a financial strain on the smaller dairies.
25.       The problem of managing the fluctuating fresh milk supply can readily
be perceived by observing the typical product mix of a dairy.
Product                  _%
Fresh milk               76
Butter                   13
Cheeses                   9
Yogurt                    2
Since fresh milk constitutes a predominant portion of the product mix, it can
.ily be appreciated that the only economic solution to the seasonal
problem is to dry the excess milk during the high season. Switching to by-
products could cause marketing and financial problems by generating inventories
1/   A unique Brazilian by-product.



ANNEX 6
Page 7
of unsaleable items in a limited market. This would be particularly true for
aged cheese with relatively high value for which there is a narrow market.
Concomitantly, greater purchases of imported powdered milk would be necessary
to stretch the fresh milk supply during the low season.
26.       There is clearly need for additional drier capacity in the country.
However, such equipment must be combined with modern facilities of a scale
that would permit maximum utilization of the total integrated dairy complex.
This would include cold storage capacity for powdered milk and by-products.
A viable enterprise ideally would have the marketing capability to profitably
place by-products in distant markets so as to have maximum flexibility in
adjusting the product mix. Existing drier capacity, though insufficient, is
also unevenly distributed. There reportedly are 29 plants in Parana and
only three in Minas Gerais, although production in those states was 0.5
million tons and 2.9 million tons respectively in 1973.
27.       Aggregate demand for milk -- pasteurized and in by-product forms --
in 1974 was an estimated 10.4 million tons, as compared with 7.3 million tons
in 1970, a growth rate of 9.7% per annum. Important factors in the broadening
demand for milk, apart from population growth and rising incomes, are (a)
education of the public on the health aspects of milk consumption; and (b)
introduction of the plastic container (in 1969), which greatly facilitated
the efficient low-cost distribution of fresh milk. Demand for fresh milk
was earlier projected to rise at a rate of 13.2% per annum to 1975 and 11% per
annum thereafter to 1980. Those projections are optimistic as compared with
the forecasts of the Fundacao Getulio Vargas, which call for growing shortfalls
in 1975 and 1980, as follows:
1975                      1980
__----  --------('000 tons) -
Production                10,339                     13,273
Demand                    10,985 - 11,178            14,026 - 14,782
Balance                     -646 -   -839              -753 - -1,509
Percent increase           1975 - 1980          =     27.7% - 32.2%
Growth rate                1975 - 1980          =      5.0% -  5.8%
28.       In 1972, the production of milk by-products was as follows:
'000 tons
Butter                         33
Cheese                        70
Powdered milk                 125
Condensed milk                 35



ANNEX 6
Page 8
Of the 7.14 million tons of milk produced, 70.8% was consumed in the form of
fresh milk and 29.2% was transformed into by-products. That was the highest
proportion processed on record and reflected mainly the increase in powder~.
milk production.
29.       Demand among median and upper income groups is expanding rapidly
for specialty by-products such as multi-flavored yogurt, "doce de leite",
condensed milk, and cream. The Instituto de Desenvolvimento Industrial de
Minas Gerais (INDI) estimates that 1975 consumption of milk by-products will
be as follows:
Index
'000 tons             (1968-69 = 100)
Butter                   99                     176
Cheese                  159                     167
Powdered milk           357                     190
Although they are minor items among milk by-products (and are included in the
fresh milk category in consumption statistics), yogurt and jellied milk have
shown a particularly sharp increase in volume -- from 8-million liters in
1968 to 22.9 million liters in 1972. In the latter year, milk taken in these
forms made up 0.3% of fresh milk consumption.
30.       In recent years, the volume of milk by-products has been growing
steadily, but until 1972, that growth paralleled the uptrend in total milk
output, so that the proportion of milk processed fluctuated around 80%. As
larger, modern dairy plants are built, the tendency is to place greater em-
phasis on processed products. The main reason for that is the cost-price
squeeze at the processing level for fresh milk. Due to the trend toward
greater emphasis on by-products, there was a pronounced maldistribution of
milk during the low season when a deficit occurred in milk production. The
Government has therefore decreed that the large industries that specialize
in milk by-products, such as Nestle and Danone, must direct 40% of their
normal fresh milk receipts to the fresh milk market for a specified period.
That measure will probably become a permanent feature of milk policy in the
future as improved distribution and merchandising through the expanding super-
market system and more sophisticated consumer demand encourage a proliferation
of specialized milk by-product processing firms.
31.       Transport is a large item in the cost structure of milk, a product
which has a shelf life of only one or two days. The production zone which
supplies the dairy is typically up to 600 km in size, with the movement of
milk to market taking up to 24 hours to accomplish. A system of collection
centers usually constitutes the primary phase of the evacuation of milk. From
those centers, large tanker trucks then haul the milk to the dairy, often an
overnight run.



ANNEX 6
Page 9
32.       At the producer level, transport to the collection center is
arranged by the producers. A group of cooperative members may have a truck,
or may contract with an independent driver. Other producers, individually
or in groups, arrange transport along similar lines. Where volume is heavy
and tank trucks are required, independent truckers provide contractual service.
Independent truckers in the producing zones also may act as intermediaries
or collect milk on a commission basis for the dairy. Because of the heavy
investment required for tanker trucks in particular, independent truckers
provide an important part of equipment inventory for producers and dairies
alike.
33.       Transport costs from the producer to the collection center and from
the collection center to the dairy amount of Cr$ 0.03 to Cr$ 0.12 per liter,
depending on distance. Cooperative producers (Parana) are charged individually
on a predetermined scale according to their location. In Minas Gerais, esti-
mates run from Cr$ 0.05 to Cr$ 0.10 per liter, in all cases for the account of
the producer. Distance to the collection centers from farms averages 60 to
70 km (Minas Gerais). Taking as a base a producer price of Cr$ 1.35 per
liter, maximum trucking costs for the producer run from 7% to 9%.
34.       In order to facilitate evacuation of milk to the collection centers
there is urgent need for improvement of infrastructure in the producing areas,
such as paving of roads. During the rainy season, when output levels are high,
unpaved roads occasionally become impassable for several days at a time.
Farmers are then compelled to dump their milk because they have no equipment
to hold it, and because they must continue milking. Farmers in Brazil
apparently are not accustomed to making butter on the farm, as farmers in
the US have done in earlier periods.
35.       Local distribution from the dairy to retail outlets usually is by
unrefrigerated truck (e.g. VW Kombi). Refrigerated trucks are used for
transport of milk to distribution centers away from the dairy's own immediate
zone.
Imports
36.       Among dairy imports, powdered mill is by -far the most important
item (Tables 2 and 3). Since 1970, powdered milk imports registered a low
of 11,900 tons in 1972 and a record high of 54,100 tons in 1973. Imports have
tended to fluctuate widely over the years, but more recently the basic trend
is up, although that may reflect a lag in domestic output as demand for milk
and milk products increases. Butter imports have rebounded sharply, averaging
2,590 tons in 1972/74 -- a level not registered since the early 1960s. However,
butter remains a minor item with aggregate import value averaging US$2.1 million
in the 1972/74 period. Cheese imports number about two dozen varieties, mainly
specialty items from European countries. In contrast to the other dairy im-
ports, cheese imports have shown a steady upward trend throughout the past
decade and are likely to continue as a minor but high-value specialty category
from 60 tons in 1964 to 870 tons in 1973/74.



ANNEX 6
Page 10
37.       Very small quantities of dairy products are shipped to neighboring
countries and to the Caribbean area and that trade is rather sporadic.
Prices
38.       Prices of milk have been raised recently in order to provide
additional incentive to producers. The charges are listed below:
Producer     Wholesale       Retail
price          price
--- ------ (Cr$/liters)---------
Pre-December 1973    0.68/1                        0.73
1974    1.00                         1.40
January       1975    1.35           1.60          1.90
May           1975    1.45                          1.90
/I        Based on minimum butterfat content of 3.1%.
Prices of by-products are not controlled. Therefore they may range widely,
e.g., prices of various aged cheeses vary as much as 100% from the common
types. Current prices of representative products are listed in Table 4.
39.       Because wholesale price is fixed at Cr$ 1.60 per liter, and the
producer price is fixed at Cr$ 1.35, the Cr$ 0.15 margin allows for little
more than a break-even operation at best for fresh milk distribution. The
dairy enterprise or cooperative must rely on sales of by-products for reason-
able returns on investment.
D. Models
40.       Indicative models are presented for the cheese and butter plant and
for a powdered milk and butter plant (Tables 5 and 6).

ANNEX 7
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Miscellaneous Processing
1.        The processing of traditional and non-traditional agricultural
products for human or animal consumption is a growing activity in Brazil.
Some of these agro-industries are traditional and going through a process
of expansion and/or modernization, while others are new enterprises in the
introduction stage. Another processing activity involves agricultural
products not intended for human or animal consumption, such as wool, hides
and non-edible oils, which are usually associated with some other agro-
industrial activity.
2.        The link between production of agricultural raw materials and
processing of agro-industrial products is composed of a number of necessary
elements for which the demand is already there, and expected to grow with
the increase of production and the expansion of agro-industry. These in-
clude such industries as mixing facilities for fertilizers and manufacturing
plants for small agricultural implements, sprayers for insecticides and
seed processing equipment, plus a variety of marketing facilities.
3.        In Agro-industries Credit Project I, a number of commodities were
considered to fall within the area of miscellaneous food and feed production,
but as sub-projects were presented for financing under the project, it was
found that there was considerable interest in products not intended for
direct human or animal consumption, agricultural inputs, or marketing
facilities. Therefore, to facilitate the treatment of these sub-sectors in
the proposed project, sub-groupings have been established to accommodate
fruit and vegetable processing for human consumption (fruit juices, canned
fruit, preserves, end canned and frozen vegetables); alternative sources of
protein (egg processing); non-edible products (wool, hides, non-edible oils,
soap, and vaccines); agricultural inputs (fertilizer mixers and light agri-
cultural tools and equipment); and marketing facilities (collection centers,
wholesale markets, fish marketing facilities). Some of these industries
and facilities are taken up separately in Appendices 7-1 through 7-3.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Fruit and Vegetable Processing
A. Production
1.        Orange juice dominated the first activities of the miscellaneous
group in Agro-industries Credit Project I. With 90% of the total production
destined for export, and with production increasing from 3,800 tons in 1964 to
118,400 tons in 1973, it constitutes an excellent source of foreign currency.
Prices have dropped in the last two years, but the export trade is still
sufficiently viable to justify new facilities being constructed in Minas
Gerais and Espirito Santo, away from the areas of traditional concentration
in Sao Paulo.
2.        The industrial capacity for orange juice processing has been esti-
mated at 140,000 tons per year, but it should go up by 3,000 tons per year
in 1976 and 10,000 tons per year in 1978 when the new facilities are con-
structed. On the basis of a joint study by FAO and the Secretaria de
Agricultura de Sao Paulo, production of concentrated orange juice is expected
to go from the 133,000 tons in 1973 to 310,000 tons in 1980 and 317,000 tons
in 1985, with a geometric annual growth of 7.5%.
3.        In 1972 orange juice was 15th in export value for agricultural
products, while West Germany, United States, the Netherlands, Canada and
Sweden were the main export markets. Other fruit juices, produced almost
exclusively for domestic consumption, reached only 80,400 tons in 1972,
but the domestic market has been growing since at reduced rates as non-
traditional use of these products continues to expand. A recent law requir-
ing that natural fruit juices be incorporated in any commercial drink pur-
porting to contain such juices (Ley do Suco) will constitute a boost to
fruit production.
4.        The commercial production of fruits and vegetables, which has
grown significantly in 1974, offers possibilities for marketing and process-
ing activities that would be particularly important to states such as Santa
Catarina, Espirito Santo and Minas Gerais, who have large expanses of land
that could be used best in production of tropical and semi-tropical fruits
such as citrus (oranges, tangerines, lemons), papaya, mango, persimmon,
peach, apple, pear, grape, and avocado. A profile of selected fruit is
presented in Table 1, showing the situation in 1973. Since then, however,
extensive plans have been made bv the State of. Espirito Santo to produce and
process pineapple, orange, guava, banana, jackfruit, cashew and papaya; Santa



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 2
Catarina is considering an extensive production of avocado in the littoral
micro-region of the state, with a view to oil and feed production; and Minas
Gerais has already initiated a 1,000-ha plantation of avocado for the same
purposes and is conducting serious studies for the production and processing
of peaches, nectarines, figs, pears, plums and pecans. Macademia nut and
the leechee nut are also being considered as possible export crops. Citrus
production in 1974 has increased 25% since 1973, with oranges comprising
some 90% of the total production, estimatedat 7L.2 million--boxes.  Orange
tree population in Sao Paulo in 1974 was about 75.6 million, including 33
million new non-bearing trees (Table 2).
5.        Shipments of orange juice concentrate during 1974 came to approxi-
mately 110,000 tons; that is, 9,000 tons less than in 1973, but 20,000 to
25,000 additional tons were exported in the first quarter of 1975, bringing
the total 1974 orange juice exports to 120,000 tons. Efforts to set minimum
export prices (MEP) in 1974 were not successful and transactions were made
at considerably lower prices than those called for.
6.        Citrus production is concentrated in the project area, which in
1973 produced 94% of the oranges, 93% of the tangerines, and 84% of the
lemons in the country.  This production, in general, was located in the
Southeast region1, particularly inx the States of Sao Paulo, Minas Gerais, and
Rio de Janeiro. The average yields for the country were 54,858 fruit/ha for
oranges, 110,613 fruit/ha for lemons, and 96,309 fruit/ha for tangerines, but
yields of 111,661 oranges/ha were obtained in Goias and 121,474 oranges/ha in
Santa Catarina; yields of 144,183, 135,000, 126,310 and 123,405 lemons/ha
were harvested in Goias, Guanabara, Sao Paulo and Santa Catarina, respectively.
Tangerines achieve yields higher than the national average in Guanabara
(187,807 fruit/ha), Mato Grosso (143,018 fruit/ha), and the Federal District
(127,143 fruit/ha).
7.        The South and Southeast regions also dominate the production of
other fruit, with the South producing 90% of the peaches, 81% of the pears,
and 76% of the grapes, while the Southeast produced 94% of the papaya, 59%
of the persimmon, 46% of the pineapple, and 56% of the apples in 1973. Aver-
age yields for 1973 were as follows:

(79,184 fruit/ha) and in the States of Parana, Sao Paulo, Minas Gerais, and
