Espirito Santo. Mango yields were extraordinarily high only in Goias
(74,455 fruit/ha), while better than average yields of peaches were obtained
in Goias, Rio de Janeiro, Rio Grande do Sul, and Mato Grosso.
8.        Grape production is largely concentrated in the South region, par-
ticularly in the State of Rio Grande do Sul, but production in Santa Catarina,
although limited in area has obtained yields of 9,740 kg/ha, which is higher
than average for the country (7,080 kg/ha). Experimental plantings in Mato
Grosso also showed yields of 9,000 kg/ha, indicating the potential for this
crop in the southern part of the state.
9.        Of the slightly less than half of the national production of pine-
apple produced in the project area, the Southeast region produces the larger
portion, particularly in the States of Minas Gerais, Sao Paulo and Espirito
Santo. Average yield for the country in 1973 was 9,589 fruit/ha, but the
average in the same year for the Southeast region was considerably higher
(11,037 fruit/ha), and in Espirito Santo it reached 17,457 fruit/ha.
10.       The above figures clearly indicate that, given the economic moti-
vation of a production-absorbing processing industries and modern agricul-
tural inputs, there is no constraint on increased production, either by
enlarging the planted area, or better still, by obtaining higher yields as
has already been achieved in some states.
11.       In 1973, production of avocado in the project area, which is grown
for consumption as a fruit but which can also be processed for production of
oil for the cosmetic industry and pulp for the feed industry, reached
347,135,000 fruits, representing 69% of the national production. Operations
were concentrated mostly in the States of Sao Paulo and Minas Gerais where
yields were only slightly above the national average of 30,141 fruit/ha. How-.
ever, small plantations of avocado in Mato Grosso achieved yields of 71,000
fruit/ha, showing what can be done. Avocado oil extracting industries are
being considered in Santa Catarina, Minas Gerais and Espirito Santo as a way
to utilize some of the extensive non-arable lands.
12.       Emphasis on higher yields can already be noted from production
data from Santa Catarina for early 1975 which show yields of 11,500 fruit/ha
in pineapple, 7,131 kg/ha of onions and 22,632 kg/ha of tomatoes. All of
these figures are considerably above the average for the same state in
1973. Rio Grande do Sul reports yields of 10,740 kg/ha in grapes, an increase
of 54% over 1960 production and the largest recorded in the last 14 years.
13.       It has not been possible to obtain production data on other fruit
suitable for processing, such as strawberries and figs. However, Rio Grande
do Sul reported a production of 2,500,000 kg of strawberries in 1972, with
yields of 2,500 tons/ha, and a total industrial production of 6 million 1-kg
cans of figs in 1973, with average yields of 4,800 tons/lha in the southern
part of the state. Estimates for the same area indicate a production of 500
to 800 tons of pears and 20,000 tons of peaches. Of these, peaches and
strawberries are produced mostly for export.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 4
14.       Vegetable production in the project area is difficult to assess,
since many of the crops are recorded statistically only once every five
years for the census, and the situation can, of course, change significantly
in that time, both quantitatively and spatially. Information was gathered,
however, on some of the major crops which are considered suitable for
industrial processing (Table 3), as well as on some others which show
potential.
15.       The project area produces 98% of the potatoes in the country,
with the Southern region contributing 58% and the greater part of the other
40% produced by the Southeast, particularly Minas Gerais and Sao Paulo.
Average national yields in 1973 were 7,086 kg/ha, but in the same year,
the South averaged 11,520 kg/ha and the Southeast 8,665 kg/ha.
16.       In 1973, 90% of the garlic and 86% of the onion produced in Brazil
was harvested in the project area. The South region contributed 48% of the
garlic and the Southeast 38%; 59% of the onions came from the South, mainly
from Rio Grande do Sul. Production data for 1974 indicates a slight increase
in the onion crop in Rio Grande do Sul, although yields remained at the same
level of 7,100 kg/ha. Garlic yields averaged 2,577 kg/ha nationally, with
the South showing 2,941 kg/ha as its average.
17.       Tomato production in the project area represented 70% of the
national production, with 60% in the Southeast, particularly in the States
of Sao Paulo. While national average yields for 1973 were recorded at
19,032 kg/ha due to lower yields in the North and Northeast regions, yields
of 34,785 kg/ha were average in the Center-West region, with 40,228 kg/ha
in the Federal District and 38,432 kg/ha in Goias. Santa Catarina, which in
1973 averaged 20,892 kg/ha went to 22,632 kg/ha in 1975. Since tomatoes can
be grown in practically all the states, the possibilities of increased pro-
duction in the project area is not so much a question of agricultural techno-
logy as one of marketing and agricultural policy.
18.       Manioc is grown all over Brazil and the project area grows half
of the national production. However, no attempt is made to differentiate
between industrial-type manioc suitable for starch production and edible
manioc, which is an important staple of the Brazilian diet, particularly
in the form of "farinha." Processing manioc in Brazil is limited mainly
to the manufacture of starch or farinha and its use in the feed industry,
either as a component of balanced feed or in the form of pellets. In some
of the Caribbean countries, mainly the Dominican Republic and Puerto Rico,
deep frozen manioc is being exported to the United States and sold for
domestic consumption.
19.       Average manioc yields in the country were 12,623 kg/ha in 1973 due
to lower yields in the North and Northeast, but in the same year, average
yield for the Center-West was 15,804 kg/ha, with 17,843 kg/ha in Mato Grosso;
14,071 for the South; and 14,056 kg/ha for the Southeast. Other vegetables



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 5
suitable for industrial processing are carrots, peas, green beans and aspara-
gus, but there is no statistical information on these or other truck crops
Rio Grande do Sul is known to be exporting frozen asparagus to Europe, but
again, no figures are available.
20.       Many of the vegetables commonly processed in most developed coun-
tries, such as sweet corn and lima beans are not traditionally consumed in the
domestic market of Brazil, probably because they are not a normal component
of the traditional diet. However, as the pressures of modern life increase
and the demand on time becomes greater, particularly in the large urban
centers, plus the inconvenience caused by problems of distribution because
of urban expansion, it is likely that there will be far less resistance
from the traditional "in natura" consuming public to processed foods.
21.       Reports from Rio Grande do Sul show an estimated potential produc-
tion in the southern part of the state of 8,000 to 10,000 tons of tomatoes,
2,500 to 3,000 tons of asparagus, 3,000 to 4,000 tons of greenbeans, 800 to
1,000 tons of small cucumbers and 300 to 500 tons of small onions for
pickles. Some 80% of the asparagus production is intended for export,
either frozen or canned. Some additional products which are indicated as
having agro-industrial potential in the Pelotas region of Rio Grande do
Sul include sour oranges, guava, carrots, cauliflowe-r, greeanbeans. and
small potatoes.
B. The Industry
Structure
22.       Brazil offers a tremendous growth potential in fruit and vegetable
processing. However, the relatively high cost of metal containers and
readily available household help that can shop for and prepare fresh fruits
and vegetables has slowed expansion. As these conditions change and indi-
vidual purchasing power increases, however, the market for processed items
will become more and more important.
23.       Being a huge country, Brazil has a variety of climatic conditions
that allow a number of different fruits and vegetables to be grown, ranging
from tropical to temperate zone products. Tropical fruits may be canned in
halves or slices, made into jams and in many cases into juices or nectars,
while temperate zone fruits such as apples, peaches and pears can be canned.
Specialty items like berries can be frozen, canned or made into jams, but
pineapples can be sold only as a fresh fruit since varieties grown in Brazil
are not suitable for canning, being less acid than is normally required for
processing. World consumption of canned pineapple products is quite large
and Brazil could possibly become a major producer if it decided to enter
this market by growing suitable varieties.
24.       In the vegetable area, tomato paste has become a world commodity,
and here, too, Brazil has the climatic conditions to grow tomatoes on a



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 6
large commercial scale for export. Many of the other temperate zone vege-
tables are classified as truck crops and are grown for the local fresh
market. Items such as peas, carrots, greenbeans, and asparagus may be
canned or frozen; brussel sprouts and okra are usually frozen; garlic, onions,
peas and carrots can be dehydrated for soups; and potatoes can be prepared
as dehydrated instant mashed or frozen french fries.
25.      WInT tho -praiet area,the canning and freezing industries are con-
centrated in the State of Sao Paulo, though in the last few years, new plants
have been established in Rio Grande do Sul to process temperate zone prod-
ucts. A large tomato paste cannery is being planned in Minas Gerais and
several fruit and vegetable plants are under consideration in Rio de Janeiro
and Espirito Santo.
26.       Fruit and vegetable processing industries are primarily privately
owned national companies, the major ones being Matarazzo, Cica, Praoletti,
Industrias Alimenticias Carlos de Britto, Conservas Alimenticias Hero,
Swift-Armour, and Agape. Many are going into multi-plant operations to take
advantage of the various growing regions in Brazil, which enables them to
manufacture a wide variety of products for national distribution. There
are also many smaller companies that manufacture specialty products such as
hearts of palm, Guayabada (a paste from guavas), jams, and similar items
that are consumed mainly in the producing regions since the limited produc-
tion volume does not warrant national distribution.
27.        At present, canning is the most important method of food preser-
vation though there are a few dehydration and freezing plants either in
operation now or being planned. Freezing offers the greatest future poten-
tial as it does not alter the fresh fruit or vegetable taste as much as
canning or dehydration. The main problem in freezing is in distribution,
which requires refrigerated trucks, cold storage facilities and frozen food
cabinets in the supermarkets. A lack of home freezers is also a deterrent.
Many Brazilian families do not have refrigerators and most of the units that
are available are small, with limited freezer capacity.
28.       Brazil is rapidly becoming one of the world's most important citrus
producers, ranking behind the United States and Japan in total production
although it is already the largest exporter of frozen orange concentrate.
At present, all of the plants are located in the State of Sao Paulo, where
75.6 million trees have been planted, 44% of which are not yet bearing. New
plants have been projected in Rio de Janeiro and Espirito Santo and additional
plants will be required in Sao Paulo to utilize the fruit from the newer
trees (Table 4).
29.       The average farm size is 40 ha but the newer groves are over 200
ha, which will permit a greater degree of mechanization. Most of the proc-
essors purchase fruit directly from the growers, competing with the fresh
market for their supplies. However, one company is now growing 29% of its
requirements and it is expected to produce 50% by 1977. As the processors



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-4
Page 7
pay the growers a premium price, one of the future developments may be for
the industry to go back to growing at least part of its annual requirements.
30.       The industry at present is composed of eight privately owned
corporations, two of which control 71% of the market, three with about 7% of
the market each, and three newer companies with a total production of 8% of
the market.
Plant Operations
31.       Canning, dehydration, and freezing plants are specialized opera-
tions, each requiring specific lines for the different fruits or vegetables
to be processed. In the developed countries, food processing industries
are based on the large-volume-low-profit-margin-per-item theory. However,
in the developing nations, this is not always possible and, as a result,
two types of plants are in general use. The first is a highly specialized
mass production plant that manufactures items such as concentrated orange
juice; tomato products, mainly paste; and pineapple. The second is a
generalized plant that produces a wide variety of fruits and vegetables
based on seasonal availability. Lines are set up to handle a special item
and are disassembled when the production period for the fruit or vegetable
is over. Equipment for filling, closing, sterilizing, packaging and hand-
ling in the warehouse can be used for many common products. Most of the
plants in Brazil are of the latter type.
32.       The larger fruit and vegetable processing plants are usually
modern and well equipped, though quality control standards and plant sani-
tation may be less stringent than in Western Europe and the United States.
Since the smaller plants lack technically qualified personnel in these
areas, they suffer a greater amount of spoilage or losses due to improper
production methods.
33.       In order to control production and product costs, fruit and vege-
table processing plants should be integrated back into agriculture. Varieties
should be selected to meet climatic conditions in the growing area and be of
the type that will produce a uniform quality, high yielding crop; farmers
should work with the processing plant under a supply agreement as it is im-
possible for companies to compete with the fresh market for raw material; and
plants should have an agronomist or agricultural specialist to assist the
farmers during the growing season to insure that crop damage due to diseases,
insects, or other factors are kept to a minimum and to coordinate harvesting
so that the plant will receive an even flow of raw material to be processed.
34.       The frozen orange concentrate industry is a highly mechanized
large-scale continuous operation that normally runs 24 hours a day during
the season. Fruit is usually brought to the plant in bulk trucks that
dump their contents onto inspection belts so that defective fruit can be
removed. Conveyors elevate the oranges into storage bins where they are
stored according to their brix content. As fruit is needed for production,



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 8
oranges from one or more bins may be mixed to standardize the sugar content
of the juice. The fruit is first carefully washed to remove any pesticides
or insecticides that could contaminate the juice, graded for size, and
then conveyed to the extractors which expel the juice from the fruit and
essential oil from the skins. The juice is pasteurized, concentrated,
cut back by adding fresh juice to the concentrate, put into containers that
go through tunnel freezers and then are held in cold storage until needed
by the export market.
35.       The essential oil from the skins is centrifuged to remove the oil
from the scurry in which it is transported, after which it is stored in a
cool area to allow any remaining solids to settle, decanted and put into
containers for shipment. The waste material such as pulp and skins can be
sold as animal feed in either wet or dried form, depending on market require-
ments.
36.       Eight plants in Sao Paulo are reportedly producing 80% of the
orange juice in Brazil, which would indicate that about 20% of total orange
juice production goes to the home market for soft drinks and the like. How-
ever, that product differs from the frozen concentrate of the Sao Paulo
industry in that processing, quality standards, specifications are much less
vigorous than for the concentrate. Moreover, much of the orange juice for
local consumption may be produced in multi-juice plants.
37.       Juice oranges are grown especially for concentrate purposes and
they differ slightly from the table variety. Table oranges can be processed,
but yield drops substantially. Also, blending problems would arise if such
varieties were used and blended to meet customer specifications in an already
subtle and delicate process. Moreover, the fresh orange market is more
lucrative than the concentrate fruit market. There apparently are large
supplies of tangerines available for crushing but concentration plants do
not like to handle them because they cause problems in the extraction
process due to their smaller size and require time-consuming machinery
adjustments. Down-time and labor costs therefore rise sharply, rendering
tangerine processing less economic.
38.       The processors, however, are obliged to accept a certain volume
of tangerines from the growers and up to 5% of tangerine juice is allowable
by law as a blending ingredient. Such blending is beneficial as it improves
coloring and imparts a fuller taste to the orange juice, and since tangerine
juice always has been regarded as somewhat of a slow mover on the market,
blending is considered an excellent outlet for part of the juice tangerine
supply.
39.       Rejects from the fresh fruit market are generally discarded, as
are rejects from the concentration plants. The reason for this apparently
wasteful practice is that uniform size is important to the proper function-
ing of the extractor machinery. Once the extractors are set up for certain
sizes of oranges, a rigorous grading and screening process must sort out



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 9
the outsizes, particularly the smaller ones. These are usually discarded
because (a) the volume of such fruit does not justify the down-time expense
necessary to adjust the machinery for an extra run, and (b) returns from
sales of such rejects for consumption would be so small as to render too
costly the handling, regrading and distribution to markets. Thus, outright
disposal of rejected fruit is most economical.
40.       Although--TROPISUC-and other processors operate mainly on long-term
supplier contracts with growers, the ideal mode of operation for an enter-
prise would be to produce a substantial portion of its supply of juice
oranges and to contract the remainder. In fact, TROPISUC is planting 20,000
ha in juice and table oranges, the latter to add stability to the concentrate
plant by providing an additional source of income when the fresh fruit
market is buoyant. The grading, washing, cleaning and selection machinery
can be utilized for the table oranges, thus providing a larger base on which
to charge off fixed costs.
41.       The management of the orange juice industry evidently is very alert.
Managements work closely with their import customers and import agents.
Customer blending specifications are strictly adhered to, with the output
constantly subjected to a series of tests. One reason for this is that the
quality varies among growers according to soil, growing conditions and
variety of the juice orange. According to how they test out, batches of
juice are held in a number of refrigerated tanks until an export order is
received. Concentrate is then drawn from the tanks as called for to meet
blend requirements.
Equipment
42.       Most of the machinery for processing citrus must be imported, as
it is specialized equipment that cannot be locally made because of the
limited demand. Stainless steel tanks, conveyors, and washing and grading
equipment all can be obtained locally, but since the plants are usually
designed abroad, much of this machinery is furnished on a turn-key basis.
43.       Brazil does not manufacture high-speed food processing equipment,
but it can supply cold storage machinery, stainless steel tanks, conveyors,
cookers and retorts for sterilization. Argentina is a major supplier for
much of the processing machinery. Packaging equipment is also imported as
is specialized machinery for tomato paste, citrus, pineapple, and IQF
(individually quick freezing) equipment.
44.       The machinery for orange juice extraction is highly specialized
and, due to the development and concentration of this type of operation
in the US, two companies dominate this machinery market -- FMC Corporation
and Brown Company. FMC has a virtual monopoly in Brazil because it offers
good service facilities in Sao Paulo. Brazilian processors would not con-
sider using other machinery because of the servicing factor.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 10
45.       All extractor machinery is leased from FMC and design, layout and
operational planning revolve around it. Leases are for one year, renewable
at the option of the company. However, since it is of mutual interest to
maintain the installations, cancellation by FMC is unheard of. Rentals are
raised from year-to-year in discretionary amounts, depending largely on the
market situation. Rentals did not go up at the latest renewal (1974/75)
because of the difficult export situation.
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
46.       In Brazil, an abundance of reasonably priced fresh fruits and
vegetables are still to be found in the large urban centers. Therefore,
the basic conditions for a massive shift in marketing patterns toward
processed fruits and vegetables are still in formation. This is particularly
true of frozen products and juice concentrates and of certain dehydrated
vegetables. New developments in the coming years undoubtedly will favor
the broadening of high-volume marketing of processed foods. Major factors
are likely to be:
(a) gradually increasing transport and handling costs will cause
a shift in the ratio fresh/processed fruit and vegetable price
relationships due to the more economic distribution charac-
teristics of packaged foods, thus encouraging consumer demand
for the latter;
(b) the broadening network of supermarket chains - which now
handle 60% of the grocery trade in Rio de Janeiro and 40%
of the Sao Paulo market - is providing the basic infra-
structure for modern high-volume, mass merchandising of
frozen and packaged foods. As unit costs decline for food
processing due to increasing volume and concentrated dis-
tribution, trends in relative prices should favor processed
foods;
tc) changing life styles of the urban Brazilian, particularly
the working woman, will reinforce preferences for processed
foods that require less time and effort in preparation.
They will also have less time to devote to frequent shopping;
and
(d) rising income levels will permit storing of food in larger
quantities.
This will involve, in effect, an extension of the modern marketing infrastruc-
ture into the home in the form of larger capacity refrigerator-freezers.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 11
47.       Because of the climatic conditions prevailing in Brazil, most local
markets away from the major urban centers are adequately served by fresh
regional production. This high-cost cross-country handling, which would
raise fruit and vegetable prices to inordinate levels that smaller local
markets could not sustain, is not an essential factor in the domestic
economy.
48.       Fruit and vegetable processing at-the present time would seem to
have less priority from an overall consumption point of view than, say, beef
and poultry processing and the dairy industry.
49.       An important change in regulations governing the manufacture of
fruit juices, setting specific mimimum contents of natural juices in products
labelled as fruit juice, will have the effect of raising substantially the
volume going to domestic consumption. However, this increase in demand
should be adequately handled by the existing juice and soft drink industry
that services public establishments such as bars, sandwich stands, and
restaurants.
Export Market
50.       At this point, there would appear to be great potential for devel-
opment of fruit and vegetable processing industries directed mainly toward
the export market. Certain products for which foreign markets already
have been developed, or traditional products that can be grown in Brazil
could be promoted and marketed. Notable among these are several juices other
than frozen orange juice, viz. grape juice, pineapple juice and maracuja.
51.       Because the economics of fresh fruit distribution allow for a wide
variety of fruits for consumption in the local market, there has been little
incentive to promote frozen concentrate there. The main export destinations
are Germany, United States, the Netherlands, Canada and Sweden (Table 5).
The U.S. trade is based on equivalent re-exports to Japan or to other markets,
in accordance with U.S. regulations limiting citrus imports. The Netherlands
is considered an entrepot, from which shipments of Brazilian concentrate are
sent to various parts of the Continent. Total exports in recent years are
summarized below:
('000 tons)          (US$'000)       (US$/ton)

1969              23,245               10,910           469
1970              33,468               14,736           440
1971              77,334               35,859           464
1972              87,156               41,499           476
1973             120,989               63,622           526
1974             108,000               60,480           560

Source:   CACEX.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 12
The preliminary four-month total for 1975 compares favorably with the cor-
responding 1974 figure of 21,000 tons. Although that is not a clear indica-
tion of basic trends because sharp periodic fluctuations in shipments are
always a possibility, it does lend an encouraging tone to the current situation.
Projections indicate a doubling of orange juice exports within 8 years (Table 6).
52.       The most dynamic trade, aside from the orange juice concentrate,
has been in grape juice, which was first exported in 1971. From the 9.2 tons
exported that year, volume has increased to reach about 4,400 tons in 1974
(Table 7) for a total export value estimated at US$3.4 million. Unit value
has gone from US$348 per ton to US$766 per ton in that period. Shipments
have been mainly to the US, but Germany and Japan are also promising markets.
53.       Maracuja juice shipments are on a smaller scale, but they grew
from 72 tons in 1970 to more than 1,300 tons in 1974 (Table 8), valued at
US$10 million. South Africa, Sweden and the U.S. are the principal markets,
with the Netherlands and Germany also taking substantial shares. Unit
value rose from US$493 per ton in 1970 to US$784 per ton in 1974 (Table 7).
54.       Pineapple juice exports have expanded dramatically, going from 169
tons in 1970 to over 1,700 tons in 1974, valued at about US$78,000. Unit
values have increased from US$355 per ton to US$454 per ton. The US and
Argentina are the main destinations, but Portugal, Germany and France are
also good markets (Table 7).
55.       Only oranges are shipped in volume as fresh fruit, but they are
far over shadowed by orange juice concentrate as an export item. East
Germany, the Netherlands, and the United Kingdom are the main customers,
although Iran became a sizeable market in 1974. Orange shipments have not
followed a definite trend, averaging about 50,000 tons in the 1970-74
period. Values, however, have risen from US$3.4 million to an estimated
US$8.7 million over that period, reflecting higher unit values, which have
gone from 1JS$67 per ton to US$147 per ton (Table 8).
56.       There is scope for exports of specialty items such as asparagus,
palmito (already being shipped), brussels sprouts, okra, broccoli, various
specialty beans, guava (as paste or preserves), and various berries, most
of which can be canned or frozen. Tomato products are probably the largest
single vegetable product traded and the market is growing despite the
numerous countries taking a share of it.
57.       In the promotion of large-scale exports of fruits and vegetables,
uniformity of taste, appearance and quality are the essential elements to
attain. In that respect, it is of utmost importance to coordinate the
production and processing activities closely so that the most desirable
product is available to work with. In export-oriented agro-industries of
this type, some form of vertical integration is the most likely formula for
a successful enterprise.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-1
Page 13
Prices
58.       Shipments of orange juice were down substantially in 1974/75 due to
lagging demand in Europe. About 3,000 tons of a 10,000-ton output were in
storage at TROPISUC, in Campinas in May 1975. However, some renewed interest
was reported by the firm's agents in the Netherlands and Germany and prospects
appeared good that the stocks would be disposed of by August or September of
this year before the new crop comes in for crushing. Current export values
were US$350 per ton for oil. Brazilian exporters are confident that they can
dispose of projected increases in plant output because they can underprice
other exporting countries. The US$350 per ton price quoted by TROPISUC
management is sharply lower than the US$450 level quoted by other sources in
early 1975.

BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Egg Processing
A. Production
1.        Egg production in Brazil is not as carefully recorded as other
agricultural products. Statistics on poultry do not even separate chicken
raised for meat from those raised as egg layers, nor had there been an effort
until recently to develop specific laying breeds. Except for the relatively
more developed states the general trend is still to use double-purpose breeds
such as the New Hampshire, Rhode Island Reds or a cross-breed of these.
2.        Egg production in 1973 reached 461,430,000 in the project area
(Table 1) and the estimated production for 1974 was 500 million dozen eggs.
Assuming a 10% annual growth of GDP, of the Instituto Paulista de Economia
Agricola, expectations (IPEA) are that egg production will grow at a rate of
7.4% annually. Production of a specialized egg is also expected to start in
1975 -- genic-free eggs for the use in the preparation of human and animal
vaccines, but this of course is an activity limited in volume.
3.        Commercial production of eggs is concentrated in the center-south
of Brazil, in the Sao Paulo - northern Parana - southern Minas Gerais poultry
belt.  Output is estimated at 500 million dozen annually, said to be 97% of
total national production, which places total commercial egg output in Brazil
at approximately 515 million dozen. Although production is concentrated geo-
graphically, it is logical from the standpoint of climatic conditions and
availability of feedstuff being based generally in the grain belts. Egg pro-
duction in 1973 in the project area is given in Table 1. It is the tremendous
distances to markets that pose the question of expansion to other areas.
Development of virgin areas in upper Mato Grosso and Goias for soybean and
cereal production should encourage poultry enterprises to locate there where
they will have a significant advantage in feed transport costs. That margin
may be sufficient to provide the necessary incentive.
4.        Because of the predominating double-purpose orientation of egg pro-
ducers and uncertainties of the market, the emphasis on meat or egg produc-
tion changes from time to time. In 1973, birds for meat showed a slight in-
crease over birds for eggs (29% increase and 23% decrease, respectively).
However, probably because of increases in production costs and smaller demand
than expected, the egg-layers showed a slight gain over the broilers in 1974
(Table 2).



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-2
Page 2
5.        A review of the distribution of layers and broilers during the
1969/75 period confirms the flexibility of producers to adjust to changes in
market conditions and the fact that Sao Paulo accounts for about 90 to 95% of
the production of the country allows conclusions on production in that state
to be applied to the industry as a whole. Annual production of layers and
broilers in the State of Sao Paulo is given below:
Year           White Eggs          Brown Eggs         Broilers
1969           565,900               135,700          2,470,700
1970           670,281               118,900          2,545,355
1971           606,650               161,763          3,114,463
1972           472,654               173,149          3,905,843
1973           516,489               115,644          3,907,234
1974           423,762               101,263          5,280,646
1975 /1         18,400                16,169            380,162
/1   January 1975.
Source: Revista Avicultura Industrial.
6.        Typical of a large egg-producing operation is the cooperative COTIA,
which operates in western Sao Paulo, northern Parana and southern Minas
Gerais. The cooperative has some 500 members, each producing an average
500 cases of 30 dozen eggs per month, bringing total production to an esti-
mated 90 million dozen eggs per year. The area of operation extends some
600 to 1,000 km from the central collection point of the cooperative into the
neighboring states. Its industrial sales amount to approximately 20% of
total sales, representing a volume of 100 million dozen or 3.3 million cases,
per year.
B. The Industry
7.        There is substantial potential for expansion of egg processing
plants in Brazil based on the Sun-Egg model, a plant to be established in
the State of Sao Paulo with financing from Agro-industries Credit Project I.
This plant has an input of 150,000 eggs per day, or 416.7 cases per day,
which in 270 working days result in 112,509 cases per year.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-2
Page 3
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
8.        Consumption of eggs in Brazil follows the geographic patterns of
production and efficient rapid transport, i.e., eggs are consumed mainly in
the poultry belt and in the large urban centers where the volume distributed
results in low unit transport costs. An estimate of 5.1 kg per capita con-
sumption of eggs undoubtedly applies only to such areas because the high
price in other parts of the country -- where transport costs can exceed the
value of the eggs themselves -- is prohibitive for the general population.
In any case commercial egg production, estimated at 500 million plus dozen,
allows for only about 3.5 kg per capita on the average, since a dozen eggs
weigh no more than 0.65 to 0.7 kg. 1/
9.        Members of COTIA market their eggs through the cooperative, one of
the largest egg marketing organizations in Brazil. Unrefrigerated COTIA
trucks travel at night from collection centers to Sao Paulo (JAGUARE).
Typically, a trip such as from Londrina (PR) to Sao Paulo, would be 570 km and
the tariff Cr$ 1,500. The average load is 500 cases so cost would be Cr$ 300
per case, or Cr$ 0.1 per dozen. Trucking to COTIA is charged to the producer,
and the cost is deducted at time of payment. At an average value of, say,
Cr$ 3.30 per dozen, transport cost for the producer amounts to 9% of his
revenue.
10.       On deliveries, overall transport costs average Cr$ 1.00 per km per
truck with 500 cases. The eggs are shipped across the country, the farthest
point being Manaus. Trucks go as far as Porto Velho in Rondonia and there
boats take over and carry the eggs up to Manaus -- a distance over 3,500 km
from Sao Paulo. The shipment is unrefrigerated, but there is practically no
spoilage. Other distant markets are Belem, Bahia and other localities in
the Amazon region. Although transport costs to distant markets are substan-
tial, the prices obtained compensate for them.
11.       Producers receive the bulk of the value of the product under the
cooperative system and presumably private intermediaries meet the coopera-
tive's returns at the producer level. Some of the very large producers,
however, apparently make their own marketing arrangements since dealing
directly with distributor or retail organizations could save overhead charges
that the cooperative would have to deduct. Also large producers may be in a
position to supply distant centers and gain on whatever transport and hand-
ling savings they can achieve. The table below summarizes the cost structure 2/
of the egg market in May 1975.
1/   Based on 100 million population and five dozen eggs per bird per year.
2/   COTIA, calculation based on extra grade.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-2
Page 4
Producer price                  Cr$ 3.50 per dozen
Packing handling                Cr$ 0.50 per dozen
Wholesale commission (10%)    Cr$ 0.40 per dozen
Wholesale price                 Cr$ 4.40 per dozen
Retail margin (15%)            Cr$ 0.66 per dozen
Retail price                    Cr$ 5.06 per dozen
Actual Retail price in Porto Alegre 5.00 - 7.00 per dozen
Bahia-Manaus + 12.00 per dozen
12.       The market for eggs is roughly 80% direct consumption sales and 20%
industrial demand -- bakeries, confectioners, and other food industries. All
the trade has been in fresh eggs so far, except for a negligible quantity of
processed imports. However, a processed egg industry is likely to develop
rapidly to meet industrial demand, following the pattern in other industrial-
ized countries where egg-cracking and freezing plants have been in operation
for four decades or more. The supply of bulk. egg yolk and albumen separately
in either frozen or liquid chilled form has long been known to be more econo-
mical than transporting and handling eggs since breakage and spoilage are
virtually eliminated. A changeover to handle bulk frozen yolk and albumen for
the industrial market would require about 30 plants in the project area with
capacity similar to that of the Sun-Egg plant in Sao Paulo.
13.       In 1974, an estimated 5.3 million broilers were produced in the
State of Sao Paulo and 0.5 million laying chicken. In that state alone,
therefore, some 0.5 million dozen eggs were utilized for new stock 1/. The
national commercial hatching requirement can be estimated at roughly 491,000
dozen for broilers and 46,000 dozen for laying stock. That production is in
addition to the supply of eggs for human consumption. Imports of fertilized
eggs of about 20,000 dozen, mainly for breeding stock, were registered in 1974.
That trade, of course, will continue as it is a prime requisite for constantly
improving the breeding stock.
Prices
14.       Price structure follows the size scale.  There is minor fluctuation
between the grades, reflecting largely seasonal availabilities. At mid-May
1975, average wholesale quotations were as follows:
1/   Excluding unhatched eggs which could amount to an undetermined but im-
portant percentage.

Grade (Type)   1          Cr$ 3.50 per dozen
2         Cr$ 3.40 per dozen
3         Cr$ 3.30 per dozen
4         Cr$ 3.00 per dozen
5         Cr$ 2.30 per dozen
15.       Grading and selection generally are done by the producer.  At the
farm level, rejects run about 5% to 7%. These edible rejects usually are
sold on the farm or in the immediate locality by the producer. Although the
total volume of rejects may be large, it is not economical to collect and
handle them, because the quantities at the individual producer level are too
small, and the value of the product is too low to cover extra handling costs
and to yield a satisfactory return. At the larger center in Jaguare, rejects
and losses run about 0.05% of turnover, a low rate indeed. However, on the
turnover of COTIA at Jaguare with about 90 million dozen per year, the per-
centage works out to 45,000 dozen.
16.       Grading according to size is based on the following system of
weights.
No. of Grams         Percent
Distribution
Type 1 (Extra)   60 plus               15
Type 2 (Extra)   55 - 60               45
Type 3            50 - 55              35
Type 4            45 - 50               4
Type 5            up to 45              1
Slight premiums for all grades may be obtained for brown eggs in rural areas,
and the contrary may be true in urban areas. These consumer preferences are
similar to the pattern in the US. The difference in taste is traditional
from earlier periods when brown eggs were heavier and contained richer col-
ored yolks. However, the new breeds of white-egg laying chicken produce
equivalent size and coloring.
17.       Egg prices show a strong seasonal pattern that characterizes all
markets around the world. In Brazil, quotations are usually registered in
April, and the low point usually occurs around October. Seasonal highs and
lows in 1974 were as follows:

BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Hides, Skins, and Wool Processing
A. Production
1.        A study made in relation to a project for the construction of a
tannery in the State of Sao Paulo (SCARBORD - Couros Finos S.A.) indicates
a domestic demand for tanned cattle hides of 11.8 million in 1975, and of
23 million in 1984, assuming that the shoe and leather goods industries will
grow at the rates of 9% and 3%, respectively. An estimated 10.2 million head
for slaughter in 1975 (App. 4-1) would indicate a deficit of 1.6 million hides
in 1975, but such a situation is not supported by the stock of hides presently
held by various slaughterhouses.
2.        Hide and skin exports, which in 1969 had reached 76,679 tons, had
dwindled in 1974 to 6,313 for the January through October period, reflecting
the increasing trend to keep production for the domestic market, regardless
of the apparent export potential to Europe, indicated by a 53% annual growth
of tanned hide exports in the 1963-72 period. A resolution of the National
Foreign Trade Council (CONCEX), prohibited the export of raw hides and skins
as well as wet chrome-tanned hides with a view to regulating the domestic
supply of raw materials to tanneries.
3.        Since ox-hides are normally a by-product of slaughterhouses and
meat packing plants, the supply of raw materials is directly related to beef
production. Likewise, expansion of the hide-processing industry is largely
a question of market and expansion of the beef-processing industry. With a
projected beef production of 2,862,000 tons in 1980 (Appendix 4-1) and an
average carcass yield of 220 kg per head, an estimated 13 million ox-hides in
1980 seems reasonable.
4.        Instituto Brasileiro de Geografia e Estatistica (IBGE) statistics
show a 1973 sheep population in Brazil of 17,949,362 head, of which the pro-
ject area carried 77%, concentrated largely (95%) in the State of Rio Grande
do Sul. Other sources estimate the sheep population at 26 million head in
that year and at 26.5 million in 1974, but no statistical data are offered
to substantiate these considerably larger figures. Of the 13,789,746 sheep
recorded in 1973 for the project area, 87% were sheared, producing a reported
34,234 tons of wool (Table 1), considerably below the 36,500 tons estimated
for the year by other sources.
5.        Sheep ranching is carried out mostly in Rio Grande do Sul, where
sheep are usually raised in combination with cattle. Since returns from



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-3
Page 2
beef production in recent years have been higher than those from sheep and
wool, and available resources from private enterprise and Government have
been channeled mainly to beef, no significant expansion is expected in the
wool industry, particularly since prices of wool went down about 5% to 10%
in 1974. Some wool production is also carried out in Parana, Santa Catarina
and southern IMinas Gerais, but, together, they accounted for only about 2%
of total production in 1974.
6.        By 1973, the Departamento Nacional de Inspecao de Produtos de
Origem Animal (DIPAO) inspected 123,650 tons of hides (Table 2) while an
equal number probably went uninspected, bringing up total production to
234,903 tons (Table 3). Other hides which are processed in Brazil include
sheep, goat, nonato (unborn cow), and rabbit but these are produced in con-
siderably lesser amounts than ox-hides. Total sheep skins processed in 1970
were 3,306 tons, but, in 1973, inspected production reached only 689 tons.
In 1970, total goat skins were 1,643 tons, while nonato production was 20
tons and rabbit was 4 tons. In 1973 inspected production of the same skins
was 14.8 tons for goat skins; 114.3 for nonato, and there is no record for
rabbit skins (Table 4).
B. The Industry
Structure
7.        There are approximately 545 tanneries in Brazil, concentrated in
the southeastern and south regions, which produce more than 80% of the total
tanned hides in the country. Quality of production varies considerably,
from sophisticated leather with aniline dyes to the more rustic leathers, or
to chamois-colored hides with or without varnish and shoe leather of the
finest quality.
8.        The processing capacity of the national industry is about 10 million
hides per year, and production of raw material to supply the industry seems
assured by the expansion of the beef processing industry and the resolution
of the National Foreign Trade Council (CONCEX), which prohibits the export of
raw, wet or chromium-tanned hides.
9.        The shoe industry is perhaps the dominating factor in the leather
market since it absorbs 90% of the total production. In 1974 the demand of
the shoe industry amounted to 10,450,000 hides, with a deficit of 450,000
units which were beyond the capacity of the industry.
10.       Recent trends in the tanning industry show a reduction in the number
of tanneries, with an increase in the amount of tanned hides produced; the
grouping of small tanneries to form larger more efficient units; expansion
of existing plants and improvement and modernization of their lay-out and
equipment; adoption of the more advanced processes used in the international



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-3
Page 3
industry; modernization of the administrative aspects in industries which
were traditionally family style; and aggressive promotion in the domestic
and export markets with the introduction of new, higher-quality products.
Plant Operations
11.       Operations of a tannery can be summarized in a series of steps in-
cluding the reception of raw hides, cutting out the unusable portions, skin-
ning and scalping, tanning by vegetable or chemical products, drying, cutting
the leather into pieces, controlling thickness, second tanning, dyeing, dry-
ing and re-humidifying, stretching out of pieces, treating with special resins,
drying, removing upper layer, manual sorting of leathers, treating with resins
and pigments, applying the nitrocellulose emulsion treatment, printing and
final sealing. Leather pieces are subsequently packed and shipped to clients.
The more modern tanneries use the chrome tanning process which is faster than
the process using vegetable products and is apparently more versatile and
economical.
12.       Secondary materials utilized in the processing of raw hides are
salt, lime and various chemicals, including sulphuric acid, formic acid, cal-
cium formiate, ammonium sulphate, sodium bicarbonate, sodium sulphite, sodium
sulphate and chrome oxide.  Sub-products of-the tanning process are the
scalping residues and tallow, sold in the domestic market for various indus-
trial purposes.
Equipment
13.       Equipment used in the tanning industry include boilers, compressors,
wood pallets and trestles, weighing scales, lathes, machines for hide tanning
and for hide re-tanning, pressure cut-down stations with unit separator sets,
meat-removing machines, hydraulic dryers, hydraulic drying/dripping machines,
hide-splitting machines, hide-thinning machines, porcelain dryers, continuous
flow production lines for the removal of hides' surface layer and dust, hide
washing and humidity content control machines, hide edge drying machines,
coating machines, special tannery laboratory equipment, hide measuring elec-
tronic machines, hide-surface homogenizing machines and dust-removing systems,
hydraulic stamping presses, smoothening presses, hide-stretching vibratory
machines and a variety of workshop and standard industrial equipment.
14.       Some of the above equipment is manufactured in Brazil with satis-
factory quality, but the more sophisticated equipment will, in most cases,
require imports from the United States or European manufacturers. Produc-
tion of the higher quality leathers, in particular, will require modern
machinery which is not yet manufactured in the country.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-3
Page 4
C.  Marketin
Domestic Market
15.       Two major concentrations of the tanning industry have developed
close to their main markets: one is in the area around the city of Franca,
in the State of Sao Paulo where the men's shoe industry predominates, and
the other is in the Vale dos Sirios, in Rio Grande do Sul, where the women's
shoe industry is centered in the city of Nova Hamburgo. Historically, the
shoe industry has absorbed the bulk of the output of the tanneries. In the
1970/72 period, it took two-thirds of the hides processed (Table 5), other
leather industries took 17%, and the remainder was shipped abroad. The
domestic market, of course, includes in its clientele export-oriented enter-
prises producing various leather items.
16.       Aggregate domestic demand rose from 4.8 million hides in 1966 to 7.8
million hides in 1972, a gain of 62%. The growth rate of the herd for that
period was 8.4%. Tanning activities here apparently expanded recently at an
accelerated pace, judging by demand estimates for 1975 which range up to 11.8
million hides. Exports of semi-finished hides that year are likely to approxi-
mate 200,000. Due to world market conditions 1/, such an off-take will leave
a considerable surplus of hides in beef processing or tanning industries'
inventories, because an output of 2.3 million tons of beef will require the
slaughter of approximately 14 million head of cattle.
Export Market
17.       Exports of hides expanded four and a half times in the 1966/72 period,
from 0.5 million to 2.2 million pieces. In 1973, in conjunction with policy to
encourage leather industries and to assure the shoe industry adequate supplies
of raw material, the Government prohibited export of raw and semi-finished hides
(by resolution of the National Foreign Trade Council, (CONCEX). The intent was
two-fold: (a) to implement the aforementioned policies, and (b) to export
finished hides with greater value added content. As a result of that decree,
and also because of world market conditions, hide exports have dropped below
levels of a decade earlier. Prior to Government action, the trend to export
tanned hides and leather manufactures was already clearly in evidence, as
shown below:
1/   Described in Annex 2, Beef Processing.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-3
Page 5
(US$'000)                 (Percent)
1970   1971   1972        1970   1971   1972
Hides                     25.5   20.0   24.1         50.9   30.2   18.9
Tanned hides              15.5   14.4   40.9         31.0   21.6   31.6

Source: CACEX.
18.       Under the changed export conditions, the better-equipped, modern
tanneries have been rapidly expanding export trade in finished leathers to
Europe on a custom order basis, thus competing directly with established
European tanneries. Subsidiaries of the major European chemical companies
are producing locally tanning, coloring and finishing materials equal to
their latest production in Europe. Thus, BraWilian enterprises that have
the necessary capacity can obtain the n&terials with which to produce
high-grade finished leathers, permitting them to compete in the European
market. The larger tanneries have also imported the latest leather-working
machinery. The major shortcoming in the industry is the quality of the
hides, which is not up to the standard of Argentine or U.S. material. However,
steady improvement is being made with the introduction of modern techniques
in cattle husbandry. A small leather import trade still persists, amounting
to about US$1 million per year, mainly from Argentina. This trade consists
of specialty leathers which long-established tanneries in that country have
traditionally produced.
19.       Because of the dominant role of the shoe industry in the leather
market in Brazil, conditions, particularly affecting the export-oriented
sector, have an important bearing on the tannery industry. Exports of shoes
to the U.S. have spiralled upward at a phenomenal rate, and Brazil was able
to undersell traditional leather shoe industries in Italy and Spain. In 1973
Brazilian shoe exporters had preempted 13.5% of the US import market in terms
of volume and 11.1% in terms of value. Although at that point, the Brazilian
share of the one-billion pair per year US market was less than 2%, the domestic
shoe industry in the U.S. reacted with alarm to the sharply rising influx.
The U.S. Government therefore imposed compensatory tariffs on shoe imports
from Brazil in 1974 to partially offset the tax and export incentives accorded
the manufacturers in Brazil, already amounting to as much as 30% of the "normal"
cost of production. Brazil, nevertheless, has been able to meet European
competition in the U.S. market because of the formidable increases in labor
costs on the Continent. The slower growth in demand for leather by the shoe
industry now reflects a smaller market due to the econoric sloudoum in the
U.S. and in Europe.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-3
Page 6
20.       The experience with the U.S. tariff in 1974 has encouraged Brazilian
producers to lessen their dependence on the U.S. market by diversifying their
shipments to other markets. In 1972, exports of 13.6 million pairs, valued at
US$46.9 million, to the United States, constituted 87% of volume and 86% of
value of the total exports of the industry.
21.       Sales of hides and leather, particularly to European countries, are
usually made through brokers and agents in the importing countries, although
major tanneries that are competitive with the better grade European products
have direct representation in principal markets. Executives of such enterprises
visit the main markets in order to stay up to date on the latest developments in
production and on trends in customer requirements. The semi-annual Foire Inter-
national du Cuir, held in Paris and attended by some Brazilian producers, is in
fact "de rigueur" for tanneries that are competing in international trade of
finished leathers. Tanneries which cannot as yet compete in finished leathers,
particularly the smaller tanneries, usually conduct their trade through export
brokers and agents in the large centers such as Porto Alegre and Sao Paulo.
22.       In recent years, government policy has encouraged development of
leather consuming industries, mainly directed toward external markets. Leather
furniture, women's handbags and leather clothing manufacturers have proliferated
in collaboration with foreign financial participation or assistance and styling
know-how. As a result, the local market for higher grade leather has expanded
considerably. These industries should grow at a faster pace in the future as
Brazilian products gain acceptance in foreign markets. The rate of growth of
the shoe industry is problematic as Brazilian exporters seek to penetrate new
markets with perhaps different styling requirements. Domestic demand can be
expected to rise more rapidly in the coming years as rising income levels pro-
vide a greater degree of discretionary purchasing power for a larger proportion
of the population. Postulating that the indicated longer term growth of 9.3%
per annum mainfested over the 1966/75 period would persist through the present
decade, aggregate demand for hides would approximate 18.4 million tons by
1980, a total that is somewhat greater than estimated availabilities of 17.3
million hides based on the projected production of 2,862 tons of beef in the
same year (Annex 4, Appendix 4-1). A rather substantial deficit of 1.1 million
hides would therefore occur based on the foregoing assumptions.
23.       The production of wool is a minor activity in the agricultural sector
of Brazil. In 1973, output amounted to 34,234 tons (Table 5), which would indi-
cate availabilities of 0.34 kg per capita.
24.       Imports of wool and related fibers totaled 1,025 tons in 1971 and
1,557 tons in 1972. Assuming that the latter level prevailed in 1973, per
capita availabilities of wool would reach only 0.35 kg.
25.       Various other synthetic and natural fibers and textiles are imported
to supplement the requirements for apparel, household and other uses. Such
imports totaled about US$74 million in 1972, including USS3 million value of
wool imports.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 7-3
Page 7
26.       There is, undoubtedly, scope for marketing woolen textiles in the
southern regions of Brazil. The principal constraint would seem to be the
development of high quality medium weight textiles of synthetic fibers, or of
very low wool content that would lessen the need for woolen clothing during
the cold season.
27.       As personal incomes rise in Brazil, the demand for imported textiles
and ready-made clothing from traditional fashion centers in Europe will un-
doubtedly rise, fulfilling a considerable portion of the demand for clothing
of woolen content. This is a worldwide consumption pattern that will persist
despite high tariff on non-essential goods. Brazil should be no exception to
that trend in styles and tastes.

ANNEX 11
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUTSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Economic Justification
1.        To evaluate the economic feasibility of the project, economic rates
of return have been calculated for model plants of the major components. The
best estimate economic rates of return (ERR) are presented below:
(a)  beef processing (600 head/day);                           55.35
(b)  beef processing (300 head/day);                          37.55
(c)  soybean processing (600 tons/day);                       44.85
(d)  powdered milk and butter plant (150,000 liters/day);   53.85
(e)  cheese and butter plant (50,000 liters/day);             46.65
(f)  tannery (1,000 hides/day).                                24.35
Sensitivity tests of these rates, in relation to certain variations in cost
and benefit elements are presented in Appendix 11-1 for financial rates of
return and in Appendix 11-2 for economic rates of return. Appendix 11-3
presents specific assumptions underlying the derivation of the above rates.
2.        In the evaluation of the contribution of the project to the Brazilian
balance of payments, the export and import substitute component of the major
types of plant was estimated, as well as the cost of the foreign currency
investment. International market prices as of 1974 (unless otherwise stated)
and an exchange rate of Cr$ 7.92/US were used for the evaluation. The contri-
bution is presented in the following table:
Net Positive Dollar Inflow
4gro-industrial Category                  Over the Project Life-span
Beef processing                           40,000,000
Soybean processing                        67,000,000
Milk processing                           55,000,000
Miscellaneous processing                  54,000.000
Total                         216,000,000



ANNEX 11
Page 2
The specific investments in the miscellaneous category are unknown and there-
fore the contribution to the balance of payments for this group cannot be
calculated; however, the figure vwas estimated proportionally to the allocated
investment in the entire category (25%). The net dollar inflow over the
project's span of US$216 million compares to a foreign currency investment
estimate of US$86 million.
3.        In the evaluation of the contribution af- the project to the employ-
ment in Brazil, cost estimates were used assuming that an above normal unemploy-
ment rate exists in Brazil throughout the life-span of the project. Only the
jobs directly created in the plants under consideration were included. The
table below presents the contribution of the major components to employment:
Annual Average Man/Years
Agro-industrial catagory                  for the Project Life-span
Beef processing                           4,660
Soybean processing                         260
Milk processing                             600
Miscellaneous processing                  1,840
Total                     7,360
The specific investments in the miscellaneous category are unknown and there-
fore the contribution to the creation of new jobs cannot be calculated; how-
ever, the figure was estimated proportionally to the allocated investment in
the entire category (25%).

ANNEX 11
APPENDIX 11-1
Page 1
BRAZIL
SECOND AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Specific Assptions
for Economic Rates of Return Calculation
Beef Processins
(a) All product prices, except by-products, are world price forecasts
for Brazilian beef, which show a 2.7% annual increase over first
quarter 1975 prices through 1980 and no increase thereafter.
Actual prices are lower than Bank forecasts due to lower quality
of beef; the trend, however, is similar in both;
(b) Fixed investment includes 33% foreign currency, on which 15% duties
are collected;
(c)  57 tax is collected on added value of domestic sales:
(d) Cr$ 200/ton export taxes are collected on exported products:
(e)  New plants would save 25% of transportation costs: and
(f)  Production in first year of operation is 50% of full capacity and
75% in the following years.
Soybean Processing
(a) Product prices (oil and meal) are international prices as of 1974,
assumed to remain constant in real terms;
(b) All the production is export-oriented;
(c) Investment includes 29% foreign currency on which 15% duties are
collected; and
(d) Production in first year of operation is 50% of full capacity and
75% in the following years.
Milk Processine
(a) Product prices are 1974 domestic prices, assumed to remain
constant in real terms;
(b) PIS tax (0.5%) is collected on sales 15% ICM tax is collected on
added value-



ANNEX 11
APPNDIX 11-1
Page 2
(c) Investment includes 30% foreign currency, on which 157 duties
are collected: and
(d) Production phases to full capacity in third year of operation:
(i)  Powdered milk and butter plant:
40%, 80% of full capacity in the first and second year of
operation, respectively, and 10n% in the third and following
years- and
(ii)  Cheese and butter plant:
60%, 80% of full capacity in first and second years of
operation, respectively, and 100% in the following years.
Miscellaneous Processing
(a)  Prices of products are international prices as of 1974 for
tannery products,
(b)  Investment includes 37V foreign currency on which 15% duties are
collected; and
(c)  Production phases to full capacity of the tannery is 35% of
full capacity in first year of operation and about 100% in
the following years.
